1. A 27-year-old man with ulcerative colitis comes to the emergency department because of a 3-day history of moderate abdominal cramps and bloody diarrhea. His medications are mesalamine and azathioprine. His temperature is 38.3°C (101°F). Abdominal examination shows mild distention; there is mild, diffuse tenderness to deep palpation but no tenderness to percussion. Flexible sigmoidoscopy shows erythematous mucosa with scattered ulcerations throughout. Colonic biopsy results show several giant cells with inclusion bodies. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Ganciclovir therapy</br>b. Hydrocortisone therapy</br>c. Metronidazole therapy</br>d. Total colectomy</br>e. Observation only</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Ganciclovir therapy  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Chronic disease: Known ulcerative colitis (UC).<br /></br>• Immunosuppressed: Takes azathioprine → high risk for opportunistic infection.<br /></br>• “Bloody diarrhea + fever” could be UC flare, C-diff, CMV, or toxic megacolon.<br /></br>• Key diagnostic clue: Colonic biopsy shows “giant cells with inclusion bodies.”  This is the classic histology of cytomegalovirus (CMV) colitis (“owl-eye” intranuclear inclusions).<br /></br>• Flexible sigmoidoscopy only shows nonspecific UC-type inflammation; the biopsy is the tie-breaker.</p></br><p>Pertinent positives: fever, bloody diarrhea, immunosuppression, inclusion bodies.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no peritoneal signs, no hemodynamic instability → not yet surgical.</p></br><p>Pattern to recognize:<br /></br>UC patient on immunosuppressants + biopsy with viral inclusions = CMV superinfection mimicking a UC flare.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Differentiate flare vs superinfection: biopsy proves CMV.<br /></br>2. CMV colitis in immunocompromised = treat with IV ganciclovir first line (or oral valganciclovir if mild).<br /></br>3. Corticosteroids would worsen viremia; surgery reserved for perforation or refractory disease.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Always biopsy suspected UC flares in immunosuppressed patients—CMV colitis requires antiviral therapy, not escalation of immunosuppression.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• CMV is a double-stranded DNA herpesvirus that establishes latency; reactivates with immunosuppression (azathioprine, steroids, biologics, AIDS, transplant).<br /></br>• Virus infects endothelial &amp; epithelial cells → cytopathic effect → large cells with eosinophilic intranuclear “owl-eye” inclusions.<br /></br>• In the colon presents with fever, abdominal pain, bloody diarrhea; can precipitate toxic megacolon if untreated.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>First-line:<br /></br>• IV ganciclovir (5 mg/kg q12h) or oral valganciclovir until clinical response, then maintenance.  </p></br><p>Second-line / refractory:<br /></br>• Foscarnet or cidofovir.  </p></br><p>Monitoring:<br /></br>• CBC for neutropenia, renal function (drug toxicity).<br /></br>• Repeat colonoscopy not routinely needed unless symptoms persist.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“UC on AZA + Owl-eyes = Give the ‘GAN’ of ganci-clovir.”<br /></br>(Owl-eye on a branch; hand offers a “GAN” of grain.)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why Other Answers Are Wrong<br /></br>b. Hydrocortisone therapy<br /></br>   – Steroids treat UC flare but would exacerbate CMV viremia; biopsy proves viral, not autoimmune, etiology.  </p></br><p>c. Metronidazole therapy<br /></br>   – Used for C. difficile or anaerobic infections; CMV is a DNA virus unaffected by metronidazole.  </p></br><p>d. Total colectomy<br /></br>   – Indicated for perforation, uncontrolled hemorrhage, toxic megacolon, or medically refractory UC. Patient is stable and has treatable CMV infection.  </p></br><p>e. Observation only<br /></br>   – CMV colitis can progress to fulminant disease; antiviral therapy is required.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In an immunosuppressed UC patient with biopsy-proven CMV colitis, start IV ganciclovir—the correct, life-saving intervention.</p>
2. A 47-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 3-week history of increasing rectal bleeding. The bleeding is bright red and initially occurred only during bowel movements but now occurs between bowel movements. He loses approximately 2 teaspoons of blood daily. His stool are formed. He has not had diarrhea or pain. He has receptive anal intercourse with one male partner; they use condoms consistently. Since the bleeding began, he has abstained from sex. He has no history of similar symptoms or serious illness. He takes no medications. There is no family history of serious illness. The patient appears well. Vital signs are within normal limits. Visual examination of the rectum shows no abnormalities. Digital rectal examination shows no palpable mass, tenderness, or swelling. Test of the stool for occult blood is positive. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Anal fissure</br>b. Internal hemorrhoids</br>c. Proctitis</br>d. Rectal condylomata</br>e. Rectal prolapse</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Internal hemorrhoids  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Bright-red blood per rectum (hematochezia) = distal source.<br /></br>• “Initially occurred only during bowel movements but now occurs between bowel movements” → venous oozing typical of hemorrhoids.<br /></br>• “No pain, diarrhea, or tenesmus” → rules out fissure (painful), proctitis (pain/tenesmus/mucus), inflammatory bowel disease, etc.<br /></br>• Formed stools (not hard, ribbon-like, or diarrheal).<br /></br>• Visual inspection of anal verge/rectum normal; DRE normal → lesion is above the dentate (pectinate) line where you cannot see or palpate it externally.<br /></br>Pattern: painless bright-red bleeding + normal inspection/palpation = internal (not external) hemorrhoids.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Localize bleed: Bright red → anorectal rather than colonic.<br /></br>2. Check pain: absent → favors internal hemorrhoids (located above dentate line, visceral innervation, painless).<br /></br>3. Physical exam: no visible lesion/fissure/condyloma, no palpable mass → internal hemorrhoids remain highest on differential.<br /></br>Core takeaway: On exams, painless rectal bleeding with otherwise normal anorectal exam = internal hemorrhoids unless proven otherwise.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Internal hemorrhoids are engorged superior rectal veins within the hemorrhoidal cushions above the dentate line.<br /></br>• Risk factors: constipation/straining, pregnancy, portal HTN, prolonged sitting. (Many patients have none.)<br /></br>• Visceral innervation → bleeding but NO somatic pain.<br /></br>Clinical manifestations: intermittent painless hematochezia, blood on toilet paper or dripping into bowl; may prolapse if large.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment (high-yield)<br /></br>Diagnostics<br /></br>• Anoscopy is first-line office tool (directly visualizes internal hemorrhoids).<br /></br>• Colonoscopy indicated if ≥40–50 yr, anemia, or other red-flag features to exclude malignancy.</p></br><p>Treatment<br /></br>1. Conservative: ↑fiber (psyllium), ↑fluids, stool softener (docusate), avoid straining.<br /></br>2. Office procedures if persistent: rubber-band ligation (MC), infrared coagulation, sclerotherapy.<br /></br>3. Surgical hemorrhoidectomy for refractory grade III–IV.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“PAIN above the Pectinate is Absent” → Internal hemorrhoids (Above line = Painless).<br /></br>“External = Excruciating” to recall that external hemorrhoids or fissures hurt.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Anal fissure<br /></br>• Severe tearing pain with defecation, visible longitudinal tear in posterior midline; exam would reveal the fissure. Pain absent here.</p></br><p>c. Proctitis (infectious, IBD, radiation)<br /></br>• Presents with rectal pain, tenesmus, urgency, mucopurulent or bloody diarrhea. Patient has none of these symptoms and normal mucosa on inspection.</p></br><p>d. Rectal condylomata (HPV)<br /></br>• Verrucous, cauliflower lesions easily seen on inspection; may itch or bleed lightly if traumatized. Normal visual exam rules this out.</p></br><p>e. Rectal prolapse<br /></br>• Full-thickness intussusception of rectal wall protruding through anus, visible as concentric rings of mucosa; causes sensation of mass and mucus leakage. Not found on exam.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Key Takeaway<br /></br>Painless bright-red bleeding in an adult with an otherwise normal anorectal exam = think internal hemorrhoids; confirm with anoscopy and start fiber/stool-softener therapy.</p>
3. A 26-year-old woman is brought to the emergency department 2 hours after the sudden onset of severe abdominal pain and fainting. She has no other history of serious illness, and she takes no medications. Her last menstrual period was 6 weeks ago. Temperature is 37.2°C (99.0°F), pulse is 110/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 90/50 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 98%. Examination shows no jugular venous distention. Breath sounds are equal over both lung bases. On cardiac examination, a normal S₁ and S₂ are heard. Abdominal examination shows mild distention and diffuse tenderness with guarding and rebound. There is 1+ pedal edema bilaterally. Results of laboratory studies are shown:</br>Hemoglobin 10 g/dL</br>Hematocrit 30%</br>Leukocyte count 12,000/mm³</br>Platelet count 268,000/mm³</br>Serum</br>Na⁺ 147 mEq/L</br>K⁺ 4.6 mEq/L</br>β-hCG 3000 mIU/mL (N<3)</br>Transvaginal ultrasonography shows no intrauterine pregnancy. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. CT scan of the abdomen</br>b. Hysterosalpingography</br>c. Hysteroscopy</br>d. Laparoscopy</br>e. MRI of the pelvis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Laparoscopy  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Sudden-onset severe lower abdominal pain + syncope → suggests an acute intra-abdominal catastrophe (most common in a reproductive-age woman = ruptured ectopic pregnancy or ruptured corpus-luteum cyst).<br /></br>• 6 wks since LMP + β-hCG 3000 mIU/mL → the pregnancy is far enough along that a gestational sac should be visible on transvaginal US (discriminatory zone ≈1500–2000 mIU/mL). “No intra-uterine pregnancy” therefore strongly points to an ectopic pregnancy.<br /></br>• Hypotension (90/50), tachycardia (110/min), tender distended abdomen with guarding/rebound, and Hgb 10 g/dL → hemoperitoneum with acute blood loss.<br /></br>• No JVD, clear lungs, normal heart sounds → not cardiogenic; the shock is very likely hemorrhagic.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Pregnancy with β-hCG above the discriminatory zone but empty uterus = ectopic until proven otherwise.<br /></br>2. Hemodynamic instability + peritoneal signs = presumed rupture with ongoing intra-abdominal bleed.<br /></br>3. In an unstable patient you do not spend time on additional imaging—you go straight to the procedure that can both confirm the diagnosis and treat it (evacuate blood, control the bleeding).<br /></br>4. Diagnostic AND therapeutic modality of choice = emergent laparoscopy (or laparotomy if a lot of blood is expected).  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (Ectopic Pregnancy)<br /></br>• Implantation outside uterine cavity (≈95 % in ampulla of fallopian tube). Risk factors: prior ectopic, tubal surgery, PID, infertility treatments, smoking.<br /></br>• As trophoblastic tissue invades, it can erode through the tubal wall and rupture, causing massive intra-peritoneal bleeding.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• Hemodynamically stable, unruptured: methotrexate or laparoscopic salpingostomy.<br /></br>• Hemodynamically unstable or evidence of rupture: immediate surgical exploration (laparoscopy vs laparotomy).<br /></br>• Labs: type &amp; screen, quantitative β-hCG; imaging: transvaginal US is initial, but if nondiagnostic and the patient is stable serial β-hCG can be followed. None of that applies here because she is unstable.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Positive β-hCG + Empty uterus + Instability = Go to OR.”<br /></br>Use the mnemonic P.E.A.R.:<br /></br>P – Positive pregnancy test<br /></br>E – Empty uterus on TVUS<br /></br>A – Acute abdomen/instability<br /></br>R – Rush to OR (laparoscopy/laparotomy)  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. CT scan of the abdomen<br /></br>• Takes time, exposes fetus to ionizing radiation, and is unnecessary in the setting of a clear clinical picture. CT cannot treat the problem. Would be considered only in a stable patient with uncertain diagnosis.  </p></br><p>b. Hysterosalpingography<br /></br>• Contrast study used to evaluate tubal patency in infertility work-ups. Contraindicated in pregnancy and in acute abdomen.  </p></br><p>c. Hysteroscopy<br /></br>• Direct visualization of the uterine cavity—useful for intra-uterine pathology (polyps, fibroids, adhesions). It will not detect a tubal pregnancy or stop bleeding in the peritoneal cavity.  </p></br><p>e. MRI of the pelvis<br /></br>• Although MRI has no ionizing radiation, it is unnecessary, time-consuming, and still does not offer therapy.  </p></br><p>Core Take-away<br /></br>In a pregnant woman with β-hCG above the discriminatory zone, no intra-uterine pregnancy on TVUS, and signs of hemorrhagic shock, presume ruptured ectopic pregnancy and proceed directly to operative laparoscopy/laparotomy for simultaneous diagnosis and treatment.</p>
4. A 10-year-old girl is brought to the office by her parents because of a 3-year history of impulsive and inattentive behavior. Parents had been trying to manage her behavior at home and school, but during the past 6 months, she has had a marked decline in functioning at school and extracurricular activities. She has been in trouble for being disruptive and not following instructions. She has no history of serious illness and receives no medications. Growth and development are appropriate for age. Physical and mental status examinations show no abnormalities. Without treatment, this patient is at greatest risk for which of the following?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Generalized anxiety disorder</br>b. Homelessness</br>c. Schizophrenia</br>d. Substance use disorder</br>e. Unemployment</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Substance use disorder  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues: 10-year-old, ≥6-month history of inattention + impulsivity, functional decline at both school and home, normal exam → Classic presentation of ADHD (attention-deficit/hyperactivity disorder).<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: normal growth/development, no psychosis, no mood or anxiety symptoms, no neurological deficits → rules out alternative psychiatric or neurologic conditions.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Identify the disorder (ADHD).<br /></br>2. Recall long-term natural history if untreated.<br /></br>3. Highest-frequency adverse outcome on USMLE/NBME lists = later substance use (alcohol, nicotine, cannabis, cocaine, etc.).<br /></br>Core takeaway: Untreated ADHD markedly elevates the risk of developing a substance use disorder in adolescence and adulthood.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• ADHD = neurodevelopmental disorder characterized by deficits in dopaminergic/noradrenergic signaling in the prefrontal cortex → impaired executive function, impulse control.<br /></br>• Chronic impulsivity + reward-seeking behavior → self-medication with stimulants/depressants → progression to substance abuse.<br /></br>• Epidemiology: Up to 30-50 % of untreated ADHD patients develop a substance use disorder vs 10 % in the general population. Early effective treatment (especially stimulants) reduces, not increases, this risk.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics (quick hits)<br /></br>1. First-line meds:<br /></br>   • Methylphenidate, mixed amphetamine salts.<br /></br>   • Atomoxetine (NE reuptake inhibitor) if stimulants contraindicated.<br /></br>2. Adjuncts: α2-agonists (guanfacine, clonidine).<br /></br>3. Non-pharm: Parent management training, behavioral classroom interventions, CBT (cognitive behavioral therapy).<br /></br>4. Evaluation: Vanderbilt or Conners rating scales; symptoms must be present in ≥2 settings before age 12.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ADHD LATE” = Long-term risks<br /></br>• A – Academic underachievement<br /></br>• D – Delinquency (conduct/antisocial)<br /></br>• H – High-risk behaviors (driving accidents, STIs)<br /></br>• D – Drug use disorder (most tested)  </p></br><p>Mnemonic hook: Kids with ADHD may “Self-stimulate with Substances.”  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Generalized anxiety disorder – Anxiety can co-occur but is not the MOST strongly linked long-term risk; prevalence increase is modest compared with substance abuse.<br /></br>b. Homelessness – Possible in severe, untreated psychiatric illness (schizophrenia, SUD), but less directly tied to ADHD alone.<br /></br>c. Schizophrenia – No established etiologic relationship; psychosis risk is not higher in ADHD patients.<br /></br>e. Unemployment – Adults with childhood ADHD can have job instability, yet data show substance use disorder remains the single most common and highest-yield adverse outcome emphasized on exams.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Untreated ADHD → greatest evidence-based risk = development of substance use disorder; early diagnosis and stimulant therapy mitigate this trajectory.</p>
5. An 8-year-old boy is brought to the physician by his mother because he has had the rash shown for 1 week; the rash began as a small 1-cm lesion on his cheek. His younger brother has had a similar rash for 5 days. The patient had an upper respiratory tract infection 8 days ago treated with an over-the-counter decongestant. His temperature is 36.7°C (98°F). Which of the following is the most likely explanation for these findings?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Adverse effect of a drug</br>b. Bacterial infection</br>c. Fungal infection</br>d. Immune deposits</br>e. Viral infection</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Viral infection (parvovirus B19 → erythema infectiosum, “slapped-cheek” disease)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>1. Age/setting: 8-year-old school-age child.<br /></br>2. Rash pattern: Began as a single cheek lesion, now spreads over 1 week, bright red on the face (image shows classic “slapped cheek”).<br /></br>3. Recent prodrome: Mild upper-respiratory illness 8 days earlier.<br /></br>4. Household spread: Younger brother developed the same eruption 5 days later → highly contagious.<br /></br>5. Afebrile, nontoxic.</p></br><p>These findings strongly point to erythema infectiosum caused by parvovirus B19, a common viral exanthem in children.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>• URI-like prodrome → think viral.<br /></br>• Bright red, well-demarcated malar rash sparing the nasolabial folds (“slapped cheek”) + lacy reticular rash that spreads to trunk/extremities (seen on picture) → buzz-phrase for parvovirus B19.<br /></br>• Secondary cases in siblings within 1 week (incubation 4-14 days).<br /></br>Core takeaway: Parvovirus B19 causes a characteristic biphasic illness—mild prodrome followed days later by the slapped-cheek exanthem.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Parvovirus B19 (small, non-enveloped ssDNA virus).<br /></br>• Infects erythroid precursors → transient aplastic crises in patients with hemoglobinopathies, hydrops fetalis in pregnant women.<br /></br>• Immune-complex–mediated rash appears after viral replication peaks; therefore, children are usually afebrile and no longer contagious once rash erupts.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment<br /></br>Typical presentation → no labs needed.<br /></br>Management: Supportive (acetaminophen, hydration).<br /></br>Counsel parents: child may return to school once rash appears (no longer infectious).<br /></br>Special situations: Test IgM in pregnant women or patients with chronic hemolytic anemia exposed to the child.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Five–B-19”:<br /></br>5th disease = Parvo B19 → 5-day URI then 5-day later slapped-cheek rash.<br /></br>Picture a “High-five slap” on the cheeks to recall both name and appearance.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Adverse drug reaction: Usually starts shortly (hours-days) after starting a new medication, presents as morbilliform trunk rash or urticaria, not a localized malar eruption; no new drug besides OTC decongestant many days prior and sibling has same rash (drug reactions are not contagious).  </p></br><p>b. Bacterial infection: Impetigo (Staph/Strep) causes honey-colored crusts, not confluent erythema; cellulitis would be tender, warm, unilateral, and patient would likely be febrile. Contagious spread in household is slower and requires skin contact.  </p></br><p>c. Fungal infection: Tinea corporis/capitis produces annular, scaly plaques with central clearing, often pruritic; not the bright, confluent facial erythema shown.  </p></br><p>d. Immune deposits: Henoch-Schönlein purpura (IgA vasculitis) causes palpable purpura on buttocks/legs, arthralgia, abdominal pain; lesions are violaceous/purpuric, not erythematous malar rash.  </p></br><p>Understanding these distinguishing features prevents common exam traps and solidifies recognition of parvovirus B19 erythema infectiosum.</p>
6. A 16-year-old girl comes to the office for a routine health maintenance examination. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Menarche was at the age of 12 years. Menses occur at irregular 28- to 34-day intervals with light flow; her last menstrual period was 10 weeks ago. She participates in after-school sports, including soccer and track, and receives grades of A’s and B’s. She appears well groomed, affable, and engages readily in discussion. She is 168 cm (5 ft 6 in) tall and weighs 51 kg, or 112lb; BMI is 18 kg/m2. Temperature is 37.0 °C (98.6 °F), pulse is 65/min, and blood pressure is 110/60 mm Hg. Sexual maturity rating is stage 4 for breast and pubic hair development. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Measurement of serum free testosterone concentration</br>b. Pelvic ultrasonography</br>c. Progesterone challenge test</br>d. Urine pregnancy test</br>e. Reassurance only</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Urine pregnancy test  </p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Last menstrual period was 10 weeks ago” → ≥ 6 weeks without menses = secondary amenorrhea in someone who previously cycled.<br /></br>• “16-year-old girl” and no statement excluding sexual activity → any post-menarche female can be pregnant.<br /></br>• Normal vitals, BMI 18, no virilization, no galactorrhea → no immediate red flags for endocrine tumor, PCOS, or POI; but pregnancy must always be excluded first.<br /></br>• Athletic participation and low-normal BMI could suggest hypothalamic (functional) amenorrhea, but this is never evaluated until pregnancy is ruled out.  </p></br><p>Key pattern: FIRST STEP in any amenorrhea (&gt; 6 weeks) = pregnancy test (serum β-hCG or urine hCG).  </p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise algorithm for secondary amenorrhea:<br /></br>1. Confirm pregnancy status → urine or serum hCG.<br /></br>2. If negative, check TSH and prolactin.<br /></br>3. If normal, do progesterone withdrawal test, etc.  </p></br><p>Because step 1 hasn’t been done, the correct next action is a urine pregnancy test.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Always “rule-out pregnancy” before pursuing any endocrine or imaging work-up in a reproductive-age female with missed periods.  </p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (brief)<br /></br>• Secondary amenorrhea: cessation of menses ≥ 3 cycles or ≥ 6 months in women who previously menstruated.<br /></br>• Most common cause overall = pregnancy (↑β-hCG maintains corpus luteum → progesterone → no withdrawal bleed).<br /></br>• Other etiologies: hypothalamic-pituitary (stress, weight loss, functional), thyroid disease, hyperprolactinemia, PCOS, premature ovarian insufficiency, anatomic.  </p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Diagnostic Tests &amp; Management<br /></br>• Urine hCG (qualitative) detects pregnancy ~14 days after conception; inexpensive, rapid, non-invasive—hence first-line.<br /></br>• If positive → initiate prenatal care.<br /></br>• If negative → proceed to serum prolactin &amp; TSH; then progesterone challenge, estradiol, LH/FSH, imaging as indicated.  </p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Pregnancy Is The First Thought” (P-I-T-F):<br /></br>P = Pregnancy test<br /></br>I = Initial step in amenorrhea<br /></br>T = TSH / prolactin AFTER negative test<br /></br>F = Further work-up (progesterone challenge, imaging)  </p></br><p>Visual: Picture a stop sign reading “STOP—Think Pregnancy!” before any other arrows on the amenorrhea flowchart.  </p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Serum free testosterone: Useful for hyperandrogenic states (e.g., polycystic ovary syndrome, androgen-secreting tumor). No hirsutism/virilization here and pregnancy must be ruled out first.<br /></br>b. Pelvic ultrasonography: Evaluates structural anomalies, ovarian cysts, or pregnancy viability but is not the initial test; obtain only after positive hCG or if concern for anatomic pathology persists.<br /></br>c. Progesterone challenge test: Performed after negative pregnancy, TSH, and prolactin results to determine estrogen status and outflow tract integrity. Too early in work-up.<br /></br>e. Reassurance only: Missing menses for &gt; 6 weeks requires evaluation; cannot be dismissed without excluding pregnancy.  </p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Any adolescent or adult woman who misses a period should have pregnancy excluded first. A urine pregnancy test is the most appropriate initial step in this 16-year-old with 10 weeks of amenorrhea.</p>
7. A 19-year-old college student is brought to the emergency department by her roommate because of increasing confusion during the past 24 hours. She also has had decreased appetite for 2 days. On arrival, she is difficult to arouse. Her temperature is 38.9°C (102°F), and respirations are 28/min and deep. Her pulse is 124/min, and blood pressure is 80/42 mm Hg while sitting; her pulse is 100/min, and blood pressure is 110/50 mm Hg while supine. The lungs are clear to auscultation. Abdominal examination shows mild diffuse tenderness to palpation. Serum studies show:</br>Na⁺ 130 mEq/L</br>K⁺ 5.2 mEq/L</br>Cl⁻ 98 mEq/L</br>HCO₃⁻ 7 mEq/L</br>Glucose 700 mg/dL</br>Arterial blood gas analysis on room air shows:</br>pH 7.16</br>Pco₂ 20 mm Hg</br>Po₂ 108 mm Hg</br>This patient most likely has which of the following primary acid–base disorders?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Metabolic acidosis</br>b. Metabolic acidosis and respiratory alkalosis</br>c. Respiratory acidosis</br>d. Respiratory acidosis and metabolic alkalosis</br>e. Respiratory alkalosis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Metabolic acidosis  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis – Key Clues<br /></br>• Young patient with type 1 diabetes “look” (college age) + very high glucose (700 mg/dL) → think diabetic ketoacidosis (DKA).<br /></br>• Kussmaul respirations: “28/min and deep” = classic compensatory hyperventilation for metabolic acidosis.<br /></br>• HCO₃⁻ 7 mEq/L (very low) and pH 7.16 (acidic).<br /></br>• Anion gap = Na – (Cl + HCO₃) = 130 – (98 + 7) ≈ 25 → high-anion-gap metabolic acidosis.<br /></br>• PaCO₂ 20 mm Hg (low) → respiratory compensation, not a primary disorder.<br /></br>• Winter’s formula (expected PaCO₂) = 1.5(HCO₃⁻) + 8 ± 2 = 1.5(7) + 8 ≈ 18.5 (16.5–20.5). Measured PaCO₂ (20) sits in this range → compensation is appropriate → single primary disturbance: metabolic acidosis.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify acidemia (pH &lt; 7.35).<br /></br>2. Look at HCO₃⁻ (↓) vs PaCO₂ (↑ or ↓). The big drop in HCO₃⁻ (7) points to metabolic cause.<br /></br>3. Check PaCO₂: also ↓; therefore lungs are trying to blow off CO₂.<br /></br>4. Apply Winter’s formula to rule out a mixed picture. Because measured PaCO₂ matches predicted, there is no additional respiratory alkalosis or acidosis.<br /></br>Core takeaway: In any high-anion-gap metabolic acidosis, always use Winter’s formula to decide if a second disorder is present.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Brief Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>DKA = absolute/relative insulin deficiency → ↑ lipolysis → ↑ free fatty acids → hepatic ketogenesis (β-hydroxybutyrate &amp; acetoacetate) → high-anion-gap metabolic acidosis. Osmotic diuresis → volume depletion → hypotension, tachycardia. K⁺ often appears high (5.2) but total body K⁺ is depleted.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Dx / Tx Pearls<br /></br>• Labs: serum/urine ketones, β-hydroxybutyrate, BMP every 2–4 h.<br /></br>• Initial therapy: 1) Large-volume isotonic saline, 2) IV regular insulin drip, 3) Potassium repletion once K⁺ &lt; 5 mEq/L and urine output adequate, 4) Search &amp; treat precipitating factors (infection, MI, etc.).<br /></br>• Bicarbonate only if pH &lt; 6.9.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memory Aids<br /></br>“Kussmaul = K for Ketoacidosis” (deep, labored breathing).<br /></br>Winter is coming → use “Winter’s formula” to check if the CO₂ fits.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Answers Are Wrong<br /></br>b. Metabolic acidosis + respiratory alkalosis<br /></br>• Would require PaCO₂ lower than predicted (&lt; 16 mm Hg here). Current PaCO₂ fits Winter’s formula, so no extra respiratory alkalosis.</p></br><p>c. Respiratory acidosis<br /></br>• Would show elevated PaCO₂ (&gt; 40 mm Hg) with a secondary elevation in HCO₃⁻, opposite of this vignette.</p></br><p>d. Respiratory acidosis and metabolic alkalosis (two opposite processes)<br /></br>• Would give ↑ PaCO₂, ↑ HCO₃⁻, and pH could be near-normal or slightly abnormal. Not present here (both PaCO₂ and HCO₃⁻ are low; pH is clearly acidemic).</p></br><p>e. Respiratory alkalosis<br /></br>• Primary disturbance would be low PaCO₂ with secondary ↓ HCO₃⁻. pH would be alkalemic (&gt; 7.45). Our patient is acidemic with extremely low HCO₃⁻.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>The profoundly low bicarbonate, high anion gap, and compensatory low PaCO₂ that matches Winter’s formula identify a single primary disorder: high-anion-gap metabolic acidosis (classic DKA presentation).</p>
8. A 54-year-old man comes to the office for a follow-up examination 12 weeks after undergoing aortic valve replacement. The patient is doing well and has been participating in physical therapy as part of his recovery. His only current symptom is mild fatigue. Medical history also is remarkable for hypertension. Current medications are warfarin, metoprolol, and lisinopril. He has no known medication allergies. Vital signs are within normal limits. On cardiac examination, a mechanical valve click is heard. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. The patient has a dental cleaning appointment scheduled for next week at noon. Which of the following is most appropriate to recommend in preparation for this patient’s dental visit?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. 5-Day course of azithromycin starting the morning of the cleaning</br>b. 3-Day course of levofloxacin starting 3 days before the cleaning</br>c. 24-Hour course of metronidazole starting the morning of the cleaning</br>d. Single dose of amoxicillin the morning of the cleaning</br>e. No pharmacotherapy is indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Single dose of amoxicillin the morning of the cleaning (2 g PO 30–60 min before the procedure)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues that trigger endocarditis-prophylaxis thinking:<br /></br>• Mechanical aortic valve click → prosthetic heart valve (MAJOR indication).<br /></br>• Upcoming dental cleaning → manipulation of gingival tissue &amp; possible mucosal perforation (high-bacteremia procedure).<br /></br>• No penicillin allergy reported.  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives: prosthetic valve + dental work. <br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no drug allergy (so standard first-line agent can be used).  </p></br><p>Pattern: “Prosthetic valve + dental procedure = give single-dose amoxicillin.”  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)  </p></br><ol></br><li></br><p>Identify if prophylaxis is indicated.<br /></br>   AHA/ACC guidelines (2017 update): give prophylaxis for<br /></br>   • Any prosthetic heart valve or valve repair device<br /></br>   • Prior infective endocarditis<br /></br>   • Certain congenital heart diseases<br /></br>   • Cardiac transplant + valvulopathy  </p></br></li></br><li></br><p>Determine if the procedure is high-risk.<br /></br>   • Dental procedures that manipulate gingiva or periapical region → YES.  </p></br></li></br><li></br><p>Choose the correct regimen.<br /></br>   • First-line: Amoxicillin 2 g PO 30–60 min before procedure (single dose).<br /></br>   • Alt (penicillin allergy): Clindamycin 600 mg, Azithro 500 mg, or Clarithro 500 mg.  </p></br></li></br></ol></br><p>Core takeaway: Only ONE dose is needed; extended courses are neither recommended nor effective.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>Transient bacteremia with viridans group streptococci occurs during dental work. Because prosthetic material has no native endothelium, organisms can adhere easily, forming vegetations → prosthetic valve endocarditis (high morbidity &amp; mortality). Short-course prophylactic antibiotics given just before the bacteremia window markedly reduce risk.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Notes  </p></br><p>• Warfarin continuation has no bearing on antibiotic prophylaxis choice.<br /></br>• No additional labs/imaging required beforehand unless specific bleeding or INR concerns exist.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“PAVE the way”: Prophylaxis for<br /></br>P – Prosthetic valve<br /></br>A – h/o endocarditis<br /></br>V – Valvular transplant (heart transplant with valve dz)<br /></br>E – Extreme congenital lesions  </p></br><p>Drug &amp; dose: “2 A.M.” → 2 g Amoxicillin Morning (30–60 min)  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. 5-day azithromycin course:<br /></br>   – Overly long; guidelines call for SINGLE dose. Reserved only if penicillin-allergic.  </p></br><p>b. 3-day levofloxacin:<br /></br>   – Fluoroquinolones are not recommended for endocarditis prophylaxis; duration also wrong.  </p></br><p>c. 24-hour metronidazole:<br /></br>   – Targets anaerobes, not viridans strep; wrong spectrum and duration.  </p></br><p>e. No pharmacotherapy:<br /></br>   – Would be correct for most patients, but a prosthetic valve is a clear indication. Omitting prophylaxis exposes patient to high-risk prosthetic valve endocarditis.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Many students remember “most patients don’t need prophylaxis” and forget the specific AHA exceptions (prosthetic valve, prior IE, certain congenital lesions, transplant w/ valve disease).</p>
9. A 62-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 3-week history of progressive shortness of breath with exertion, fever, and chills. Two days ago, he coughed up a small amount of blood-tinged, thick sputum; otherwise, his cough has been nonproductive. Three years ago, he underwent right kidney transplant for end-stage renal disease caused by polycystic kidney disease. His medications are prednisone, cyclosporine, sirolimus, and a daily multivitamin. He does not smoke cigarettes. His temperature is 38.4 °C (101.1 °F), pulse is 98/min, respirations are 28/min, and blood pressure is 120/80 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 94%. Crackles are heard in the right middle and lower lung fields. Abdominal examination shows a well-healed scar over the right lower quadrant. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Laboratory studies show: Leukocyte count 9700/mm³; Serum urea nitrogen 24 mg/dL; Glucose 160 mg/dL; Creatinine 1 mg/dL. Serum electrolyte concentrations are within the reference ranges. A chest x-ray shows consolidation of the middle and lower lobes of the right lung. Bronchoscopy is performed; biopsy results are shown. Cyclosporine therapy is discontinued. Which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Amphotericin B</br>b. Isoniazid</br>c. Ketoconazole</br>d. Penicillin</br>e. Rifamycin</br>f. Trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>A. Amphotericin B  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Solid-organ transplant on triple immunosuppression (prednisone, cyclosporine, sirolimus) → marked T-cell dysfunction → high risk for invasive molds, especially Aspergillus species.<br /></br>• Subacute (3-week) fever, pleuritic symptoms, hemoptysis, focal consolidation → classic presentation of invasive pulmonary aspergillosis (IPA).<br /></br>• Hemoptysis + “blood-tinged, thick sputum” and focal lobar infiltrate are more typical of Aspergillus than Pneumocystis, viral, or typical bacterial pneumonias.<br /></br>• Bronchoscopy/biopsy (image not shown) in IPA classically demonstrates thin, septate hyphae branching at acute (≈45°) angles. Recognition of this pattern is the key pathologic clue that points to Aspergillus and rules out mucormycosis (broad, non-septate, 90° branching).<br /></br>• Cyclosporine is stopped to decrease immunosuppression and improve host defense, but a potent systemic antifungal is still required.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Immunocompromised host + focal infiltrate + hemoptysis ➞ think invasive mold.<br /></br>Step 2: Biopsy shows acute-angle, septate hyphae ➞ Aspergillus.<br /></br>Step 3: First-line IV therapy for severe invasive aspergillosis on USMLE/NBME: Amphotericin B (some sources now prefer IV voriconazole, but Amphotericin remains the classic board answer).<br /></br>Core takeaway: In a transplant patient with hemoptysis and biopsy showing acute-angle septate hyphae, give Amphotericin B.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Aspergillus fumigatus is an opportunistic mold whose inhaled conidia germinate in alveoli when neutrophil or T-cell function is impaired.<br /></br>• Angio-invasion ➞ thrombosis, tissue necrosis, and hemorrhage ➞ pleuritic chest pain &amp; hemoptysis.<br /></br>• Untreated mortality exceeds 70%.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>• Initial therapy: IV Amphotericin B deoxycholate or liposomal formulation. (Newer guidelines favor voriconazole or isavuconazole, but Amphotericin is the exam-preferred drug.)<br /></br>• Add reduction of immunosuppression + possible surgical resection for localized necrosis.<br /></br>• Monitor renal function &amp; electrolytes (Amphotericin nephrotoxicity).<br /></br>• CT chest often shows “halo sign” (ground-glass opacity surrounding a nodule) or “air-crescent sign” during recovery.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“A for Acute-angle Aspergillus → treat with Amphotericin.”<br /></br>or visualize the 45° ‘A’ angle on histology pointing to the ‘A’ drug Amphotericin.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Isoniazid – For Mycobacterium tuberculosis. Histology would show caseating granulomas or acid-fast bacilli, not septate hyphae. TB rarely produces acute-angle branching on biopsy.<br /></br>C. Ketoconazole – Oral agent for superficial dermatophyte or chronic mucocutaneous infections; inadequate for life-threatening invasive mold disease.<br /></br>D. Penicillin – Covers Streptococcus pneumoniae; lobar consolidation possible but biopsy would show gram-positive diplococci, not hyphae. No hemoptysis-angioinvasive pattern.<br /></br>E. Rifamycin (rifampin/rifabutin) – Also an antitubercular/antimycobacterial drug; same reasoning as isoniazid.<br /></br>F. Trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole – First-line for Pneumocystis jirovecii pneumonia (PCP). PCP produces diffuse bilateral interstitial infiltrates (“ground-glass”), not focal lobar consolidation and septate hyphae.</p></br><p>Key Pitfall to Avoid<br /></br>• Confusing PCP with Aspergillus: PCP = diffuse infiltrates, dry cough, no hemoptysis, silver-stained cysts. Aspergillus = focal lesions, necrosis, hemoptysis, branching septate hyphae.</p>
10. A 19-year-old man is admitted to the hospital after sustaining head trauma in a motorcycle collision. In the field, his Glasgow Coma Scale score was 3, and he was immediately intubated and mechanically ventilated. A CT scan of the head without contrast shows severe bilateral global cortical injury, effacement of white and gray matter distinction, and global edema. Neurosurgical evaluation determines that the patient would gain no benefit from surgical intervention. Twenty-four hours after admission, his temperature is 37.8°C (100°F), pulse is 108/min, and blood pressure is 98/46 mm Hg. The pupils are fixed and dilated with no corneal reflex. He does not respond to painful stimuli, and he has no gag response. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Apnea test</br>b. Arterial blood gas analysis</br>c. Blood cultures</br>d. Repeat CT scan of the head</br>e. MRI of the brain</br>f. EEG</br>g. Lumbar puncture</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Apnea test  </p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Massive traumatic brain injury on CT + neurosurgery says “no benefit from surgery” → likely catastrophic, non-survivable injury.<br /></br>• Exam 24 h later: fixed, dilated pupils; absent corneal &amp; gag reflexes; no motor response to pain → classic brain-stem areflexia.<br /></br>• Vitals nearly normal (T 37.8 °C, BP 98/46) → prerequisites for brain-death testing can be met (normothermia, at least minimal SBP &gt; 100 mm Hg ideal, but &gt; 90 acceptable with pressors).<br /></br>• Key next step: formally confirm brain death before withdrawal of support → first-line confirmatory maneuver = apnea test.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Suspect brain death when coma + absent brain-stem reflexes.<br /></br>2. Prerequisites: no sedatives/paralytics, normothermia, eucarbia/euvolemia, SBP ≥ 90 mm Hg.<br /></br>3. Gold-standard confirmatory test = Apnea test:<br /></br>   • Pre-oxygenate, disconnect ventilator while giving O2 via catheter, watch chest for 8-10 min.<br /></br>   • If no spontaneous breaths AND PaCO₂ rises to ≥ 60 mm Hg or ≥ 20 mm Hg above baseline → test positive ⇒ brain death.<br /></br>4. Ancillary studies (EEG, cerebral flow) only if apnea test cannot be completed.</p></br><p>Take-home: To legally declare brain death, you must document apnea in the setting of absent brain-stem reflexes—do the apnea test.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Global cortical injury with brain-stem edema interrupts reticular activating system and cranial-nerve nuclei (III = fixed pupils, V/VII = absent corneal, IX/X = absent gag). When the medullary respiratory centers are destroyed, CO₂ rise fails to trigger breathing → apnea despite hypercapnia.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Diagnostic / Treatment Pearls<br /></br>Apnea test protocol:<br /></br>1. Preconditions met (see above).<br /></br>2. Get baseline ABG (PaCO₂ 35–45).<br /></br>3. Pre-oxygenate 100 % FiO₂ for 10 min.<br /></br>4. Disconnect ventilator, provide 100 % O₂ by cannula/catheter.<br /></br>5. Observe 8–10 min.<br /></br>6. Repeat ABG: if PaCO₂ ≥ 60 mm Hg (or ↑ ≥ 20) with no respiratory effort → positive.<br /></br>If patient becomes unstable (hypotension, desaturation) terminate &amp; use ancillary test (EEG, radionuclide cerebral blood flow).</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Brain DEATH check = D E A T H”:<br /></br>• Drugs cleared<br /></br>• EtCO₂ baseline<br /></br>• Absent brain-stem reflexes<br /></br>• Temperature ≥ 36 °C<br /></br>• Hypotension avoided (SBP ≥ 90)<br /></br>Then perform Apnea test.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Arterial blood gas analysis – ABGs are part of the apnea test but, by themselves, do not establish brain death. They measure PaCO₂ rise but must be paired with observation for spontaneous breaths.  </p></br><p>c. Blood cultures – Indicated for suspected sepsis; nothing in the vignette suggests infection. They do not aid in brain-death determination.  </p></br><p>d. Repeat CT head – Imaging can show structural cause but cannot confirm loss of brain function. CT already shows global injury; repeat imaging won’t change management.  </p></br><p>e. MRI brain – More sensitive for axonal injury but, like CT, insufficient for legal brain-death declaration.  </p></br><p>f. EEG – Ancillary study; used only when apnea test cannot be performed or is inconclusive. Not first-line when apnea test is feasible.  </p></br><p>g. Lumbar puncture – Used for meningitis, SAH, etc.; contraindicated in high ICP and irrelevant for brain-death evaluation.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Key Takeaway<br /></br>When coma + absent brain-stem reflexes are present, the required next step to declare brain death is the apnea test; other studies are ancillary or unnecessary.</p>
11. An 81-year-old woman comes to the clinic for a routine health maintenance examination. She has felt well but reports painless bruising on her right arm that she noticed 3 days ago after removing a bandage from a healing insect bite. She has noticed similar bruising during the past few years with bandage use and after accidental bumping of her arms against hard surfaces. She has hypertension treated with hydrochlorothiazide-triamterene. She takes no other medications. The patient appears well. Vital signs are normal. Physical examination shows a 4-cm area of ecchymosis with tearing of the skin on the right forearm at the site of the previously removed bandage. The remainder of the physical examination discloses no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management of this patient’s skin findings?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Coagulation studies</br>b. Oral vitamin D₃ therapy</br>c. Oral vitamin K therapy</br>d. Topical hydrocortisone therapy</br>e. Reassurance only</br></br></br><p>I want to be sure we’re on the same page before I write the full explanation.</p></br><p>In an elderly patient with painless ecchymoses and skin “tears” on the dorsal forearm after minimal trauma, the classic Board answer is “reassurance only” (senile/solar purpura → no work-up needed).  </p></br><p>However, the answer you marked as “correct” reads “a. Coagulation studies,” which does not fit the usual teaching for this vignette.</p></br><p>Could you please confirm which option you want treated as the correct answer?</p>
12. A 58-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 1-month history of nonproductive cough. He has not had shortness of breath or weight loss. He has type 2 diabetes mellitus, hypertension, and chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD). His medications are insulin, metformin, salmeterol, ipratropium, and lisinopril. During the examination, the patient coughs frequently. His temperature is 37 °C (98.6 °F), pulse is 78/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 156/70 mm Hg. Pulmonary examination shows a prolonged expiratory phase; no wheezes are heard. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient’s cough?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Adverse effect of lisinopril</br>b. Heart failure</br>c. Interstitial lung disease</br>d. Vocal cord dysfunction</br>e. Worsening COPD</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Adverse effect of lisinopril (ACE-inhibitor–induced cough)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 1-month history of a dry, non-productive cough<br /></br>• No new dyspnea, fever, weight loss, sputum, or wheezing<br /></br>• Vital signs essentially normal; lungs clear except for baseline prolonged expiration (expected in COPD)<br /></br>• Only medication on his list that is classically associated with a chronic dry cough = lisinopril (an ACE inhibitor)  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives that point away from other causes:<br /></br>– Heart failure: no orthopnea, PND, crackles, LE edema, weight gain<br /></br>– COPD exacerbation: no increased sputum, purulence, or wheezes; normal RR &amp; O2 sat given<br /></br>– Interstitial lung disease (ILD): no inspiratory “Velcro” crackles, clubbing, or occupational exposure<br /></br>– Vocal-cord dysfunction: no episodic wheezing/stridor, often provoked by exercise  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify chronic, nonproductive cough without systemic/respiratory decompensation.<br /></br>2. Review drug list for culprits → ACE inhibitors produce a bradykinin-mediated dry cough in ~5-20% of pts, typically within weeks to months of starting but can occur after years.<br /></br>3. No alternate diagnosis on exam or history fits better. Therefore, ACE-I adverse effect is the most likely etiology.</p></br><p>Take-home: Always think “ACE-I → dry cough” in any adult with an otherwise unexplained chronic cough.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• ACE converts angiotensin I → angiotensin II and degrades bradykinin.<br /></br>• ACE inhibition (lisinopril, enalapril, etc.) → ↑ bradykinin &amp; substance P in the pulmonary tissue → irritation of airway sensory nerves → persistent, non-productive cough.<br /></br>• Onset: days to months (median ≈ 1–2 mo).<br /></br>• Risk factors: female sex, Asian ethnicity, smoking, underlying lung disease—but can occur in anyone.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Management<br /></br>1. Discontinue ACE inhibitor.<br /></br>2. Substitute an angiotensin-receptor blocker (ARB) such as losartan or valsartan; ARBs do NOT increase bradykinin, so cough resolves in 1–4 weeks.<br /></br>3. No additional testing is necessary if temporal relationship to ACE-I is clear and exam is otherwise unremarkable.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ACE puts a tickle in your ACE-way”:<br /></br>• ACE = Accumulated Cough from Elevated bradykinin.<br /></br>Visual: imagine a “lisinopril lace” tickling the back of the throat causing a dry cough.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>b. Heart failure<br /></br>– Would cause exertional dyspnea, orthopnea, crackles, peripheral edema, possible S3. None are present; vitals stable.</p></br><p>c. Interstitial lung disease<br /></br>– Dry cough + dyspnea, bibasilar end-inspiratory crackles (“Velcro”), clubbing, restrictive PFTs; unlikely with normal lung exam and no risk factors.</p></br><p>d. Vocal cord dysfunction<br /></br>– Episodic throat tightness, inspiratory stridor/wheezing during attacks, normal spirometry between episodes. Cough is not persistent daily for a month.</p></br><p>e. Worsening COPD<br /></br>– Typically triggers increased sputum volume/purulence, wheezing, or progressive dyspnea. Exam here shows no wheezes; patient feels well.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Core Concept<br /></br>Chronic, dry, non-productive cough in a patient on an ACE inhibitor = ACE-I cough due to bradykinin accumulation; switch to an ARB to resolve the symptom.</p>
13. A 57-year-old woman, gravida 4, para 4, comes to the office because of an 8-week history of urinary urgency. During this time, she has had sudden strong urges to void and is often unable to reach the bathroom in time. She urinates three to four times nightly. There is no pain with urination. She does not have urine loss when coughing, laughing, or sneezing. She has no history of serious illness. Examination shows a mild cystocele and mild rectocele. A Q-tip test shows no urethral hypermobility. The cervix, uterus, and adnexa are normal. Urinalysis shows:</br>RBC 50–100/hpf</br>WBC 0–1/hpf</br>Epithelial cells 3–5/hpf</br>Bacteria none</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Cystoscopy</br>b. Nitrofurantoin therapy</br>c. Oxybutynin therapy</br>d. Placement of a midurethral sling</br>e. Recommendation for the patient to keep a voiding diary</br>f. Ultrasonography of the pelvis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Cystoscopy  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 57-year-old post-menopausal woman (age &gt;35 = malignancy risk).<br /></br>• 8-week history of urgency, frequency, nocturia → looks like “urge incontinence/overactive bladder.”<br /></br>• UA: 50–100 RBC/hpf, essentially no WBCs or bacteria → painless micro-/gross hematuria without infection.<br /></br>• No stress symptoms (no leakage with cough), Q-tip test negative → rules out stress incontinence from urethral hyper-mobility.<br /></br>Key pattern: New‐onset irritative bladder symptoms + hematuria in an older patient = must rule out bladder/urothelial carcinoma.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify urinary complaint → urgency/urge incontinence.<br /></br>2. Check UA → significant hematuria.<br /></br>3. Hematuria in patient &gt;35 y with no infection = “alarm” finding.<br /></br>4. Next diagnostic step = look in the bladder and upper tract (cystoscopy ± CT urogram).<br /></br>Take-home message: Before treating presumed overactive bladder, always evaluate unexplained hematuria for malignancy.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Urothelial (transitional-cell) carcinoma often produces painless hematuria and irritative voiding symptoms (frequency, urgency, dysuria).<br /></br>• Risk factors: age, smoking, occupational exposures, cyclophosphamide, pelvic radiation. (History not always present).<br /></br>• The tumor irritates the detrusor muscle → overactivity → urge symptoms.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment Pearls<br /></br>• Initial hematuria work-up (per AUA/ACR):<br /></br>  – Cystoscopy to visualize bladder &amp; urethra ± biopsy.<br /></br>  – Imaging (CT urogram or ultrasound) for upper tracts.<br /></br>• Only after malignancy is excluded do you treat overactive bladder (lifestyle, antimuscarinics, β3-agonists, Botox, neuromodulation).<br /></br>• Stress incontinence (leak with Valsalva, positive Q-tip) is treated surgically with mid-urethral sling.<br /></br>• Recurrent UTIs show pyuria/bacteriuria → treat with antibiotics.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Blood + Bladder Bother in someone Beyond 35 → Bring the Scope.”<br /></br>(Any hematuria + irritative symptoms in adult → cystoscopy.)</p></br><hr /></br><p>Why the Other Options Are Wrong<br /></br>b. Nitrofurantoin therapy<br /></br>   • Would address cystitis, but UA lacks pyuria/bacteriuria; treating empirically delays malignancy work-up.  </p></br><p>c. Oxybutynin therapy<br /></br>   • First-line drug for overactive bladder, but you must exclude structural lesions (e.g., cancer, stones) causing hematuria first.  </p></br><p>d. Mid-urethral sling<br /></br>   • Used for stress incontinence (leakage with cough/laugh, positive Q-tip). Patient has urge incontinence and no urethral hyper-mobility.  </p></br><p>e. Voiding diary<br /></br>   • Helpful for characterizing incontinence sub-type, but hematuria is an immediate red flag that supersedes conservative measures.  </p></br><p>f. Ultrasonography of the pelvis<br /></br>   • Pelvic US assesses uterus/ovaries; renal/bladder US might miss small bladder tumors. Cystoscopy directly visualizes mucosa and is guideline-recommended initial test for lower-tract hematuria.  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Core Takeaway<br /></br>In any patient with unexplained hematuria—especially those &gt;35 years—rule out urothelial carcinoma with cystoscopy before initiating symptomatic therapy for presumed benign bladder conditions.</p>
14. A 24-year-old man is being evaluated in the hospital because of increasing oxygen requirements. He was admitted earlier today because of an isolated comminuted pelvic fracture he sustained when he was pinned between a forklift and a wall while working at a warehouse. On admission, he received 6 units of packed red blood cells, 6 units of fresh frozen plasma, and 5 units of platelets for resuscitation. He underwent external pelvic fixation with right lower extremity fasciotomies for treatment of compartment syndrome. He was extubated at the completion of the operation. Medical history is unremarkable, and he takes no routine medications. Six hours later, temperature is 37.5 °C (99.5 °F), pulse is 120/min, respirations are 22/min, and blood pressure is 105/65 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on an FiO₂ of 1.0 via non-rebreather mask shows an oxygen saturation of 80%. Physical examination discloses tachypnea. Breath sounds are coarse bilaterally. The patient is intubated and mechanically ventilated. Arterial blood gas analysis on an FiO₂ of 1.0 discloses a PaO₂ of 60 mm Hg. Chest x-ray shows bilateral patchy infiltrates. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient’s respiratory failure?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Aspiration pneumonia</br>b. Fat embolism</br>c. Pulmonary contusion</br>d. Pulmonary embolism</br>e. Transfusion-related acute lung injury</br>f. Ventilator-associated pneumonia</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Transfusion-related acute lung injury (TRALI)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Massive transfusion: 6 U PRBC + 6 U FFP + 5 U platelets within a few hours – classic set-up for a transfusion reaction.<br /></br>• Onset ≤ 6 hours after transfusion → key TRALI criterion.<br /></br>• Severe hypoxemia: PaO₂ 60 mm Hg on FiO₂ 1.0 → PaO₂/FiO₂ ≈ 60 (ARDS range).<br /></br>• Bilateral patchy infiltrates without evidence of volume overload → non-cardiogenic pulmonary edema.<br /></br>• No chest trauma, no neuro changes, no petechiae, no long delay → argues against fat embolism or pulmonary contusion.</p></br><p>Take-home pattern: “Acute (&lt;6 h) bilateral infiltrates + refractory hypoxemia after large-volume blood product administration = TRALI.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify temporal relationship (transfusion finished &lt; 6 h ago).<br /></br>2. Recognize ARDS picture (PaO₂/FiO₂ &lt; 300 with bilateral opacities).<br /></br>3. Exclude cardiogenic edema (no HTN, no JVD/S3; young healthy heart; received component therapy rather than massive crystalloids).<br /></br>4. Apply definition → TRALI is the only reaction fulfilling all above.</p></br><p>Core concept: Donor anti-leukocyte antibodies activate recipient neutrophils in the pulmonary microvasculature → capillary leakage → acute, non-cardiogenic pulmonary edema.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Most commonly donor anti-HLA or anti-neutrophil Abs in plasma-containing products (FFP, platelets).<br /></br>• Mechanism: Neutrophil sequestration &amp; degranulation → ↑ pulmonary capillary permeability.<br /></br>• Onset: Within 6 h of transfusion (often 1–2 h).<br /></br>• Clinical: Acute hypoxemia, dyspnea, fever, hypotension or normotension, bilateral infiltrates.<br /></br>• Mortality ≈ 5–10 %. No long-term sequelae in survivors.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Management &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>Immediate:<br /></br>1. Stop transfusion.<br /></br>2. Supportive care: oxygen, often mechanical ventilation with low tidal-volume ARDS protocol.<br /></br>3. Hemodynamic support as needed (rarely diuretics—fluid overload is not the issue).  </p></br><p>No role for steroids or diuretics. Future: patient should receive washed or leukoreduced products.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“TRALI = Two-Hit Lung Injury”<br /></br>• Hit 1: Transfusion (donor Abs)<br /></br>• Hit 2: Recipient neutrophils → Lung capillary leak<br /></br>Remember the 6’s:<br /></br>• &lt; 6 hours after transfusion<br /></br>• PaO₂/FiO₂ &lt; 200 (often &lt; 100)<br /></br>• 6 letters in the mnemonic word “TRALI.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Aspiration pneumonia<br /></br>– Usually follows witnessed gastric content aspiration, has focal/lobar infiltrates (dependent segments) and develops over 24–48 h; not immediately after transfusion and not diffuse ARDS.</p></br><p>b. Fat embolism<br /></br>– Classically 24–72 h after long-bone/pelvic fractures and presents with triad: respiratory distress, neurologic signs (confusion), and petechial rash. Onset here is too early (6 h) and there are no neuro or skin findings.</p></br><p>c. Pulmonary contusion<br /></br>– Requires thoracic trauma; manifests within 24 h with focal opacities at site of impact. Patient had no chest trauma.</p></br><p>d. Pulmonary embolism<br /></br>– Produces hypoxemia but typically clear CXR (“Westermark sign”) or focal wedge; does not cause bilateral alveolar infiltrates and ARDS pattern immediately post-transfusion.</p></br><p>f. Ventilator-associated pneumonia<br /></br>– Requires ≥ 48 h of mechanical ventilation; patient was extubated after OR and re-intubated only minutes ago, far too early.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In any patient who develops abrupt, severe hypoxemia with bilateral infiltrates within 6 hours of receiving plasma-containing blood products, think TRALI, stop the transfusion, and provide supportive respiratory care.</p>
15. A 62-year-old man comes to the physician 24 hours after a 20-minute episode of weakness of the right side of the face and right upper and lower extremities. His blood pressure is 130/78 mm Hg. Cardiac and neurologic examinations, an ECG, and a CT scan of the head show normal findings. Carotid duplex ultrasonography shows less than 50% stenosis of the proximal left internal carotid artery. Which of the following interventions will result in the greatest reduction of this patient’s risk for a cerebral infarction?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Aspirin therapy</br>b. Dipyridamole therapy</br>c. Propranolol therapy</br>d. Warfarin therapy</br>e. Left carotid endarterectomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Aspirin therapy  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues: 62-year-old, transient (20-min) unilateral face/arm/leg weakness → classic transient ischemic attack (TIA).<br /></br>• Normal CT: no established infarct (expected in TIA).<br /></br>• Carotid duplex: &lt;50 % stenosis of symptomatic side → considered “mild” stenosis.<br /></br>• No atrial fibrillation on ECG, no valvular disease, BP reasonable (130/78).<br /></br>• Question: “Which intervention gives the greatest reduction in future cerebral infarction risk?” i.e., evidence-based secondary prevention after a non-cardioembolic TIA with mild carotid disease.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify event = TIA → mandates secondary stroke prevention.<br /></br>2. Determine stroke subtype: non-cardioembolic (normal ECG, no valve disease) and NOT high-grade carotid (&gt;70 %).<br /></br>3. Guideline hierarchy for non-cardioembolic TIA:<br /></br>   • First-line: antiplatelet—aspirin 50–325 mg daily (Class I, Level A evidence).<br /></br>   • Addition of dipyridamole or switch to clopidogrel are alternatives but aspirin monotherapy already confers the largest evidence-based risk reduction compared with placebo.<br /></br>4. Surgical/anticoag options are reserved for other scenarios (see below).</p></br><p>Take-home: After a TIA due to presumed small-artery or low-grade carotid disease, daily aspirin is the single most effective, guideline-supported intervention to cut future stroke risk.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• TIA = brief cerebral ischemia without infarction. Platelet-rich thrombi from diseased vessels or carotid plaques are common.<br /></br>• Aspirin irreversibly inhibits platelet cyclo-oxygenase-1 → ↓ thromboxane A₂ → less platelet aggregation, reducing microthrombus formation that precipitates TIA/stroke.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• Antiplatelet options:<br /></br>  – Aspirin (gold standard).<br /></br>  – Aspirin + extended-release dipyridamole or clopidogrel are acceptable alternatives if aspirin-intolerant or recurrent events.<br /></br>• Anticoagulation (warfarin, DOACs) is indicated only for cardioembolic sources (e.g., atrial fibrillation, prosthetic valves).<br /></br>• Carotid endarterectomy (CEA) indicated when symptomatic ≥70 % stenosis (or 50–69 % in select men) plus operative risk &lt;6 %. Not helpful in &lt;50 %.<br /></br>• Aggressive risk-factor control: statin, BP control, smoking cessation—but the question asks for the SINGLE most effective intervention; evidence favors aspirin in this vignette.</p></br><p>Memory Tips<br /></br>“ASA for Any non-cardioembolic Stroke or Attack” → ASA = Aspirin; remember the A’s.<br /></br>“CEA only when Carotid is Critical” → &gt;70 % = Critical.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Dipyridamole monotherapy – Weaker stroke-prevention data; usually used only WITH aspirin (Aggrenox). Alone, inferior to aspirin.<br /></br>c. Propranolol – β-blocker treats HTN/angina; no proven stroke-recurrence benefit in normotensive patient and not recommended as sole therapy.<br /></br>d. Warfarin – Prevents cardioembolic stroke; increases hemorrhage risk and not indicated without atrial fibrillation or other cardioembolic source.<br /></br>e. Left carotid endarterectomy – Only beneficial if symptomatic stenosis ≥70 % (or 50–69 % men with low surgical risk). At &lt;50 %, surgery confers no benefit and may harm.</p></br><p>Core Concept<br /></br>For patients with a recent TIA or non-cardioembolic ischemic stroke and mild (&lt;50 %) carotid stenosis, daily aspirin remains the single best-proven therapy to reduce the risk of subsequent cerebral infarction.</p>
16. A 1-day-old male newborn is evaluated in the hospital nursery before discharge. He has swelling over his skull. He was born at term to a 27-year-old woman, gravida 1, para 1, following an uncomplicated pregnancy and forceps-assisted delivery. Apgar scores were 7 and 10 at 1 and 5 minutes, respectively. He weighs 4536 g (10lb. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows a 3 × 3-cm well-demarcated area of boggy swelling over the left parietal skull region that does not cross suture lines. The skin over the area appears normal. Which of the following is the most appropriate test to screen for potential complications associated with this patient’s skull abnormality?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Chromosomal microarray test</br>b. Determination of INR</br>c. Dilated funduscopic examination</br>d. Echocardiography</br>e. Measurement of serum bilirubin concentration</br>f. Platelet function assay</br>g. Ultrasonography of the head</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Measurement of serum bilirubin concentration  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “3 × 3-cm well-demarcated … boggy swelling … does NOT cross suture lines” → Classic description of a cephalohematoma (subperiosteal hemorrhage limited by cranial sutures).<br /></br>• Forceps-assisted delivery &amp; macrosomia (birth wt 4536 g) are strong risk factors for scalp birth trauma.<br /></br>• Normal skin color and vitals, no systemic instability → rules out infection or subgaleal hemorrhage (which is ill-defined, crosses sutures, and may cause shock).  </p></br><p>Key positives: instrumented delivery, localized fluctuant mass, confined by sutures.<br /></br>Key negatives: no crossing of sutures, no hemodynamic compromise.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Cephalohematoma = pooling of blood between skull bone and periosteum → RBC breakdown over 1–2 weeks → excess unconjugated bilirubin → neonatal hyperbilirubinemia → risk of kernicterus if levels are high.<br /></br>Therefore every infant with a sizable cephalohematoma warrants early bilirubin assessment (transcutaneous or serum) and follow-up.  </p></br><p>Take-home: Local scalp bleeding lesions (cephalohematoma, bruising) raise bilirubin; screen early.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Shearing of subperiosteal vessels during operative vaginal delivery or macrosomic birth.<br /></br>• Because the periosteum adheres at sutures, blood collection is suture-bounded.<br /></br>• Blood resorption → hemolysis → ↑ unconjugated bilirubin load on immature neonatal liver → physiologic jaundice can be exaggerated → kernicterus (bilirubin encephalopathy) if not recognized.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostics &amp; Management<br /></br>1. Baseline total serum bilirubin (or TcB).<br /></br>2. Plot on Bhutani nomogram to determine need for:<br /></br>   • Early repeat level (q-12–24 h)<br /></br>   • Phototherapy<br /></br>   • Exchange transfusion if severe.<br /></br>3. Parental education &amp; close outpatient follow-up (2–3 d).<br /></br>Most cephalohematomas resolve spontaneously; aspiration is contraindicated (infection risk).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Cephalo- Heme- (blood) → Heme breakdown → Bilirubin”<br /></br>Mnemonic: “Cephalo BILI check.”<br /></br>Caput succedaneum: “Caput Crosses” sutures (edema, no bilirubin worry). Subgaleal: “Scary Sub” (crosses &amp; shock).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Chromosomal microarray – used for congenital anomalies or developmental delay, not isolated scalp hematoma.<br /></br>b. INR – evaluates coagulation disorders; cephalohematoma here is traumatic, not coagulopathic (normal vitals, no bleeding elsewhere).<br /></br>c. Dilated funduscopic exam – screens for retinopathy of prematurity in preterm/oxygen-exposed infants, unrelated to scalp hematoma.<br /></br>d. Echocardiography – indicated for murmurs, congenital heart disease suspicion, not scalp bleeding.<br /></br>f. Platelet function assay – for suspected platelet disorders (petechiae, mucosal bleeding), not traumatic subperiosteal bleed.<br /></br>g. Cranial ultrasonography – useful for intraventricular hemorrhage in preterm infants or to rule out intracranial bleed if neurologic signs present; localized periosteal bleed without neuro deficits does not require it.  </p></br><p>Key differentiator: Only bilirubin measurement directly addresses a predictable, serious downstream complication (hyperbilirubinemia) of cephalohematoma.</p>
17. A 37-year-old woman comes to the physician for a follow-up examination. She has had hypopituitarism since she underwent surgical excision of a large pituitary tumor followed by radiation therapy 15 years ago. She says that she is generally doing well but has noticed some increased fatigue, sleepiness, and a 2.3-kg (5-lb weight gain during the past 6 months. Medications include levothyroxine, hydrocortisone, and an oral contraceptive. Her vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows no abnormalities. The most appropriate next step in management is measurement of which of the following?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. 24-Hour radioactive iodine uptake</br>b. Serum free thyroxine (FT₄) concentration</br>c. Serum free triiodothyronine (FT₃) concentration</br>d. Serum thyroid-stimulating hormone concentration</br>e. Serum thyrotropin-releasing hormone concentration</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Serum free thyroxine (FT₄) concentration  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key condition: Long-standing panhypopituitarism after pituitary surgery + radiation.<br /></br>• Current therapy: Physiologic hormone replacement (levothyroxine, hydrocortisone, estrogen/progestin).<br /></br>• Presenting symptoms: Fatigue, sleepiness, mild weight gain – classic for under-replacement of thyroid hormone.<br /></br>• Crucial clue: Central (secondary) hypothyroidism → pituitary TSH secretion is absent or unreliable. Therefore TSH cannot be used to titrate levothyroxine.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Patient is already on levothyroxine.<br /></br>2. Symptoms suggest relative hypothyroidism.<br /></br>3. In central hypothyroidism TSH is low, normal, or occasionally mildly elevated but biologically inactive → not a valid marker of adequacy.<br /></br>4. Best way to assess adequacy = directly measure serum FT₄ (goal upper-half of normal range).<br /></br>Take-home: In any patient with pituitary disease, dose adjustments of thyroid replacement are guided by FT₄, not TSH.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Primary hypothyroidism: thyroid gland failure → ↑TRH, ↑TSH; TSH is sensitive gauge of therapy.<br /></br>• Secondary (pituitary) or tertiary (hypothalamic) hypothyroidism: ↓ or inappropriately “normal” TSH due to pituitary/hypothalamic damage.<br /></br>• Exogenous levothyroxine provides T₄ → converted peripherally to T₃ → negative feedback on TRH/TSH absent or blunted because axis is damaged.  </p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• Target FT₄: mid-to-upper normal range.<br /></br>• Check FT₄ 6–8 weeks after any dose change.<br /></br>• Other hormones: cortisol replacement (hydrocortisone), sex-steroid replacement as needed.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Central = Check the Central hormone” → go straight to the effector (FT₄) not the messenger (TSH).<br /></br>Mnemonic: PITUITARY problem → Pretend TSH Is Trash.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. 24-Hour radioactive iodine uptake<br /></br>   • Used to evaluate etiology of hyperthyroidism (Graves vs thyroiditis vs toxic nodule). Not useful in hypothyroidism or dose titration.  </p></br><p>c. Serum free triiodothyronine (FT₃)<br /></br>   • FT₃ fluctuates, is last to fall in hypothyroidism, and is not recommended for replacement monitoring.  </p></br><p>d. Serum thyroid-stimulating hormone (TSH)<br /></br>   • Appropriate for primary hypothyroidism, but unreliable in central disease because pituitary can’t mount proper TSH response. Would likely appear “normal,” masking under-replacement. Common test-taking trap.  </p></br><p>e. Serum thyrotropin-releasing hormone (TRH)<br /></br>   • Virtually never measured clinically due to hypothalamic secretion and rapid degradation; no role in assessing thyroid hormone replacement.  </p></br><p>Core Takeaway<br /></br>In central (secondary or tertiary) hypothyroidism, levothyroxine dosing must be guided by serum FT₄ levels, NOT TSH.</p>
18. A 64-year-old man comes to the office to request help for alcohol use disorder. He says he has tried to stop drinking alcohol on his own on a few occasions but has relapsed within several days each time. He has never had delirium tremens or other severe withdrawal symptoms. He has not consumed alcohol during the past 7 days. He reports no substantial symptoms of alcohol withdrawal at this time. He has severe degenerative spine disease, for which he is on long-term disability leave. He has been taking prescribed hydrocodone and acetaminophen for 11 years. He does not smoke cigarettes. He has been drinking six 12-oz beers daily for 9 years. He is divorced and lives alone. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows decreased movement in the low back secondary to pain. No other abnormalities are noted. Which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy for this patient’s alcohol use disorder?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Acamprosate</br>b. Bupropion</br>c. Lorazepam</br>d. Naltrexone</br>e. Paroxetine</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Acamprosate  </p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Goal: long-term relapse prevention in alcohol use disorder (AUD).<br /></br>• Currently 7 days sober → no acute withdrawal management needed.<br /></br>• Chronic prescribed opioid (hydrocodone) for spine disease → any drug that blocks μ-opioid receptors will precipitate withdrawal and abolish analgesia.<br /></br>• No mention of depression or anxiety that would warrant an SSRI or bupropion specifically for mood.  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives: motivated to stay abstinent, opioid use.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no current withdrawal, no opioid abstinence window, no depressive/anxious symptoms stated.  </p></br><p>Pattern: “Opioids on board + wants relapse prevention” → choose acamprosate, avoid naltrexone.  </p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Determine phase of care → relapse prevention (patient already abstinent).<br /></br>2. Check first-line pharmacotherapies → naltrexone or acamprosate.<br /></br>3. Screen for contraindications:<br /></br>   • Naltrexone: active opioid use, acute hepatitis/liver failure. CONTRAINDICATED here.<br /></br>   • Acamprosate: only contraindication is severe renal impairment (CrCl &lt; 30 mL/min) – not present.<br /></br>4.
