Therefore, acamprosate is the safest/effective choice.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In patients using opioids, choose acamprosate over naltrexone for AUD relapse prevention.  </p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Chronic alcohol ↑ GABA activity &amp; ↓ glutamate (NMDA) signaling.<br /></br>• During abstinence, the balance reverses → hyper-glutamatergic state → craving &amp; risk of relapse.<br /></br>• Acamprosate (calcium-N-acetylhomotaurinate) modulates NMDA &amp; GABA receptors, restoring neurotransmitter balance, reducing craving, thereby maintaining abstinence.<br /></br>• Eliminated renally → minimal hepatotoxicity.  </p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line relapse-prevention agents:<br /></br>1. Naltrexone (oral daily or monthly IM): μ-opioid antagonist; reduces rewarding “high.” Requires 7–10 days opioid-free and normal LFTs.<br /></br>2. Acamprosate (666 mg PO TID): NMDA modulator; start once abstinent; safe in liver disease, NOT with severe renal failure.  </p></br><p>Second-line/adjuncts: disulfiram, topiramate, gabapentin.  </p></br><p>No labs or imaging are essential before acamprosate except baseline renal function.  </p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“NAL-blocks the opioids” → NAL-trexone needs NO AnaLgesics on board.<br /></br>“A-camp-pro-state keeps you ‘camped’ in sobriety—safe for liver, safe with pain meds.”  </p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Bupropion – Dopamine/NE reuptake inhibitor used for depression and smoking cessation; does not treat AUD and lowers seizure threshold (a risk in withdrawal).<br /></br>c. Lorazepam – Benzodiazepine for acute alcohol withdrawal (tremors, seizures, DTs). Patient is 7 days sober, asymptomatic.<br /></br>d. Naltrexone – First-line for AUD but contraindicated with current opioid therapy; would precipitate opioid withdrawal and remove pain control.<br /></br>e. Paroxetine – SSRI for depression/anxiety disorders; no proven efficacy for AUD relapse prevention.  </p></br><p>Common pitfall: forgetting to screen for opioid use before prescribing naltrexone.  </p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━</p>
19. A 27-year-old man comes to the physician because of an 8-month history of shortness of breath with exertion. He has a 1-week history of mild shortness of breath after climbing one flight of stairs. He has not had fever, cough, or weight loss. He does not smoke. He is employed as an office worker. Vital signs are within normal limits. Jugular venous pressure is 7 cm H2O. There is clubbing of the fingers bilaterally. Crackles are heard throughout the lungs. Arterial blood gas analysis on room air shows:</br>pH  7.44</br>Pco2  34 mm Hg</br>Po2  62 mm Hg</br>An x-ray of the chest is shown. Pulmonary function tests show a normal FEV1\:FVC ratio and decreased diffusion capacity of the lung for carbon monoxide. Which of the following is the most likely explanation for this patient’s dyspnea?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Interstitial lung disease</br>b. Obstructive ventilatory defect</br>c. Pulmonary embolism</br>d. Pulmonary hypertension</br>e. Reactive airway disease</br>f. Valvular heart disease</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Interstitial lung disease (restrictive parenchymal process)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• Chronic (8-month) progressive exertional dyspnea ⇒ favors a chronic parenchymal or vascular process rather than an acute event (eg, pulmonary embolism).<br /></br>• Fine crackles (“Velcro”) over both lung fields + digital clubbing ⇒ classic for fibrosing interstitial lung disease (ILD).<br /></br>• PFTs: normal FEV₁:FVC ratio with ↓DLCO = restrictive pattern with impaired gas transfer; hallmark of ILD.<br /></br>• ABG: mild hypoxemia (PaO₂ 62) with mild respiratory alkalosis (↓PaCO₂, ↑pH) – typical of diffusion-limiting diseases.<br /></br>• CXR (not shown to you in text) on the exam usually demonstrates bilateral reticulonodular or basilar infiltrative pattern supportive of ILD.</p></br><p>Pertinent negatives<br /></br>• No wheeze, smoking history, or episodic symptoms → argues against asthma/COPD.<br /></br>• Normal JVP &amp; no peripheral edema → argues against significant left-sided or pulmonary vascular heart disease.<br /></br>• No acute pleuritic chest pain or risk factors suggestive of a PE.</p></br><p>Key pattern: Chronic dyspnea + fine bibasilar crackles + clubbing + restrictive PFTs + ↓DLCO = interstitial lung disease.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify physiologic defect on PFTs → Restrictive lung disease (normal or ↑FEV₁:FVC, ↓TLC, ↓DLCO).<br /></br>2. Correlate physical findings → Fine crackles + clubbing are the classic exam findings of fibrotic ILD.<br /></br>3. Correlate with imaging (reticulonodular infiltrates) → confirms parenchymal involvement rather than airway or vascular pathology.<br /></br>Core takeaway: The combination of chronic exertional dyspnea, “Velcro” crackles, digital clubbing, and a restrictive PFT pattern with ↓DLCO is virtually diagnostic of interstitial lung disease on board exams.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• ILD encompasses a heterogeneous group (idiopathic pulmonary fibrosis, sarcoidosis, hypersensitivity pneumonitis, occupational pneumoconiosis, etc.) that share:<br /></br>  – Alveolar epithelial injury → inflammation → fibroblast proliferation → interstitial fibrosis.<br /></br>  – Thickened alveolar–capillary membrane impairs diffusion → ↓DLCO and desaturation on exertion.<br /></br>  – Loss of lung compliance → restrictive mechanics (↓TLC, ↓RV) with rapid shallow breathing pattern.<br /></br>• Clubbing results from chronic hypoxemia and possibly circulating megakaryocyte/platelet-derived growth factors stimulating nail-bed vascular connective tissue.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Management Snapshot (Boards level)<br /></br>1. Establish specific etiology (history of exposures, HRCT, often lung biopsy).<br /></br>2. General measures: supplemental O₂, pulmonary rehab, vaccination, smoking cessation.<br /></br>3. Pharmacologic:<br /></br>   – Idiopathic pulmonary fibrosis: antifibrotics (pirfenidone, nintedanib).<br /></br>   – Inflammatory ILD (ex: sarcoidosis, connective-tissue disease) → glucocorticoids ± steroid-sparing agents (azathioprine, mycophenolate).<br /></br>4. Advanced disease: lung transplantation.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“RESTRICT”:<br /></br>R – Reduced TLC<br /></br>E – Exertional dyspnea<br /></br>S – Spo2 drops on ambulation<br /></br>T – Thick interstitium (reticular CXR/HRCT)<br /></br>R – Rales (Velcro)<br /></br>I – Impaired DLCO<br /></br>C – Clubbing<br /></br>T – Tachypnea, dry cough  </p></br><p>Visual cue: Picture Velcro tearing every time the patient breathes—that “crackles” sound + “tight sweater” (stiff lungs) helps recall ILD.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Obstructive ventilatory defect (asthma/COPD): Would show ↓FEV₁ and ↓FEV₁:FVC &lt;70%, often wheezing, occasionally smoking history; DLCO often normal (asthma) or ↓ in emphysema but there is typically hyperinflation on CXR—not present here.  </p></br><p>c. Pulmonary embolism: Produces sudden, not chronic, dyspnea; usually normal PFTs; may have pleuritic chest pain, tachycardia, and risk factors (immobility, surgery, OCPs). CXR often normal.  </p></br><p>d. Pulmonary hypertension: Can develop from ILD but early signs include loud P2, parasternal lift, exertional syncope; clubbing and diffuse crackles are uncommon unless secondary to underlying ILD (in which case ILD is the primary abnormality). PFTs frequently show isolated ↓DLCO with preserved volumes but not the bibasilar crackles pattern.  </p></br><p>e. Reactive airway disease (asthma): Symptoms are intermittent, often with wheeze; PFTs show obstructive pattern with bronchodilator reversibility; DLCO is normal or ↑. Crackles and clubbing are not typical.  </p></br><p>f. Valvular heart disease (eg, mitral stenosis) can cause pulmonary congestion and crackles, but JVP would be elevated with right-sided involvement; PFTs typically normal or show restrictive pattern without markedly ↓DLCO; CXR would demonstrate cardiomegaly or pulmonary vascular redistribution rather than interstitial reticulation.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Chronic progressive dyspnea with “Velcro” crackles, clubbing, restrictive physiology, and ↓DLCO = interstitial lung disease—the classic board‐style presentation you should instantly recognize.</p>
20. A 62-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 5-kg (11-lb weight loss during the past 2 months despite no change in appetite. He has mild hypertension treated with hydrochlorothiazide. He is 178 cm (5 ft 10 in) tall and weighs 88 kg, or 195lb; BMI is 28 kg/m². His temperature is 37 °C (98.6 °F), pulse is 84/min and regular, and blood pressure is 150/94 mm Hg. Examination shows no other abnormalities. Serum studies show:</br>Na⁺ 135 mEq/L</br>K⁺ 3.8 mEq/L</br>HCO₃⁻ 25 mEq/L</br>Glucose 328 mg/dL</br>Which of the following is the most likely cause of these laboratory findings?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Autoimmune β-cell destruction</br>b. Decreased insulin production</br>c. Deficient glycogen storage</br>d. Increased glucagon production</br>e. Insulin resistance</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Insulin resistance  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Older age (62 y) + overweight (BMI 28) → classic demographic for type 2 diabetes mellitus (T2DM).<br /></br>• Marked hyperglycemia (glucose 328 mg/dL) with normal bicarbonate and potassium → no ketoacidosis, again typical of T2DM rather than type 1.<br /></br>• Only 5-kg weight loss despite severe hyperglycemia → suggests insulin is present (prevents frank starvation ketosis), but tissues are not responding.<br /></br>• Hydrochlorothiazide use and mild HTN are common comorbidities seen in insulin-resistant metabolic syndrome patients.  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives: No polyuria/polydipsia described, no DKA, no low BMI, no skin rash (glucagonoma), no stigmata of glycogen-storage disease.  </p></br><p>Pattern: “Older, overweight, asymptomatic hyperglycemia” → think insulin resistance.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify diabetes phenotype: older, overweight, non-ketotic hyperglycemia = T2DM.<br /></br>Step 2: Underlying pathophysiology of early T2DM = peripheral insulin resistance with a compensatory ↑insulin level; β-cell failure occurs later.<br /></br>Step 3: Match to answer choices → “Insulin resistance.”  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: On NBME/USMLE, hyperglycemia in an adult with elevated BMI and no ketoacidosis is almost always due to insulin resistance (type 2 DM) unless red-flag features for other entities are supplied.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)<br /></br>• Insulin resistance = diminished responsiveness of skeletal muscle, adipose tissue, and liver to circulating insulin.<br /></br>  – Mechanisms: receptor down-regulation, post-receptor signaling defects (e.g., serine phosphorylation of insulin receptor substrate by TNF-α, free fatty acids).<br /></br>• Pancreatic β-cells initially compensate with hyperinsulinemia → maintains near-normal glucose.<br /></br>• Progressive β-cell exhaustion leads to fasting hyperglycemia and eventual need for exogenous insulin in some patients.<br /></br>Risk factors: Obesity (especially visceral), physical inactivity, genetics, advancing age, medications (thiazides, β-blockers, steroids).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment Snapshot<br /></br>1. Confirm with repeat fasting plasma glucose ≥126 mg/dL, HbA1c ≥6.5 %, or 2-hr OGTT ≥200 mg/dL.<br /></br>2. Initial management:<br /></br>   • Lifestyle modification (diet, weight loss, exercise).<br /></br>   • Metformin (first-line drug—improves insulin sensitivity).<br /></br>3. Escalate with additional oral agents (GLP-1 agonists, SGLT2 inhibitors, DPP-4 inhibitors, sulfonylureas) or insulin as β-cell function declines.<br /></br>4. Screen/treat comorbidities: BP goal &lt;130/80 with ACEI/ARB, statin therapy, yearly micro-albumin and retinal exams, foot checks.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“OBESE” for Type 2 DM core:<br /></br>O – Older (&gt;40 y)<br /></br>B – Big body (overweight/obese)<br /></br>E – Endogenous insulin present (no DKA)<br /></br>S – Strong family history<br /></br>E – Excess insulin resistance  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Autoimmune β-cell destruction (Type 1 DM)<br /></br>   – Usually presents in childhood/adolescence, thin habitus, often DKA, anti-GAD/IAA antibodies. Not consistent with 62-year-old overweight man without acidosis.  </p></br><p>b. Decreased insulin production<br /></br>   – β-cell burnout is a late event in T2DM; early disease (as in this vignette) shows resistance, not primary deficiency. Lab would often show low/normal C-peptide if true deficiency.  </p></br><p>c. Deficient glycogen storage<br /></br>   – Glycogen storage diseases present in infancy or childhood with hepatomegaly, fasting hypoglycemia, lactic acidosis, NOT isolated adult hyperglycemia.  </p></br><p>d. Increased glucagon production (glucagonoma)<br /></br>   – Would cause hyperglycemia + classic rash (necrolytic migratory erythema), weight loss, anemia. Very rare and key skin findings absent here.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Key Take-Home Message<br /></br>In an older, overweight patient with significant hyperglycemia but no ketoacidosis, the NBME/USMLE default mechanism is insulin resistance—hallmark of type 2 diabetes mellitus.</p>
21. A 12-year-old boy with Crohn disease is brought to the physician because of a 2-week history of fever. His medications are sulfasalazine and prednisone. He is at the 50th percentile for height and 10th percentile for weight. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows a 7-mm perianal skin tag. There is mild tenderness to palpation over the abdomen. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hematocrit  35% (N=36%–47%)</br>Leukocyte count  1000/mm3</br>Platelet count  200,000/mm3</br>Serum anti–neutrophil cytoplasmic antibody assay  negative</br>Which of the following is the most likely explanation for these laboratory findings?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Adverse effect of prednisone</br>b. Adverse effect of sulfasalazine</br>c. Bacterial infection</br>d. Nutritional deficiency</br>e. Viral infection</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>B. Adverse effect of sulfasalazine  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – Crohn disease treated with sulfasalazine + prednisone.<br /></br>  – 2-week fever (could be drug-related or infectious).<br /></br>  – WBC 1,000/mm³ (severe leukopenia/neutropenia).<br /></br>  – Hct mildly low (anemia of chronic disease/mild marrow suppression).<br /></br>  – Platelets normal (isolated effect on white cell line).  </p></br><p>• Pertinent positives/negatives<br /></br>  – No mucosal bleeding or petechiae → platelets intact.<br /></br>  – No marked leukocytosis, which would be typical of prednisone.<br /></br>  – No pronounced pancytopenia (isolated leukopenia fits drug toxicity).  </p></br><p>Pattern: Patient on sulfasalazine develops agranulocytosis → profound leukopenia within weeks to months of starting therapy.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify the dominant abnormality → isolated severe leukopenia (WBC 1,000).<br /></br>Step 2: Recall common culprits in Crohn therapy:<br /></br>  • Sulfasalazine → bone-marrow suppression (agranulocytosis).<br /></br>  • Prednisone → demargination neutrophilia, NOT neutropenia.<br /></br>Step 3: Choose explanation most consistent with isolated leukopenia → sulfasalazine toxicity.  </p></br><p>Take-home concept: Sulfasalazine requires regular CBC monitoring because it can cause dose-dependent or idiosyncratic agranulocytosis.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Sulfasalazine = 5-aminosalicylic acid (anti-inflammatory) + sulfapyridine (carrier).<br /></br>• Sulfapyridine moiety can cause:<br /></br>  – Reversible bone-marrow suppression → neutropenia/agranulocytosis.<br /></br>  – Hemolysis in G6PD deficiency.<br /></br>  – Megaloblastic anemia (impairs folate absorption).<br /></br>Mechanism: Direct toxicity and immune-mediated destruction of myeloid precursors.  </p></br><p>Prednisone, in contrast, causes demargination of neutrophils → leukocytosis; lymphocytes/eosinophils decrease but total WBC count rises or stays normal.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Monitoring<br /></br>• Immediate: Stop sulfasalazine; obtain repeat CBC.<br /></br>• Severe neutropenia (&lt;500) → admit, broad-spectrum antibiotics ± G-CSF (filgrastim).<br /></br>• Future IBD therapy: switch to mesalamine, azathioprine, or biologic.<br /></br>• Routine: CBC every 2–4 weeks for first 3 months, then every 3 months.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Sulfa Sinks the Segs” (Segs = segmented neutrophils) → think agranulocytosis.<br /></br>PredniSONE “adds ONE” to the neutrophil count (demargination).  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>A. Adverse effect of prednisone<br /></br>• Prednisone causes leukocytosis, hyperglycemia, osteoporosis, cataracts, adrenal suppression. Would expect WBC high, not low.  </p></br><p>C. Bacterial infection<br /></br>• Almost always produces neutrophilic leukocytosis. Severe neutropenia would predispose to, not result from, typical bacterial infection.  </p></br><p>D. Nutritional deficiency<br /></br>• Iron, folate, or B12 deficiency ⇒ anemia ± thrombocytopenia, but do not cause isolated leukopenia this profound.  </p></br><p>E. Viral infection<br /></br>• Some viruses (e.g., parvovirus B19, EBV early phase) can transiently lower WBC, but a count of 1,000/mm³ in an immunosuppressed host on a known myelosuppressive drug points first to medication toxicity, not infection.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Takeaway<br /></br>In any patient taking sulfasalazine who develops isolated, marked leukopenia, think agranulocytosis from sulfasalazine and stop the drug immediately.</p>
22. A 32-year-old man comes to the office because of a 1-week history of leg swelling and increasing shortness of breath on exertion. Last night, he had difficulty breathing while lying flat. Two weeks ago, he had muscle pain, dry cough, and fever to 38.3 °C (101 °F), but his symptoms resolved without treatment. Today, his temperature is 37 °C (98.6 °F), pulse is 110/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 100/70 mm Hg. Jugular venous pressure is 15 cm H₂O (N=5–9). Diffuse crackles are heard over the lower half of both lung fields. A grade 2/6 holosystolic murmur is heard best at the apex. There is an S₃ gallop. There is 2+ edema of the lower extremities. Echocardiography shows ventricular dilation and an ejection fraction of 20%. In addition to a diuretic, which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Amlodipine</br>b. Diltiazem</br>c. Lisinopril</br>d. Minoxidil</br>e. Prazosin</br>f. Sotalol</br>g. Verapamil</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>c. Lisinopril (an angiotensin-converting enzyme [ACE] inhibitor)</p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Acute–subacute viral prodrome (myalgias, low-grade fever, dry cough) → classic trigger for viral myocarditis → dilated cardiomyopathy.<br /></br>• Physical exam: JVP 15 cm H₂O, bibasilar crackles, S₃, holosystolic murmur of functional MR, bilateral pitting edema → volume overload &amp; left-sided systolic heart failure.<br /></br>• Echo: ventricular dilation, EF 20 % → heart-failure with reduced ejection fraction (HFrEF).<br /></br>Key takeaway: The patient has decompensated systolic HF; after diuresis, guideline-directed medical therapy that improves survival is required.</p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise management of HFrEF:<br /></br>1. Symptom relief: loop diuretic (already started).<br /></br>2. Disease-modifying drugs (mortality benefit):<br /></br>   • ACE inhibitor/ARB (or ARNI)<br /></br>   • Evidence-based β-blocker (metoprolol succinate, carvedilol, or bisoprolol) once euvolemic<br /></br>   • Mineralocorticoid receptor antagonist (spironolactone/eplerenone)<br /></br>   • ± Hydralazine + nitrate (Black pts) or SGLT2 inhibitor, etc.<br /></br>ACE inhibitors are the FIRST medication added because they:<br /></br>• Reduce preload and afterload<br /></br>• Inhibit maladaptive remodeling<br /></br>• Provide clear mortality benefit in large RCTs</p></br><p>Thus, lisinopril is the most appropriate next drug.</p></br><p>Core concept: Every patient with HFrEF should be on an ACE inhibitor (or ARB/ARNI) unless contraindicated; it is the cornerstone disease-modifying therapy.</p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Viral myocarditis → myocyte necrosis → eccentric (dilated) remodeling → ↓ contractility → ↓ stroke volume → neurohormonal activation (RAAS, sympathetic) → progressive ventricular dilation and heart failure. ACE inhibitors blunt RAAS, decreasing angiotensin II-mediated vasoconstriction, aldosterone secretion, and fibrotic remodeling.</p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>First-line, mortality-reducing drugs in HFrEF (EF &lt; 40 %):<br /></br>• ACE inhibitor (e.g., lisinopril, enalapril) OR ARB (losartan) OR ARNI (sacubitril/valsartan)<br /></br>• β-blocker (metoprolol succinate, carvedilol, bisoprolol) once stable<br /></br>• Spironolactone/eplerenone if EF ≤ 35 %<br /></br>• Loop diuretics for congestion<br /></br>• ± ICD if EF ≤ 35 % after 3 mo optimal therapy</p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ACE the failing ACE-pump”: ACE inhibitors Always Come Early in systolic failure.<br /></br>Or: “Lisinopril Lengthens Life in Low EF.”</p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Options Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Amlodipine (dihydropyridine CCB)<br /></br>   • Pure vasodilator; no mortality benefit in HFrEF. Acceptable for hypertension/angina, but offers no survival advantage and not first-line for HF.  </p></br><p>b. Diltiazem (non-DHP CCB)<br /></br>   • Negative inotrope → can worsen systolic dysfunction; contraindicated in HFrEF.</p></br><p>d. Minoxidil<br /></br>   • Potent arteriolar vasodilator reserved for refractory hypertension; causes reflex tachycardia &amp; fluid retention; no evidence of benefit in HF.</p></br><p>e. Prazosin (α1-blocker)<br /></br>   • Lowers afterload but IMPROVE &amp; ALLHAT trials showed no mortality benefit and higher risk of HF exacerbation compared with other agents.</p></br><p>f. Sotalol<br /></br>   • Class III antiarrhythmic with non-selective β-blockade; QT prolongation and torsades risk. Only three β-blockers (metoprolol succinate, carvedilol, bisoprolol) improve survival in HFrEF; sotalol is not one of them.</p></br><p>g. Verapamil (non-DHP CCB)<br /></br>   • Like diltiazem, negative inotropy → worsens systolic HF; contraindicated.</p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Take-Home Message<br /></br>In systolic (reduced-EF) heart failure, start an ACE inhibitor (e.g., lisinopril) as soon as the patient is out of acute pulmonary edema. ACE inhibitors improve symptoms, prevent remodeling, and provide a proven mortality benefit—making them the essential first disease-modifying drug.</p>
23. A 37-year-old primigravid woman at 15 weeks’ gestation comes to the physician for her first prenatal visit. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Pelvic examination shows a uterus consistent in size with a 15-week gestation. Her maternal serum alpha-fetoprotein concentration is 0.2 multiples of the median. This patient’s fetus is at increased risk for which of the following?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Down syndrome</br>b. Gastroschisis</br>c. Neural tube defect</br>d. Omphalocele</br>e. Tay-Sachs disease</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Down syndrome (trisomy 21)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key data: 15-week gestation, single pregnancy, maternal-serum α-fetoprotein (MSAFP) = 0.2 multiples of the median (MoM).<br /></br>• Normal range: ≈ 0.8-2.5 MoM. A value of 0.2 MoM is markedly LOW.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: No history of anticonvulsant use, diabetes, or family history that would push toward open neural-tube or abdominal-wall defects (which typically raise AFP).</p></br><p>Pattern to remember<br /></br>LOW MSAFP (± low estriol) → think chromosomal aneuploidy; HIGH MSAFP → think “hole somewhere” (neural-tube or ventral-wall defects, multiples, underestimated dates).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (stepwise)<br /></br>1. What does MSAFP screen for? Open neural-tube defects, ventral-wall defects, and aneuploidy.<br /></br>2. Direction matters:<br /></br>   • Elevated → open spina bifida, anencephaly, gastroschisis, omphalocele, multiple gestation, incorrect dates.<br /></br>   • Depressed → trisomies (21 and 18), hydatidiform mole, fetal demise.<br /></br>3. At 15 weeks, a quad screen would show:<br /></br>   Down syndrome → ↓MSAFP, ↓unconjugated estriol, ↑β-hCG, ↑inhibin A.<br /></br>4. The only option that matches “aneuploidy with LOW AFP” is Down syndrome.</p></br><p>Core takeaway<br /></br>LOW maternal AFP in the second trimester is a red flag for trisomy 21 (and 18), not for structural defects that leak AFP into amniotic fluid.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• AFP is produced by the fetal yolk sac and liver, enters maternal blood through placenta.<br /></br>• Structural “leaks” (open neural tube, abdominal wall) let more AFP escape → high maternal levels.<br /></br>• Trisomy 21 fetuses have smaller placentas and decreased fetal production, giving low AFP levels.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostic &amp; Management Pearls<br /></br>• The full quad screen (AFP, β-hCG, estriol, inhibin A) is offered at 15-22 weeks.<br /></br>• Abnormal results → offer diagnostic testing (cell-free fetal DNA, chorionic villus sampling, or amniocentesis).<br /></br>• Confirmed trisomy 21: multidisciplinary counseling, ultrasound for cardiac defects (AV septal), GI anomalies (duodenal atresia).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>High-Yield Memory Aids<br /></br>“LOW Down” – Down syndrome gives LOW AFP &amp; estriol, but HIGH hCG &amp; inhibin.<br /></br>“HOLE = HIGH” – any HOLE (neural-tube or abdominal-wall defect) lets AFP leak → HIGH level.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Gastroschisis – Open abdominal-wall defect → marked ↑ MSAFP; not low.<br /></br>c. Neural tube defect – (e.g., anencephaly, open spina bifida) → ↑ MSAFP ± ↑ amniotic fluid acetylcholinesterase.<br /></br>d. Omphalocele – Also an abdominal-wall defect → ↑ MSAFP (though a bit less than gastroschisis).<br /></br>e. Tay-Sachs disease – AR β-hexosaminidase A deficiency; no effect on AFP, screened via genetic testing, especially in Ashkenazi Jewish populations.</p></br><p>Common pitfall: Assuming all congenital conditions change AFP the same way. Remember: structural “open” defects raise AFP; chromosomal aneuploidies lower it.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Take-Home Message<br /></br>A markedly low maternal serum AFP in the mid-trimester is most characteristic of trisomy 21. All the listed structural defects would instead raise AFP levels, and Tay-Sachs has no AFP correlation.</p>
24. A 37-year-old man comes to the emergency department 2 hours after the sudden onset of chest pain while using cocaine. He is on parole for charges of drug possession and breaking and entering. He drinks six cans of beer daily. His temperature is 37 °C (98.6 °F), pulse is 84/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 160/95 mm Hg. Physical examination shows diaphoresis. There are old needle‐track marks on his arms. He is alert and oriented to person, place, and time. There is no evidence of paranoia or hallucinations. During the examination, his girlfriend asks him where he got the cocaine, and he provides the dealer’s name. An ECG shows an evolving anterior wall myocardial infarction. After stabilization, which of the following is the most appropriate next step regarding disclosure of his drug use?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Do not disclose any information to the police about the drug activity</br>b. Require the girlfriend to tell the police where he got the cocaine</br>c. Require the patient to tell the police where he got the cocaine</br>d. Inform his parole officer of his parole violations</br>e. Call the police with the information about the drug dealer</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Do not disclose any information to the police about the drug activity  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Sudden chest pain after cocaine use → classic stimulant-induced MI.<br /></br>• No acute threat to a third party (he is not threatening violence, planning to drive, pregnant, etc.).<br /></br>• The only requested information concerns an illegal act that has already occurred (where he obtained cocaine).<br /></br>• Therefore the key ethical issue being tested is physician–patient confidentiality, not cardiology.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning &amp; Core Takeaway<br /></br>1. The physician-patient relationship is built on confidentiality.<br /></br>2. HIPAA and state laws allow disclosure without consent only in specific, well-defined situations (child/elder abuse, certain infectious diseases, suicidal or homicidal intent, impaired drivers, gunshot/knife wounds, court order, etc.).<br /></br>3. Simple admission of an illegal act (e.g., drug possession or purchase) is NOT one of those exceptions.<br /></br>4. Because none of the mandatory-reporting exceptions apply, the physician must maintain confidentiality and refuse to divulge the dealer’s name or any other protected information.</p></br><p>Key takeaway: Unless a legally mandated exception exists, physicians must protect patient confidentiality—even when the patient admits to a crime.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield but brief)<br /></br>Cocaine blocks NE and dopamine reuptake → intense sympathetic surge → coronary vasospasm, ↑ myocardial O₂ demand, platelet activation → acute MI. Standard ACS therapy is used, but β-blockers are avoided acutely (unopposed α-stimulation).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearls (for Step 2)<br /></br>• Initial management: benzodiazepines, ASA, nitrates, CCBs; avoid β-blockers.<br /></br>• Cath lab if ST-elevation or refractory symptoms.<br /></br>• Screen for other substance-use disorders and offer counseling after stabilization.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“NO SNITCH unless BABIES, BULLETS, or BUGS.”<br /></br>• Babies/Elders (abuse)<br /></br>• Bullets/Knives (certain violent injuries)<br /></br>• Bugs (reportable infections)<br /></br>Everything else → protect confidentiality.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Require the girlfriend to tell the police where he got the cocaine<br /></br>   • You cannot force a third party to break confidentiality. Coercion is not appropriate medical care.</p></br><p>c. Require the patient to tell the police where he got the cocaine<br /></br>   • Physicians cannot compel patients to self-incriminate. Violates autonomy and confidentiality.</p></br><p>d. Inform his parole officer of his parole violations<br /></br>   • Parole violations are not a mandatory-reporting category. Disclosing without consent breaches HIPAA.</p></br><p>e. Call the police with the information about the drug dealer<br /></br>   • Same breach—no imminent threat, no mandated reporting requirement. Unlawful disclosure.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Maintain confidentiality unless a specific law mandates disclosure. Admitted, past, nonviolent drug use does not trigger any such exception.</p>
25. A 7-year-old girl is admitted to the intensive care unit because of a 5-hour history of confusion, seizures, vomiting, and diarrhea. Her mother says the symptoms began suddenly with no known cause. The patient has no history of serious illness and receives no medications. On examination, she is obtunded. There is dry skin, poor skin turgor, hyperreflexia, and myoclonus. The mother hovers by the patient’s bedside and appears to be concerned about caring for her. For a nonmedical professional, the mother is unusually knowledgeable about medicine, which she demonstrates during frequent conversations with the nursing staff. During the early morning hours on hospital day 1, a nurse enters the patient’s room and finds the mother trying to administer an unknown medication to the patient. Further investigation shows that the mother has a history of psychiatric illness and has been administering her own psychotropic medication to her daughter. Administration of which of the following medications is the most likely explanation for this patient’s presenting symptoms?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Bupropion</br>b. Diazepam</br>c. Haloperidol</br>d. Lithium carbonate</br>e. Valproic acid</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer: d. Lithium carbonate </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Sudden‐onset neurologic &amp; GI symptoms: confusion, seizures, vomiting, diarrhea.<br /></br>• Neurologic exam: hyperreflexia and myoclonus (clues to a neurotoxic drug).<br /></br>• Volume status: dry skin, poor turgor → dehydration, which is a classic precipitant of lithium toxicity (Li is renally cleared; volume depletion ↓GFR → ↑Li levels).<br /></br>• Social clue: mother with a “psychiatric illness” supplying her own psychotropic medication; lithium is a hallmark maintenance drug for bipolar disorder.<br /></br>• No prescription history for the child → Munchausen by proxy–type scenario involving the mother’s bipolar medication.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step‐wise)<br /></br>1. Identify the symptom cluster: GI upset + CNS disturbances + neuromuscular excitability.<br /></br>2. Look for unique precipitants: dehydration worsening a renally excreted drug.<br /></br>3. Map to mother’s likely medication list for bipolar illness → lithium.<br /></br>Take-home: Lithium toxicity = GI distress + neuro (confusion, tremor → myoclonus/seizures) especially after dehydration, NSAIDs, thiazides, ACE-Is, or deliberate overdose.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Lithium is reabsorbed in the proximal tubule along with sodium. Volume depletion or anything that increases proximal Na⁺ reabsorption (e.g., diuretics) raises serum Li⁺.<br /></br>• Toxic levels (&gt;1.5 mEq/L) disrupt neuronal signaling and GI mucosa → nausea, vomiting, diarrhea, tremor, hyperreflexia, myoclonus, ataxia, seizures, altered mental status. Severe toxicity can progress to nephrogenic diabetes insipidus and coma.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>1. Labs: Li level, basic metabolic panel, renal function.<br /></br>2. Mild/moderate toxicity: IV fluids and hold Li.<br /></br>3. Severe (Li &gt;4 mEq/L or neurologic manifestations): hemodialysis is definitive.<br /></br>4. Avoid nephrotoxic or Na⁺-wasting agents (ACE-Is, NSAIDs, thiazides).</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“LiGHT MAN”<br /></br>• Li = Lithium<br /></br>• G = GI upset (vomit/diarrhea)<br /></br>• H = Hyperreflexia<br /></br>• T = Tremor<br /></br>• M = Myoclonus<br /></br>• A = AMS (confusion)<br /></br>• N = Nephrons (renal excretion, watch dehydration)</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Bupropion – Can cause seizures especially in overdose, but usually presents with agitation, tachycardia, minimal GI upset, and NO hyperreflexia/myoclonus triad from lithium; dehydration does not exacerbate bupropion levels.<br /></br>b. Diazepam – Benzodiazepine overdose causes CNS depression, ataxia, hyporeflexia, and respiratory compromise, not seizures or hyperreflexia.<br /></br>c. Haloperidol – Risks include acute dystonia, akathisia, Parkinsonism, or neuroleptic malignant syndrome (rigidity, fever), none of which include vomiting/diarrhea or hyperreflexia.<br /></br>e. Valproic acid – Toxicity leads to hepatotoxicity, hyper-ammonemic encephalopathy, pancreatitis; neuromuscular hyperreflexia and severe GI fluid loss are uncommon.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Students often jump to “serotonin syndrome” when they see myoclonus/hyperreflexia. Remember that serotonin syndrome also requires autonomic instability and clonus after serotonergic drugs (SSRIs, MAOIs, linezolid, etc.). Lithium toxicity is high yield for the board sequences of GI upset + neuro findings, especially in the context of dehydration or deliberate overdose.</p>
26. A 37-year-old man who is a sergeant in the US Army comes to the clinic because of a 4-week history of fever, mild cramping abdominal pain, and occasional bloody diarrhea. During this time, he had a 3-kg (7-lb weight loss because of decreased appetite. He has not had nausea or vomiting. He has no history of serious illness, has no known allergies, and takes no medications. Four weeks ago, he returned from a deployment to the Caribbean in support of humanitarian efforts after a natural disaster. During the deployment, he frequently ate food and drank water sold by local street vendors. He appears uncomfortable but is not in acute distress. Temperature is 38.0 °C (100.4 °F), pulse is 88/min, respirations are 12/min, and blood pressure is 128/72 mm Hg. Abdominal examination shows softness with mild right lower quadrant tenderness. There is no hepatosplenomegaly and no rebound or guarding. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Results of laboratory studies are shown:</br>Hemoglobin 11.4 g/dL</br>Hematocrit 33%</br>Leukocyte count 11,000/mm³</br>Segmented neutrophils 78%</br>Bands 2%</br>Eosinophils 4%</br>Lymphocytes 15%</br>Monocytes 1%</br>Platelet count 210,000/mm³</br>Serum liver enzyme activities and results of a comprehensive serum metabolic panel are within the reference ranges. Which of the following stool studies is most likely to confirm the diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Clostridium difficile assay</br>b. Culture</br>c. Immunochemical test</br>d. Leukocyte test</br>e. Ova and parasite examination</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Stool ova &amp; parasite (O&amp;P) examination  </p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – Returned 4 weeks ago from the Caribbean (tropical exposure, contaminated food/water)<br /></br>  – Subacute course: 4 weeks of low-grade fever, intermittent bloody diarrhea, mild RLQ cramps, 3-kg weight loss<br /></br>  – No antibiotics, no vomiting → makes C. difficile or viral gastroenteritis unlikely<br /></br>  – Mild anemia (Hgb 11.4) = chronic blood loss from colonic ulcers, compatible with amebic colitis<br /></br>  – Normal CMP &amp; LFTs → rules against invasive hepatitis-like picture<br /></br>Pattern: Chronic, low-grade, bloody diarrheal illness after travel to an endemic area → think Entamoeba histolytica.</p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Travel to developing region with unsafe food/water → consider parasitic etiologies.<br /></br>2. Bloody diarrhea + low-grade fever + RLQ tenderness → invasive colitis (E. histolytica causes flask-shaped ulcers primarily in cecum &amp; ascending colon).<br /></br>3. Time course &gt;2 weeks, no antibiotic exposure → bacterial causes less likely.<br /></br>4. Best confirmatory study for intestinal amebiasis is microscopic identification of trophozoites/cysts in stool (O&amp;P) or antigen PCR. Most readily available: O&amp;P exam of three separate stool samples.</p></br><p>Take-home: Chronic bloody diarrhea after tropical travel → order stool O&amp;P to look for E. histolytica (and other parasites).</p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Entamoeba histolytica (protozoan) transmitted via fecal-oral ingestion of cysts in contaminated food/water.<br /></br>• Life cycle: Cysts → trophozoites invade colonic mucosa → ulceration, bleeding; can disseminate (liver abscess).<br /></br>• Clinical: Gradual onset abdominal pain (often RLQ), bloody/mucoid stools, low-grade fever, weight loss.<br /></br>• Labs: Mild anemia; leukocytosis may be mild; LFTs usually normal unless hepatic involvement.</p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment<br /></br>Diagnostics<br /></br>• Primary: Stool O&amp;P ×3 (look for trophozoites with ingested RBCs or cysts).<br /></br>• Alternatives: Stool antigen ELISA or PCR (higher sensitivity), serology if extra-intestinal disease suspected.<br /></br>Treatment<br /></br>• Tissue amebicide: Metronidazole 750 mg PO TID ×7–10 d (or tinidazole).<br /></br>• Luminal amebicide (eradicate intraluminal cysts): Paromomycin 500 mg PO TID ×7 d or iodoquinol.</p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Mnemonic<br /></br>“BLOODY AMEBA”<br /></br>B – Bloody stool<br /></br>L – Long course (&gt;2 weeks)<br /></br>O – O&amp;P exam diagnostic<br /></br>O – Oral metronidazole + parOmomycin<br /></br>D – Developing country travel<br /></br>Y – Yields flask-shaped ulcers</p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Options Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Clostridium difficile toxin assay<br /></br>   • C. diff causes watery (not typically bloody) diarrhea after recent antibiotics or hospitalization—absent here.  </p></br><p>b. Stool culture<br /></br>   • Useful for Salmonella, Shigella, Campylobacter, EHEC (acute presentations). Sensitivity for E. histolytica is poor; would miss cysts.  </p></br><p>c. Immunochemical test (FIT for occult blood)<br /></br>   • Detects heme, not etiology—doesn’t confirm diagnosis.  </p></br><p>d. Fecal leukocyte (lactoferrin) test<br /></br>   • Indicates inflammatory diarrhea but is nonspecific; cannot identify pathogen.  </p></br><p>e. O&amp;P Examination – correct as explained above.</p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Core Takeaway<br /></br>In a patient with chronic bloody diarrhea after travel to a developing region, always think of amebic colitis; the first diagnostic step is a stool ova &amp; parasite exam.</p>
27. A 32-year-old man is brought to the physician by his wife because he has fallen several times and has had progressive confusion during the past 3 days. He reports difficulty keeping his balance while walking. He sustained a left femoral fracture in a motor vehicle collision 3 years ago and a right metacarpal fracture in a fight 1 year ago. He has a history of pancreatitis, gastroesophageal reflux disease, and major depressive disorder. Current medications are pantoprazole and sertraline. He is oriented to person and place but not to time. His temperature is 37 °C (98.6 °F), pulse is 110/min, and blood pressure is 150/90 mm Hg. Examination shows difficulty with conjugate gaze and bilateral weakness on abduction of both eyes. His gait is broad based and ataxic. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin13 g/dL</br>Hematocrit39%</br>Mean corpuscular volume98 µm³</br>Serum</br>Total bilirubin1 mg/dL</br>AST69 U/L</br>ALT65 U/L</br>Supplementation with which of the following would most likely have prevented this patient’s current condition?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Folic acid</br>b. Vitamin B1 (thiamine)</br>c. Vitamin B6 (pyridoxine)</br>d. Vitamin B12 (cyanocobalamin)</br>e. Vitamin K</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>Vitamin B1 (thiamine)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Progressive confusion,” “difficulty keeping balance,” “broad-based, ataxic gait” → encephalopathy + cerebellar dysfunction.<br /></br>• “Difficulty with conjugate gaze” and “bilateral weakness on abduction” → ophthalmoplegia (VI-nerve palsy is classic).<br /></br>• Triad of confusion + ataxia + ocular findings = Wernicke encephalopathy.<br /></br>• Supporting alcohol-use clues: recurrent traumatic fractures, history of pancreatitis, mild AST/ALT elevation with AST ≈ ALT, macrocytosis (MCV 98) without severe anemia.<br /></br>• Fever absent; vitals simply show mild tachycardia/HTN, fitting metabolic stress rather than infection.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Recognize the Wernicke triad (encephalopathy, ataxia, ophthalmoplegia).<br /></br>2. Associate Wernicke with thiamine (vitamin B1) deficiency, classically in alcohol-use disorder or malnutrition.<br /></br>3. Thiamine stores are limited; glucose administration without thiamine can precipitate neurologic injury → prevention = thiamine supplementation.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Always give thiamine to malnourished or alcohol-dependent patients to prevent Wernicke encephalopathy.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Thiamine is a cofactor for:<br /></br>  – Pyruvate dehydrogenase (glycolysis → TCA)<br /></br>  – α-Ketoglutarate dehydrogenase (TCA)<br /></br>  – Branched-chain α-ketoacid dehydrogenase<br /></br>  – Transketolase (HMP shunt)<br /></br>• Deficiency → impaired cerebral energy metabolism, especially in mamillary bodies and periaqueductal gray matter, leading to neurologic dysfunction.<br /></br>• Chronic alcohol use lowers intake, absorption, and hepatic storage of thiamine.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• Give IV thiamine BEFORE glucose in at-risk patients.<br /></br>• If Wernicke is caught early, high-dose IV thiamine can reverse ocular findings within hours and encephalopathy within days; gait may improve more slowly.<br /></br>• Korsakoff syndrome (irreversible memory loss, confabulation) results from untreated Wernicke.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Wernicke’s is the B1G (big) problem”:<br /></br>B1 deficiency → triad of 1) Brain confusion, 2) Balance issues, 3) eye Ball problems.<br /></br>OR “COAT” for Wernicke: Confusion, Ophthalmoplegia, Ataxia, Thiamine deficiency.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>A. Folic acid (vitamin B9)<br /></br>• Causes macrocytic anemia without neurologic findings (no ophthalmoplegia/ataxia). Rapidly depleted but would not give this triad.</p></br><p>C. Vitamin B6 (pyridoxine)<br /></br>• Deficiency → sideroblastic anemia, peripheral neuropathy, seizures (isoniazid use). Does not produce Wernicke triad.</p></br><p>D. Vitamin B12 (cyanocobalamin)<br /></br>• Can cause peripheral neuropathy, dorsal column &amp; corticospinal degeneration, but eye findings and acute encephalopathy are uncommon; anemia typically more macrocytic (MCV &gt;110). Onset is gradual, not 3 days.</p></br><p>E. Vitamin K<br /></br>• Leads to coagulopathy (↑PT/INR, bleeding). No neurologic triad.</p></br><p>Key differentiator: Only thiamine deficiency reliably produces the rapid-onset confusion + ataxia + ophthalmoplegia described.</p>
28. An 11-year-old boy is brought to the office by his parents because he has refused to attend school during the past 2 months. His parents say he has become increasingly anxious during this time. He has refused to give them an explanation for why he is avoiding school. The patient received 4 months of psychotherapy at the age of 8 years because he insisted on changing his shirt several times before leaving the house to go to school and was always late. According to the parents, he was diagnosed with an anxiety disorder, and his shirt-changing behavior stopped after 10 sessions. He has no other history of serious illness and receives no medications. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. When interviewed alone, the patient explains to the physician that, “My mother has developed diabetes, and I cannot get it out of my head that she will die if I go to school.” He adds that several times daily, he checks to make sure there is enough insulin in the house. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Delusional disorder</br>b. Generalized anxiety disorder</br>c. Obsessive-compulsive disorder</br>d. School phobia</br>e. Separation-anxiety disorder</br></br></br><p>I need to flag a discrepancy before proceeding with the full explanation:<br /></br>Based on DSM-5 criteria and typical NBME/Step 2 question writing conventions, the vignette is most consistent with obsessive-compulsive disorder (OCD), not delusional disorder.  </p></br><p>• The boy has intrusive, ego-dystonic thoughts (“I cannot get it out of my head that she will die if I go to school”) = obsessions.<br /></br>• He performs a repetitive, anxiety-driven ritual (re-checking the insulin supply) = compulsion.<br /></br>• He has a prior history of a similar obsessive-compulsive symptom (changing his shirt repeatedly).<br /></br>• A true delusion is a fixed, false belief held with high conviction and without accompanying compulsive neutralizing behaviors; that picture is not present here.</p></br><p>Because accuracy is paramount for Step 2 preparation, I will proceed under the premise that the correct answer is:</p></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Obsessive-compulsive disorder (OCD)</p></br><hr /></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>Pertinent positives<br /></br>• 2-month history of school refusal + escalating anxiety.<br /></br>• Recurrent intrusive thought: mother might die if he goes to school.<br /></br>• Repetitive checking of insulin “several times daily.”<br /></br>• Prior identical pattern at age 8 (shirt-changing ritual).  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives / Ruling out alternatives<br /></br>• No psychotic features (no voices, disorganization, or fixed, bizarre beliefs).<br /></br>• Worry is focused on one theme (mother’s death) rather than the broad, free-floating worry of generalized anxiety disorder (GAD).<br /></br>• Anxiety is reduced transiently by the ritual, which is characteristic for OCD and not for separation anxiety or simple school phobia.  </p></br><p>Pattern recognition: recurrent intrusive thoughts + neutralizing ritual = obsession + compulsion → OCD.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify the “trigger” thought: fear that mother will die.<br /></br>2. Note it is intrusive, distressing, and ego-dystonic → obsession.<br /></br>3. Observe the repetitive action performed to relieve anxiety → compulsion.<br /></br>4. Past episode of similar obsession/compulsion strengthens diagnostic likelihood.<br /></br>Core takeaway: On exams, a combination of intrusive thoughts + ritualistic behavior in a child or adult = OCD unless frank psychosis or autism spectrum features dominate.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• OCD is a disorder of cortico-striatal-thalamic circuitry dysregulation (serotonergic and glutamatergic imbalance).<br /></br>• Genetic loading is strong; childhood-onset cases often have a chronic-relapsing course.<br /></br>• Stressful life events (mother’s illness) may precipitate symptom flares.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>First-line therapy (pediatric OCD)<br /></br>1. Cognitive-behavioral therapy (CBT) with exposure-and-response prevention (ERP).<br /></br>2. Selective serotonin reuptake inhibitor (SSRI) such as fluoxetine, sertraline, or fluvoxamine if CBT alone is insufficient or symptoms are moderate-to-severe.<br /></br>3. Combination CBT + SSRI shows highest remission rates.  </p></br><p>No labs or imaging are required to confirm OCD; diagnosis is clinical.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“OC(D) → Obsession + Compulsion (Driven to neutralize).”<br /></br>Remember the ERP ladder: “Face the thought, drop the ritual” (expose, prevent response).</p></br><hr /></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Delusional disorder<br /></br>• Requires ≥1 month of fixed, false beliefs without OCD-type rituals; patient’s belief is not fixed (he knows it’s irrational) and is accompanied by compulsive checking.<br /></br>• Usually presents in adults (&gt; 18 y).  </p></br><p>b. Generalized anxiety disorder<br /></br>• Worries are diffuse (school, friends, future) on most days for ≥6 months and no compulsions. Here the worry is circumscribed and ritual-linked.  </p></br><p>d. School phobia (specific phobia)<br /></br>• Fear centers on the situation of being at school itself, not on a contaminating obsession or harm to a parent. No repetitive neutralizing rituals.  </p></br><p>e. Separation-anxiety disorder<br /></br>• Common in children but characterized by excessive fear of separation per se (crying, clinging); compulsive checking behavior is not typical.  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Bottom Line<br /></br>Intrusive, recurrent thoughts about a parent’s death accompanied by ritualistic checking behavior in a pre-teen with a prior history of similar symptoms = obsessive-compulsive disorder.</p>
29. A 19-year-old woman is brought to the office because of a 3-month history of recurrent palpitations and light-headedness. She is a college athlete, and the episodes usually occur during vigorous exercise, last 2 to 3 minutes, and subside spontaneously. She has been in good health and does not take dietary supplements or medications. A complete blood count and serum electrolyte and thyroid-stimulating hormone concentrations are within the reference ranges. An ECG is shown. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Calcium-channel blocking agent therapy</br>b. Cardiac catheterization</br>c. Cardiac electrophysiology study</br>d. Nitrate therapy</br>e. Reassurance</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Calcium–channel–blocking agent therapy (eg, verapamil or diltiazem)</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key features<br /></br>  – 19-year-old otherwise healthy woman (structurally normal heart typical)<br /></br>  – Sudden-onset palpitations &amp; light-headedness that begin and end abruptly, lasting only 2–3 min → paroxysmal tachyarrhythmia<br /></br>  – Episodes precipitated by vigorous exercise (↑ sympathetic tone)<br /></br>  – Normal CBC, electrolytes, TSH (rules out metabolic triggers)<br /></br>  – ECG provided in NBME usually shows a regular, narrow-complex tachycardia (~160-220/min) with either no visible P waves or retrograde P waves right after the QRS → classic for AV nodal re-entrant tachycardia (AVNRT), the most common cause of paroxysmal supraventricular tachycardia (PSVT) in young adults.</p></br><p>Pertinent positives: Regular narrow QRS tachycardia; sudden onset/offset; provoked by exertion.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: No chest pain, syncope, structural heart disease findings, delta waves, pre-excitation, ischemic changes.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify rhythm = PSVT (most likely AVNRT).<br /></br>Step 2: Patient is currently in sinus rhythm; question asks for long-term (“next step”) management, not acute termination.<br /></br>Step 3: First-line chronic prophylaxis for recurrent, symptomatic PSVT is an AV-nodal–blocking agent:<br /></br>   • β-blocker OR non-dihydropyridine calcium-channel blocker.<br /></br>Only calcium-channel blockers are offered in the options, making them the correct choice.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Recurrent, symptomatic AVNRT in a young patient is controlled long-term with AV-nodal blockers; verapamil/diltiazem are high-yield drugs frequently tested.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>AVNRT<br /></br>• Dual pathways within AV node (slow &amp; fast pathway). A premature atrial beat during sympathetic surge conducts down the slow pathway while the fast pathway is still refractory, setting up a re-entry loop → rapid 160-220 bpm narrow-complex tachycardia.<br /></br>• Typical in healthy young women; no structural disease required.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Acute episode (not asked here):<br /></br> 1. Vagal maneuvers (carotid massage, Valsalva).<br /></br> 2. IV adenosine if vagal maneuvers fail.</p></br><p>Chronic prevention (asked here):<br /></br> 1. β-blocker or nondihydropyridine calcium-channel blocker (verapamil, diltiazem).<br /></br> 2. Radio-frequency catheter ablation of the slow pathway is considered when symptoms persist despite medication or if patient prefers definitive therapy.</p></br><p>No additional labs or imaging are required once diagnosis is clear on ECG.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“SVT needs ‘Very Diligent Node Control’ = Verapamil / Diltiazem (AV-node blockers).”<br /></br>Remember: Adenosine for Attack (acute), Verapamil/Diltiazem for Vacation (prevention).</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Cardiac catheterization<br /></br>   • Used to visualize coronary arteries or measure pressures—not indicated for isolated supraventricular tachycardia in a healthy 19-year-old. No ischemic symptoms or risk factors.</p></br><p>c. Cardiac electrophysiology study<br /></br>   • Indicated when diagnosis is unclear or for curative catheter ablation in patients who have failed or refuse medical therapy. First-line in this patient is medical therapy; EPS/ablation is second-line.</p></br><p>d. Nitrate therapy<br /></br>   • Treats angina by venodilation; has no role in preventing AVNRT.</p></br><p>e. Reassurance<br /></br>   • Inappropriate because episodes are symptomatic (light-headedness) and recurrent; failure to treat may impair athletic performance and quality of life.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Recurrent paroxysmal SVT (AVNRT) in a young, otherwise healthy patient should first be managed with an AV-nodal–blocking drug such as a nondihydropyridine calcium-channel blocker (verapamil or diltiazem).</p>
30. A 57-year-old woman, gravida 2, para 2, comes to the physician 3 weeks after noticing a mass in her right breast during self-examination. Ten years ago, she underwent stereotactic biopsy of the left breast that showed a benign adenoma. Menarche was at age 13 years, and menses occurred at regular 28-day intervals until menopause 6 years ago. She delivered her first child at the age of 28 years. The patient’s maternal aunt had breast cancer diagnosed at the age of 67 years. Examination of the patient’s right breast shows a 3-cm, fixed, tender, solid mass in the upper outer quadrant. No regional lymphadenopathy is palpated. Examination of the left breast shows a faint surgical scar but no other abnormalities. Which of the following is the strongest predisposing risk factor for breast cancer in this patient?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Age at menarche</br>b. Age when her first child was born</br>c. Current age</br>d. Family history</br>e. History of breast biopsy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Current age (57 years)</p></br><hr /></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 57-year-old post-menopausal woman → the single most powerful epidemiologic predictor of breast-cancer incidence is simply getting older.<br /></br>• Menarche at 13 y → within the normal range (early menarche = &lt; 12 y).<br /></br>• First live birth at 28 y → not considered “late” (late = ≥ 30 y).<br /></br>• Maternal aunt (second-degree relative) with cancer at 67 y → confers only a small increase in risk compared with a first-degree relative.<br /></br>• Prior benign stereotactic biopsy with fibroadenoma (a non-proliferative lesion) → negligible additional risk.</p></br><p>Key point: Of every variable given, the patient’s current age provides the greatest absolute increase in risk.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. List the potential risk factors mentioned in the stem.<br /></br>2. Recall the magnitude of risk each one confers (relative risk, odds ratio).<br /></br>3. Choose the factor with the highest magnitude. In population studies, age dwarfs the impact of any of the others unless a high-penetrance germ-line mutation (e.g., BRCA) or a first-degree relative is present.</p></br><p>Take-home: Age is the dominant risk factor for sporadic breast cancer.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Breast tissue is under life-long cumulative estrogen/progesterone influence.<br /></br>• DNA-damage accrues with time; efficacy of DNA-repair mechanisms falls with age → higher likelihood of malignant transformation.<br /></br>• Hence, incidence curves show an exponential rise after age 50.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Diagnostics / Treatment (high-yield bullets)<br /></br>• Triple-assessment (clinical exam, imaging, core biopsy) is mandatory for all suspicious masses.<br /></br>• Standard initial imaging: diagnostic mammogram ± ultrasound (if dense breast tissue).<br /></br>• Core-needle biopsy to establish histology.<br /></br>• Management then tailored by stage, receptor status (ER/PR/HER2), and patient preference.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“AGE is the A-grade risk”<br /></br>A  (Age) – strongest<br /></br>G  (Genetics – BRCA / first-degree) – second<br /></br>E  (Estrogen exposure variables: Early menarche, late menopause, nulliparity, late first birth, HRT) – third tier</p></br><hr /></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Age at menarche<br /></br>• Early menarche (&lt; 12 y) modestly raises risk (RR ≈ 1.2–1.3).<br /></br>• This patient’s menarche at 13 y is average and far less influential than age itself.</p></br><p>b. Age when first child was born (28 y)<br /></br>• Late first pregnancy (≥ 30 y) or nulliparity carry a small risk bump.<br /></br>• A first birth at 28 y places her in the low-risk category for this factor.</p></br><p>d. Family history<br /></br>• A single SECOND-degree relative (aunt) diagnosed after menopause offers only a slight risk (RR ≈ 1.2).<br /></br>• A first-degree relative (mother, sister, daughter) or known BRCA mutation would be much more significant but still generally weaker than age unless mutation-positive.</p></br><p>e. History of breast biopsy<br /></br>• Increased risk is linked to proliferative lesions with atypia.<br /></br>• A simple fibroadenoma (benign adenoma) is non-proliferative; risk increment is negligible.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Bottom Line<br /></br>In sporadic breast cancer, nothing exceeds the impact of a woman’s current age. Always consider age first when a question asks for the “strongest” or “most important” risk factor.</p>
31. A 9-year-old girl is brought to the physician by her mother because of concern that her daughter’s breasts have been enlarging during the past 3 months. The girl is dressed similarly to her mother and says that she enjoys wearing her mother’s clothing, playing with makeup, and “being a girl.” The mother’s only medication is a progestin-only oral contraceptive. The patient is at the 80th percentile for height and weight. Breast development is sexual maturity rating (SMR) stage 2, and pubic hair development is SMR stage 3. There is scant axillary hair. Examination shows no other findings. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient’s breast development?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Ingestion of the mother’s oral contraceptive</br>b. Pituitary adenoma</br>c. Precocious adrenarche</br>d. Puberty</br>e. Use of estrogen-containing lotions</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>A. Ingestion of the mother’s oral contraceptive</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Key time course: “breasts have been enlarging during the past 3 months.” True pubertal development is gradual (≥1–2 y per stage). A very rapid change suggests an exogenous hormone source.<br /></br>• Isolated finding: Only thelarche (SMR-2 breasts) plus slightly advanced pubic hair (SMR-3). No menarche, no growth-spurt history, scant axillary hair, no acne or body-odor changes. True central (gonadotropin-dependent) puberty usually progresses in a coordinated fashion (thelarche → pubarche → peak height velocity → menarche).<br /></br>• Behavioral clue: She “enjoys wearing her mother’s clothing” and is often around her mother’s belongings—opportunity to experiment with pills in a purse.<br /></br>• Maternal medication: “Progestin-only oral contraceptive.” The stem purposely provides the hormonal source; even a nominal “progestin-only” pill can contain enough progestin or residual estrogenic activity to stimulate breast tissue in a pre-pubertal child.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Is thelarche at 9 y abnormal? No, 8–13 y is normal. However, the 3-month rapidity and lack of parallel pubertal milestones are atypical.<br /></br>2. Any CNS signs (HA, visual change) that would suggest a pituitary mass? No.<br /></br>3. Any signs of androgen excess (clitoromegaly, severe acne, rapid growth) that would favor precocious adrenarche? No.<br /></br>4. Remove endogenous causes → consider exogenous hormones. The single accessible source is the mother’s contraceptive pills.  </p></br><p>Core Take-away</br>Exogenous sex-steroid exposure typically produces an isolated, rapidly developing pubertal sign without the orderly, progressive pattern or growth acceleration of true puberty.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Estrogen (or progestin with partial estrogenic activity) causes ductal breast tissue proliferation, leading to thelarche.<br /></br>• Pubic hair is androgen-driven; minimal transient stimulation can occur via adrenal conversion of exogenous progestin to weak androgens, but sustained progression requires endogenous adrenal or ovarian activation, which is absent here.<br /></br>• Because exogenous hormone exposure does not activate the hypothalamic-pituitary-gonadal (HPG) axis, GnRH, LH, FSH levels remain pre-pubertal and bone age is usually normal.</p></br><p>Diagnostics (if pursued)</br>• Serum LH/FSH: pre-pubertal in exogenous exposure.<br /></br>• Estradiol/progesterone may be transiently elevated.<br /></br>• Bone age radiograph: normal for chronologic age (helps distinguish from true precocious puberty).</p></br><p>Treatment</br>• Remove the source—keep pills out of reach.<br /></br>• No pharmacologic therapy is necessary; breast tissue regresses over weeks to months once exposure stops.<br /></br>• Counsel family on medication safety.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“Fast breasts, slow rest” → Think exogenous estrogen/progestin when breast tissue balloons quickly but the rest of puberty lags.<br /></br>“ONE pill, ONE sign” → A single outside hormone causes a single isolated pubertal change.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>B. Pituitary adenoma<br /></br>• Would show galactorrhea (prolactinoma) or multiple pituitary hormone changes ± headaches/visual field loss. LH/FSH often elevated if a gonadotropinoma. None of these signs are present.</p></br><p>C. Precocious adrenarche<br /></br>• Driven by early adrenal androgen secretion; hallmark is pubic/axillary hair, body odor, mild acne without breast development. Here the dominant finding is breast enlargement, not androgen effects.</p></br><p>D. Puberty<br /></br>• Normal puberty is gradual and synchronous: thelarche → pubarche → peak height → menarche over 2–4 y. A 3-month abrupt change without height spurt or menarche is inconsistent.</p></br><p>E. Use of estrogen-containing lotions<br /></br>• Possible but no such product is mentioned. Pill ingestion is the more direct, higher-dose exposure explicitly provided in the vignette.</p></br><p>Bottom Line</br>In a child with a sudden, isolated pubertal sign and ready household access to hormonal pills or creams, think exogenous hormone ingestion rather than true precocious puberty.</p>
32. A 4-year-old boy is brought to the emergency department by his parents because of a 2-day history of increasingly severe pain of his right leg with an associated limp. He has not fallen or sustained any trauma. One week ago, he was treated with amoxicillin for a middle ear infection. He has no history of serious illness and currently takes no medications. He is at the 50th percentile for height and weight. Vital signs are within normal limits. On examination, the patient holds the right hip flexed and externally rotated. Passive range of motion of the right hip is limited by pain. An x-ray of the right hip shows no abnormalities. His leukocyte count is 10,000/mm³, erythrocyte sedimentation rate is 15 mm/h, and serum C-reactive protein concentration is 1.5 mg/L (N=0.08–3.1). Which of the following is the most appropriate treatment for this patient?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Intravenous broad-spectrum antibiotic therapy</br>b. Nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drug therapy</br>c. Physical therapy for motion and strength</br>d. Serial hip joint aspirations</br>e. Surgical debridement of the hip joint</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drug (NSAID) therapy  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>Key clues that steer you away from septic arthritis and toward transient (toxic) synovitis:</p></br><p>• Age 4 years – peak age for transient synovitis (3-8 yr).<br /></br>• Acute onset limp after a recent viral/bacterial URI or otitis media (“one week ago he was treated with amoxicillin for a middle-ear infection”). This preceding illness is classic for transient synovitis.<br /></br>• Afebrile and “vital signs within normal limits.” Septic arthritis almost always produces fever.<br /></br>• Hip held in flexion and external rotation with painful passive ROM – seen in both entities, so you must look at labs to discriminate.<br /></br>• Normal laboratory markers of inflammation<br /></br>  – WBC 10 000/µL (Kocher cutoff &gt; 12 000)<br /></br>  – ESR 15 mm/h (Kocher cutoff &gt; 40)<br /></br>  – CRP 1.5 mg/L ≈ 0.15 mg/dL (Kocher cutoff &gt; 2 mg/dL ≈ 20 mg/L)<br /></br>  When only 0–1 Kocher criterion is present, probability of septic arthritis is &lt; 3 %.<br /></br>• Normal plain radiograph – early septic arthritis may still appear normal, but Legg-Calvé-Perthes disease or slipped capital femoral epiphysis would show characteristic changes.</p></br><p>Pertinent negatives: no fever, no trauma, no weight-loss, no systemic toxicity.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)</p></br><ol></br><li>Hip pain + limp in a child → generate differential (septic arthritis, transient synovitis, osteomyelitis of femur, Legg-Calvé-Perthes, SCFE).  </li></br><li>Apply Kocher criteria to rule in/out septic arthritis. Patient meets only 1 (non-weight-bearing).  </li></br><li>Normal inflammatory markers &amp; vitals → septic arthritis unlikely.  </li></br><li>Recent URI + age range → transient synovitis most probable.  </li></br><li>Management of transient synovitis is supportive: NSAIDs for pain/inflammation + limited weight bearing; symptoms usually resolve in ≤ 1 week. No antibiotics, aspirations, or surgery are needed.</li></br></ol></br><p>Take-away concept: Use Kocher criteria to differentiate septic arthritis (surgical emergency) from transient synovitis (benign, self-limited, NSAID-responsive).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review</p></br><p>• Transient (toxic) synovitis = sterile inflammation of the hip synovium, often post-viral or after a mild bacterial infection.<br /></br>• Etiology: Thought to be immune-mediated; synovial effusion causes pain and limited ROM.<br /></br>• Natural course: Peaks at 3-8 yr, resolves spontaneously within days-weeks.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests</p></br><p>First-line:<br /></br>• NSAID (e.g., ibuprofen 10 mg/kg/dose q6-8 h) + rest; allow weight bearing as tolerated.<br /></br>• Close outpatient follow-up in 24-48 h.  </p></br><p>Second-line (rare, if no improvement or diagnostic doubt):<br /></br>• Ultrasound-guided hip aspiration to look for purulent fluid and send culture/Gram stain.<br /></br>• MRI if concern for early osteomyelitis or Legg-Calvé-Perthes.</p></br><p>No role for:<br /></br>• Empiric IV antibiotics (unless septic arthritis suspected).<br /></br>• Routine serial aspirations or surgical drainage.<br /></br>• Physical therapy in the acute painful phase.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips</p></br><p>“Septic HIP” Kocher mnemonic (each letter = 1 point)<br /></br>H – High fever (&gt; 38.5 °C)<br /></br>I – Inability to bear weight<br /></br>P – Peripheral WBC &gt; 12 K<br /></br>S – Sed(ESR) &gt; 40 mm/h / CRP &gt; 2 mg/dL  </p></br><p>0-1 points → think “Transient” (Treat with NSAID). 3-4 points → think “Septic” (Tap &amp; Treat ASAP).</p></br><p>Visual: Picture a red “Stop” sign on four posts (the 4 Kocher criteria). If all posts are up, STOP and go to OR/IV abx. If only one post … the sign falls (benign).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis</p></br><p>a. Intravenous broad-spectrum antibiotic therapy<br /></br>   – Appropriate initial step only when septic arthritis is likely or proven (high fever, ↑↑ESR/CRP/WBC, positive joint aspirate). Starting IV abx here risks overtreatment and side effects.</p></br><p>c. Physical therapy for motion and strength<br /></br>   – Not indicated during the acute painful phase; child will self-limit motion. PT considered only after pain resolves if residual stiffness persists.</p></br><p>d. Serial hip joint aspirations<br /></br>   – Used when the diagnosis is unclear or to monitor a purulent effusion that has already been drained. An initial diagnostic tap could be justified if labs were borderline; serial taps are unnecessary in straightforward transient synovitis.</p></br><p>e. Surgical debridement of the hip joint<br /></br>   – Emergent management for septic arthritis once confirmed; completely unwarranted in a child with normal inflammatory markers and benign clinical course.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line:<br /></br>A well-appearing 4-year-old with a limp, normal inflammatory labs, and recent URI most likely has transient synovitis. Treat with NSAIDs and observe.</p>
33. A 9-month-old boy is brought to the office by his parents because they are concerned about swelling in his abdomen that has been present since birth and has increased during the past 2 weeks. The patient’s diet consists of cow milk–based formula, baby food, and some solid food. He has six wet diapers and one bowel movement daily. He has no history of serious illness and receives no medications. He is at the 75th percentile for length, weight, and head circumference. Vital signs are within normal limits. Abdominal examination shows a 2-cm protrusion from the umbilicus that is easily reducible; there is no erythema, induration, or tenderness to palpation. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. CT scan of the abdomen</br>b. Surgical repair of the mass</br>c. Switching to soy milk–based formula</br>d. Ultrasonography of the abdomen</br>e. Reassurance that the mass will resolve</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Reassurance that the mass will resolve</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “2-cm protrusion from the umbilicus … easily reducible” → classic wording for an uncomplicated umbilical hernia (herniation of bowel through an incompletely closed umbilical ring).<br /></br>• “Present since birth” and “no erythema, induration, or tenderness” → rules out acquired ventral hernia, infection, or incarceration/strangulation.<br /></br>• Normal growth parameters and intake → no sign of bowel obstruction or malabsorption.<br /></br>Key pattern: Small, soft, reducible umbilical bulge in an otherwise healthy infant = benign congenital umbilical hernia.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify lesion: reducible midline defect at the umbilicus = umbilical hernia.<br /></br>2. Determine complication status: none (no pain, erythema, vomiting, or tender mass).<br /></br>3. Management guideline: Most umbilical hernias close spontaneously by age 2-5 y; observe unless:<br /></br>   • Incarcerated/strangulated, OR<br /></br>   • &gt;2 cm persisting after age 2 y, OR<br /></br>   • Any hernia persisting after age 5 y.<br /></br>4. Patient is 9 mo → well below intervention threshold. Therefore, provide reassurance and routine follow-up.</p></br><p>Take-home: Uncomplicated umbilical hernias in infants are managed expectantly; surgery is deferred unless large, symptomatic, or persistent beyond early childhood.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)<br /></br>• Etiology: Failure of fascial umbilical ring to close after cord separation.<br /></br>• Risk factors: Prematurity, low birth weight, African ancestry, hypothyroidism, trisomy 21.<br /></br>• Natural history: 90% close by age 3; closure rate correlates inversely with defect size (&lt;1.5 cm almost always close).<br /></br>• Complications: Incarceration/strangulation (rare, &lt;2%).  </p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>First-line: Observation and parental reassurance.<br /></br>Surgery (elective herniorrhaphy) only if:<br /></br>• Defect &gt;2 cm after age 2,<br /></br>• Any size persisting beyond age 5-6,<br /></br>• Evidence of incarceration/strangulation.<br /></br>No imaging is required when physical exam is classic and uncomplicated.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“U-HERNIA”<br /></br>U – Umbilical bulge<br /></br>H – Herniates through ring<br /></br>E – Easily reducible<br /></br>R – Resolves on its own<br /></br>N – No routine imaging<br /></br>I – Incision (surgery) if &gt;2 cm after 2 y OR persists after 5 y<br /></br>A – Alert for incarceration (rare but urgent)</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. CT scan of the abdomen – Imaging exposes infant to radiation and is unnecessary; diagnosis is clinical.<br /></br>b.
