Surgical repair – Indicated only for large, persistent, or complicated hernias; not warranted at 9 mo with a 2-cm, reducible defect.<br /></br>c. Switching to soy milk–based formula – Used for cow’s-milk-protein allergy (bloody stools, eczema); unrelated to a structural hernia.<br /></br>d. Ultrasonography – Although harmless, it adds no information because the hernia is palpable and reducible; guidelines do not recommend routine imaging.  </p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>A small, reducible umbilical hernia in an infant &lt;2 y is expected to close spontaneously—provide reassurance and routine pediatric follow-up.</p>
34. A pediatric clinic implements a screening test for determining alcohol use in adolescent patients. The screening test has an 85% sensitivity and 95% specificity. A 15-year-old boy has a positive result prior to being examined by the physician. The prevalence of alcohol use in the adolescent patients in this clinic is 10%. Which of the following best explains how the positive result on the screening test affects the probability that this patient consumes alcohol?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Decreases minimally</br>b. Decreases substantially</br>c. Increases minimally</br>d. Increases substantially</br>e. Neither increases nor decreases</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Increases substantially  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key data: adolescent (age does not modify calculations), positive screen, test characteristics (sensitivity 85 %, specificity 95 %), clinic prevalence (pre-test probability) 10 %.<br /></br>• Task: decide how a positive result changes the probability that THIS patient drinks alcohol. This is asking for the post-test (posterior) probability = Positive Predictive Value (PPV) or a Bayes calculation.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Convert test characteristics into a likelihood ratio (LR+).<br /></br>   LR+ = Sensitivity ÷ (1 − Specificity) = 0.85 ÷ 0.05 = 17.<br /></br>2. Convert pre-test probability (10 %) to odds.<br /></br>   Pre-test odds = 0.10 ÷ 0.90 = 1 : 9 ≈ 0.11.<br /></br>3. Apply LR+: Post-test odds = 0.11 × 17 ≈ 1.87.<br /></br>4. Convert back to probability: 1.87 ÷ (1 + 1.87) ≈ 0.65 (65 %).  </p></br><p>Probability jumps from 10 % to ~65 % → a large (roughly six-fold) increase = “increases substantially.”</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (brief, because this is a biostats question)<br /></br>No specific disease mechanism—the core concept is Bayesian reasoning in diagnostic testing.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Not applicable; focus is on interpreting screening results.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>• “95 % specificity → 5 % false-positive rate; if LR+ &gt;10, think ‘big jump’ in probability.”<br /></br>• Quick PPV estimate trick for exams: create a 2 × 2 table with 1000 patients—easy mental math anchor.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Decreases minimally / b. Decreases substantially: A positive result cannot decrease probability. Eliminate immediately.<br /></br>c. Increases minimally: Would be correct if LR+ ≈ 1–3 or post-test probability rose only slightly (e.g., 10 % → 15 %). Here LR+ = 17 and probability rises to ~65 %.<br /></br>e. Neither increases nor decreases: Would be true if test offered no diagnostic information (sensitivity ≈ specificity ≈ 50 %). Not the case here.</p></br><p>Take-Home Message<br /></br>With high specificity (95 %) and good sensitivity (85 %), a positive screen in a low-prevalence (10 %) population can still “move the needle” dramatically—PPV ≈ 65 %, a substantial increase in the likelihood that the adolescent actually consumes alcohol.</p>
35. A 27-year-old woman comes to the emergency department because of “boils” on her groin and in her armpits. She has had one previous episode during pregnancy 2 years ago requiring incision and drainage. Examination shows an inflamed, tender, foul-smelling nodule in the left axilla with seropurulent drainage and erythematous papules in the inguinal-femoral region. A 2-week course of broad-spectrum antibiotics is started, and the axillary abscess is drained. Which of the following is the most likely natural course of this disease?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Complete resolution</br>b. Temporary improvement with likelihood of recurrence</br>c. Development of extramammary Paget disease</br>d. Development of inflammatory bowel disease</br>e. Development of Merkel cell carcinoma</br>f. Development of progressive necrotizing infection</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Temporary improvement with likelihood of recurrence  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key phrases: “boils,” intertriginous sites (groin, axilla), foul-smelling drainage, previous identical episode, requiring I&amp;D.<br /></br>• These are classic buzzwords for hidradenitis suppurativa (HS, aka acne inversa).<br /></br>• Pertinent positives: young female, obese-style distribution, recurrent abscesses with sinus-tract–like drainage.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: no rapidly spreading pain, crepitus, systemic toxicity → rules out necrotizing fasciitis.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Identify condition: recurrent deep, tender nodules in apocrine-bearing areas = HS.<br /></br>2. HS is a chronic, relapsing inflammatory disease of the folliculopilosebaceous unit.<br /></br>3. After I&amp;D or antibiotics, lesions improve but typically recur in the same or nearby areas → chronic course.<br /></br>Take-home: HS = “comes back again and again.”  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: follicular occlusion → rupture → neutrophilic inflammation → sinus tracts &amp; scarring.<br /></br>• Risk factors: obesity, smoking, diabetes, family history (autosomal dominant component).<br /></br>• Hurley staging:<br /></br>  – I: recurrent abscesses, no sinus tracts/scars<br /></br>  – II: sinus tracts, scarring, widely spaced lesions<br /></br>  – III: diffuse involvement with interconnected tracts<br /></br>• Natural history: waxing/waning with progressive scarring; &gt;50 % relapse within months after any single intervention.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics (high-yield outline)<br /></br>1. Acute inflamed nodule: intralesional triamcinolone, short oral antibiotics (tetracycline, clindamycin + rifampin).<br /></br>2. Chronic/refractory: oral retinoids (acitretin), hormonal therapy (OCP, spironolactone), biologics (adalimumab = only FDA-approved).<br /></br>3. Definitive for Hurley II–III: wide local surgical excision or CO₂ laser removal.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“HS Keeps Coming Home”:<br /></br>H – Hurley staging<br /></br>S – Smoking / Staph superinfection risk<br /></br>K – Keeps returning (chronic)<br /></br>C – CO₂ laser / complete excision for cure<br /></br>H – Hormonal &amp; Humira (adalimumab) for severe disease  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Complete resolution – Wrong. HS rarely resolves completely; &gt;90 % experience recurrences.<br /></br>c. Extramammary Paget – Associated with apocrine glands but not a sequela of HS.<br /></br>d. Inflammatory bowel disease – HS may coexist with Crohn disease, but most patients do NOT go on to develop IBD; not the typical “natural course.”<br /></br>e. Merkel cell carcinoma – Neuroendocrine skin tumor unrelated to HS.<br /></br>f. Progressive necrotizing infection – HS abscesses can secondarily infect but do not predictably progress to necrotizing fasciitis; that would present with systemic toxicity and severe pain out of proportion, absent here.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Hidradenitis suppurativa is chronic and relapsing—expect temporary improvement after treatment with high likelihood of recurrence.</p>
36. Patient Information Age: 62 years Gender: F, self-identified Ethnicity: unspecified Site of Care: office History Reason for Visit/Chief Concern: I’m having chest pain, and my heart is racing; I can’t seem to catch my breath. History of Present Illness: 3-day history of worsening chest pain, palpitations, and shortness of breath reports nonproductive cough and feeling warm, attributed to an upper respiratory tract infection 5 to 7 days ago symptoms exacerbated by exertion and lying flat symptoms alleviated by rest and sitting up has not had similar symptoms previously Past Medical History: 20-year history of hypertension 2-year history of osteoporosis mild anxiety Medications: amlodipine calcium supplement and vitamin D supplementation prn: alprazolam for sleep and anxiety prn: ibuprofen for occasional back pain prn: guaifenesin for recent viral respiratory infection</br>Allergies: no known drug allergies Psychosocial History: does not smoke cigarettes drinks two to four alcoholic beverages weekly does not use illicit drugs works part-time as a teller at a bank sexually active and monogamous with her husband no recent travel Physical Examination Temp 36.8 °C (98.5 °F), pulse 115/min, resp 18/min, BP 110/82 mm Hg, O₂ Sat 94% on RA, Ht 152 cm (5 ft 4 in), Wt 75 kg, or 165lb, BMI 28 kg/m² Appearance: awake, alert, interactive HEENT: pupils equal, round, reactive to light; conjunctivae anicteric; mucous membranes moist Pulmonary: bilateral basilar crackles approximately one-quarter up the posterior lung fields; no rhonchi or wheezes Cardiac: tachycardia with regular rhythm, S₁ and S₂ without murmurs, rubs, or gallops; jugular venous pressure 7 cm H₂O above sternal angle Abdominal: no abnormalities Extremities: no abnormalities Neurologic: no abnormalities Diagnostic Studies ECG and parasternal (left) and four-chamber (right) echocardiography views are shown</br>Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. ardiac tamponade</br>b. Dilated cardiomyopathy</br>c. Myocardial sarcoidosis</br>d. Pericardial effusion</br>e. Postmyocardial infarction (Dressler) syndrome</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Cardiac tamponade  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Recent “viral upper-respiratory infection” → classic antecedent of acute pericarditis/effusion.<br /></br>• Pleuritic chest pain that worsens on lying flat and improves on sitting up → pericardial source.<br /></br>• Tachycardia + exertional dyspnea + can’t “catch her breath”.<br /></br>• Elevated JVP (7 cm above sternal angle; normal ≤3–4 cm) and basilar crackles (pulmonary venous congestion) → impaired cardiac filling.<br /></br>• ECG (not shown to you) on USMLE-type questions with tamponade typically shows sinus tachycardia, low-voltage QRS complexes ± electrical alternans.<br /></br>• Echo (parasternal + 4-chamber views) almost certainly demonstrates a rim of anechoic fluid around the heart with diastolic collapse of the right-sided chambers—pathognomonic for tamponade.  </p></br><p>These hemodynamic‐compromise clues convert a “simple” pericardial effusion into cardiac tamponade.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify pericardial pain pattern (pleuritic, positional).<br /></br>2. Look for evidence of pericardial fluid → recent viral prodrome, likely effusion on echo.<br /></br>3. Decide whether the effusion is causing hemodynamic instability. Clues:<br /></br>   • Sinus tachycardia (compensation).<br /></br>   • Elevated JVP.<br /></br>   • Pulmonary congestion / dyspnea.<br /></br>   • Echo: chamber collapse.<br /></br>   → Therefore, the correct entity is cardiac tamponade, not “pericardial effusion” alone.  </p></br><p>Take-home: Pericardial effusion + hemodynamic compromise = cardiac tamponade.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Viral pericarditis can lead to rapid accumulation of serous fluid in the pericardial sac.<br /></br>• When intrapericardial pressure rises enough to equal or exceed right-sided filling pressure, diastolic collapse of the right atrium/ventricle occurs → ↓venous return → ↓stroke volume → compensatory tachycardia and signs of congestion (JVD, crackles).<br /></br>• Beck triad: Hypotension, JVD, muffled heart sounds (not all must be present early).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnosis &amp; Management (FYI)<br /></br>Diagnostic tests<br /></br>1. ECG: low voltage, electrical alternans.<br /></br>2. CXR: “water-bottle” heart if effusion large.<br /></br>3. Echo: pericardial fluid + right-sided chamber collapse, IVC plethora.  </p></br><p>Immediate treatment<br /></br>• Urgent pericardiocentesis (needle or catheter).<br /></br>• Volume resuscitation while awaiting drainage if hypotensive.<br /></br>• Treat underlying cause (e.g., NSAIDs/colchicine for viral pericarditis once stable).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────<br /></br>Memory Tips<br /></br>• “Tampon-AID”: Acute Infection → effusion → Diastolic collapse.<br /></br>• Think “TAMPON”: Tachycardia, Absorbed (muffled) heart sounds, Maximum JVP, Pulsus paradoxus, Oh-no hypotension, Needed pericardiocentesis.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Dilated cardiomyopathy<br /></br>   • Presents with chronic HF, S3 gallop, cardiomegaly on echo, not positional pleuritic pain; no rapid change on echo.  </p></br><p>c. Myocardial sarcoidosis<br /></br>   • Younger or middle-aged, systemic sarcoid signs (uveitis, skin lesions, lymphadenopathy); conduction blocks/ventricular arrhythmias rather than positional chest pain.  </p></br><p>d. Pericardial effusion (without tamponade)<br /></br>   • Would cause similar ECG/echo findings but NO hemodynamic compromise: normal JVP, no pulmonary congestion, patient usually stable. Presence of tachycardia, elevated JVP, crackles, and chamber collapse makes tamponade the better choice.  </p></br><p>e. Post–myocardial infarction (Dressler) syndrome<br /></br>   • Autoimmune pericarditis 2–4 weeks after an MI. This patient had no recent MI and her symptoms began only days after a viral URI.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────<br /></br>Key USMLE Pearl<br /></br>Any patient with recent viral illness who now has positional pleuritic chest pain AND objective evidence of impaired filling (tachycardia, JVD, echo collapse) is a board-classic cardiac tamponade—drain first, ask questions later.</p>
37. A study is conducted to evaluate the effectiveness of testing stool for occult blood. One thousand men between the ages of 45 and 60 years who come to the physician for a routine health maintenance examination are enrolled in the study. Results show that 70 of 100 patients with colon cancer have a positive test of the stool for occult blood, and 720 of 900 patients without colon cancer have a negative test of the stool for occult blood. Which of the following is the most accurate conclusion regarding this study?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. The negative predictive value is 80%</br>b. The positive predictive value is 80%</br>c. The prevalence of colon cancer is 10%</br>d. The sensitivity is 80%</br>e. The specificity is 70%</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. The prevalence of colon cancer is 10%</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Total sample size = 1,000 men (denominator for prevalence).<br /></br>• “70 of 100 patients with colon cancer have a positive test” → True-positives (TP) = 70; False-negatives (FN) = 30. Total diseased = 100.<br /></br>• “720 of 900 patients without colon cancer have a negative test” → True-negatives (TN) = 720; False-positives (FP) = 180. Total non-diseased = 900.</p></br><p>Key pattern: They give you the full 2×2 table numbers; quickly filling that table allows rapid calculation of all test characteristics.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Determine disease totals (100 diseased, 900 non-diseased).<br /></br>2. Prevalence = diseased ÷ total population = 100 ÷ 1,000 = 0.10 (10%).<br /></br>3. Cross-check other metrics to ensure you’re not mis-reading the question.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Prevalence is purely a property of the population studied and is independent of the test’s performance.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (Brief)<br /></br>• Occult blood testing detects heme peroxidase activity; positive results can suggest colorectal carcinoma, polyps, or benign bleeding sources.<br /></br>• Screening utility depends heavily on prevalence and predictive values in the target population (age 45–60 = moderate risk group).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Test Characteristics (Calculated for reference)<br /></br>• Sensitivity = TP / (TP + FN) = 70 / 100 = 70%<br /></br>• Specificity = TN / (TN + FP) = 720 / 900 = 80%<br /></br>• Positive Predictive Value (PPV) = TP / (TP + FP) = 70 / 250 ≈ 28%<br /></br>• Negative Predictive Value (NPV) = TN / (TN + FN) = 720 / 750 = 96%</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“SnNout &amp; SpPin”: High Sensitivity (Sn) → Negative rules OUT; High Specificity (Sp) → Positive rules IN.<br /></br>Prevalence = “People with the Problem ÷ Population” (P for prevalence, P for problem, P for population).  </p></br><p>Quick 2×2 Snapshot mnemonic:<br /></br>   TPFN / FP TN<br /></br>   “Top row True, down False.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. NPV is 80% – Wrong. NPV = 96% (720 ÷ 750).<br /></br>b. PPV is 80% – Wrong. PPV = 28% (70 ÷ 250).<br /></br>d. Sensitivity is 80% – Wrong. Sensitivity = 70% (70 ÷ 100).<br /></br>e. Specificity is 70% – Wrong. Specificity = 80% (720 ÷ 900).</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Swapping sensitivity with specificity and misunderstanding that PPV/NPV depend on prevalence, whereas sensitivity/specificity do not.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Only one statement is numerically correct given the provided data: 100 out of 1,000 men have colon cancer → prevalence 10%.</p>
38. A medical director of a multisite internal medicine practice wants to maximize delivery of preventive services to patients. The practice has four sites, each employing one lead physician, one office manager, and 50 internists. Which of the following strategies is most likely to be effective?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Determination of an action plan by each site’s lead physician</br>b. Determination of an action plan by one multidisciplinary team representing all the sites</br>c. Educational program at a central location regarding the importance of preventive services</br>d. Educational program at each site regarding the importance of preventive services</br>e. Mailing of preventive-service educational materials to all patients</br>f. Random chart auditing by each office manager with results provided to the respective lead physicians</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Determination of an action plan by one multidisciplinary team representing all the sites  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Multisite internal-medicine practice” → Four geographically distinct clinics that nevertheless function under a single organizational umbrella.<br /></br>• “Wants to maximize delivery of preventive services” → A quality-improvement (QI) goal that requires standardization, measurement, and sustained engagement by multiple disciplines (physicians, nurses, office staff, managers, IT).<br /></br>• Key word: “strategy most likely to be effective.” On NBME/Step 2 questions, the most effective QI intervention is almost always the one that 1) standardizes care across the system, 2) involves frontline stakeholders from every discipline, and 3) creates ownership at every site.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. A practice-wide problem (suboptimal preventive-care delivery) is identified.<br /></br>2. Sustainable improvement requires (a) evidence-based consensus, (b) uniform workflows, (c) buy-in from everyone who touches the process (clinicians, nursing, scheduling, IT, management).<br /></br>3. The structure that best ensures points 1–2 is a multidisciplinary team with representatives from each site.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Multidisciplinary, system-wide collaboration is the cornerstone of successful quality-improvement initiatives.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (Why this matters clinically)<br /></br>Preventive services (immunizations, cancer screening, cardiovascular risk reduction) are high-value care. Missed opportunities usually arise from:<br /></br>• Process failures (no reminder system, unclear responsibility).<br /></br>• Inconsistent workflows between sites.<br /></br>• Lack of feedback loops.<br /></br>A multidisciplinary team can address all three by creating standardized order sets, electronic reminders, standing nursing protocols, and audit-and-feedback dashboards.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>QI Tools / Diagnostics &amp; Implementation<br /></br>• Assemble team → representatives from physicians, nursing, front-desk, EMR/IT, and administration at each site.<br /></br>• Perform gap analysis → chart review to quantify baseline preventive-service rates.<br /></br>• Plan-Do-Study-Act (PDSA) cycles → pilot at one clinic, refine, then spread.<br /></br>• Metrics → % patients up-to-date with immunizations, cancer screening, lipid testing, etc.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Best QI intervention = TEAM”<br /></br>T = Team-based (multidisciplinary)<br /></br>E = Every site represented<br /></br>A = Agreement on evidence-based process<br /></br>M = Measure, modify, maintain (PDSA)  </p></br><p>If you see “multisite” + “system-wide problem,” think “multidisciplinary team representing all sites.”  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Determination of an action plan by each site’s lead physician<br /></br>• Pitfall: promotes siloed approaches → inconsistent protocols, duplication of effort, unequal results.  </p></br><p>c. Educational program at a central location regarding the importance of preventive services<br /></br>d. Educational program at each site regarding the importance of preventive services<br /></br>• Education alone rarely changes behavior (knowledge ≠ performance). Needs system redesign, reminders, and accountability.  </p></br><p>e. Mailing of preventive-service educational materials to all patients<br /></br>• Passive patient education yields minimal uptake and ignores system barriers (eg, scheduling, insurance authorization).  </p></br><p>f. Random chart auditing by each office manager with results provided to lead physicians<br /></br>• Audit-and-feedback is useful, but without a standardized action plan and multidisciplinary engagement, data alone rarely drives sustained change. Also suffers from silo effect and variability in methods between sites.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Key Takeaway<br /></br>For large, multisite practices, the single most effective way to improve delivery of preventive services (or any process of care) is to empower a multidisciplinary team with representatives from every location to design, implement, and monitor a standardized, evidence-based action plan.</p>
39. A previously healthy 22-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 1-week history of a severe, frequent, nonproductive cough. His temperature is 37.7 °C (99.8 °F), pulse is 104/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 118/72 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 95%. Mild diffuse crackles are heard. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. A chest x-ray shows diffuse bilateral interstitial infiltrates. Which of the following is the most likely causal organism?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Legionella pneumophila</br>b. Mycobacterium tuberculosis</br>c. Mycoplasma pneumoniae</br>d. Staphylococcus aureus</br>e. Streptococcus pneumoniae</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer: c. Mycoplasma pneumoniae</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age and health status – previously healthy 22-year-old → classic demographic for “walking” (atypical) pneumonia, most commonly due to Mycoplasma pneumoniae.<br /></br>• Symptom profile – 1-week history, severe/frequent NON-productive cough, only low-grade fever (99.8 °F). Atypical organisms classically cause a dry cough and milder constitutional symptoms compared with typical, lobar pneumonias.<br /></br>• Physical exam – mild tachycardia, minimal hypoxemia (SpO₂ 95 %), diffuse fine crackles but otherwise benign exam. Again fits the subtle presentation of Mycoplasma.<br /></br>• CXR – diffuse bilateral interstitial (reticulonodular) infiltrates without focal lobar consolidation → textbook CXR for atypical pneumonia.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Young adult with mild systemic findings + dry cough → think “atypical” pneumonia.<br /></br>2. Among atypicals (Mycoplasma, Chlamydophila, Legionella, viruses), Mycoplasma is by far most common in healthy outpatients &lt;40 y.<br /></br>3. Absence of GI symptoms, confusion, or hyponatremia argues against Legionella.<br /></br>4. Lack of focal lobar infiltrate and sputum production argues against typical bacteria (Strep pneumo, Staph aureus).<br /></br>Take-home: In young adults, a nagging dry cough + diffuse interstitial CXR = Mycoplasma until proven otherwise.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Mycoplasma lacks a peptidoglycan cell wall → not visible on Gram stain; β-lactams are ineffective.<br /></br>• Adheres to respiratory epithelium via P1 adhesin → ciliostasis → persistent cough.<br /></br>• Generates cold-agglutinin (IgM) antibodies that can cause hemolytic anemia.<br /></br>• Outbreaks common in dorms, barracks; spread via respiratory droplets.</p></br><p>Diagnostics<br /></br>• CXR often looks “worse than the patient” (diffuse infiltrates despite mild exam).<br /></br>• Labs: cold-agglutinin hemolytic anemia, ↑ESR; definitive diagnosis by PCR or serology, but usually treated empirically.  </p></br><p>Treatment<br /></br>First-line: Macrolide (azithromycin or clarithromycin) OR doxycycline.<br /></br>Alternatives: Respiratory fluoroquinolone (levofloxacin, moxifloxacin).<br /></br>β-lactams (e.g., ceftriaxone, amoxicillin) are ineffective.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Walking pneumonia” = patient walking around despite ugly CXR.<br /></br>M y C O-P-LAS-MA<br /></br>• M = Military (barracks)<br /></br>• C = Cold agglutinins<br /></br>• O = Outbreaks in cOllege dorms<br /></br>• P = Patchy interstitial infiltrates<br /></br>• LAS = Lacks cell wALL, Azithromycin Sensitive  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Legionella pneumophila – Typically older smokers or immunocompromised; high fever, relative bradycardia, GI symptoms (diarrhea), hyponatremia; CXR may show lobar or multifocal infiltrates; diagnosed with urine Ag. None of these features are present.<br /></br>b. Mycobacterium tuberculosis – Subacute/chronic illness (weeks–months), night sweats, weight loss; CXR shows cavitary upper-lobe lesions or miliary pattern, not acute interstitial infiltrates.<br /></br>d. Staphylococcus aureus – Post-influenza pneumonia or in IV-drug users; causes rapidly progressive necrotizing lobar cavitation with purulent sputum, high fevers, severe illness.<br /></br>e. Streptococcus pneumoniae – Most common typical CAP; sudden onset high fever, productive “rusty” sputum, lobar consolidation on CXR, pleuritic chest pain—none seen here.</p></br><p>Core Concept<br /></br>Young, otherwise healthy patients with subacute dry cough and diffuse interstitial infiltrates almost always point to Mycoplasma pneumoniae, treated with macrolides or doxycycline, not β-lactams.</p>
40. A 2-year-old boy is brought to the physician by his mother because of a 1-week history of rectal tissue protruding from his anus when he strains vigorously during bowel movements. The tissue retracts spontaneously within 1 hour. His mother notes that his stools are hard. He has no history of serious illness and receives no medications. Immunizations are up-to-date. Growth and development are appropriate for age. The patient’s paternal uncle has a history of intermittent abdominal pain and diarrhea; he recently began a gluten-free diet on the advice of a friend. The patient’s family dog recently was treated for worms. Examination of the patient shows no abnormalities; the anus appears normal.</br>Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient’s symptom?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Celiac disease</br>b. Cystic fibrosis</br>c. Functional constipation</br>d. Pinworms</br>e. Rectal polyps</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Functional constipation  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key finding: “rectal tissue protruding from his anus when he strains vigorously … retracts spontaneously within 1 hour.” → classic description of transient rectal prolapse.<br /></br>• Trigger: “His stools are hard.” → indicates excessive straining from constipation.<br /></br>• Age: 2 years old, otherwise healthy and growing normally → points toward benign functional etiology rather than systemic disease.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: no chronic diarrhea, no weight loss, no foul-smelling or greasy stools, no respiratory issues, no perianal itching, normal anal exam → these argue against celiac disease, cystic fibrosis (CF), pinworm infection, and polyps.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Identify the event → rectal prolapse.<br /></br>2. Ask “Why do toddlers prolapse?”<br /></br>   • MCC (most common cause) = increased intra-abdominal pressure from constipation/straining.<br /></br>   • Other causes (CF, celiac, chronic diarrhea) usually present with malabsorption, poor weight gain, or pulmonary disease.<br /></br>3. Screen for red flags → none present (normal growth, hard—not loose—stools).<br /></br>4. Therefore, functional constipation is the simplest, highest-probability explanation.<br /></br>Core takeaway: In a healthy toddler with hard stools and transient prolapse, functional constipation is far and away the typical etiology.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Functional constipation: withholding or delayed stool passage → harder stools → need to strain → ↑ intra-abdominal &amp; intra-rectal pressure → mucosal or full-thickness rectal wall protrudes through anus.<br /></br>• Pediatric rectal prolapse is often mucosal only and self-reduces; chronic episodes risk edema and full-thickness involvement.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>1. Disimpaction if needed (polyethylene glycol or glycerin suppository).<br /></br>2. Maintenance therapy: high-fiber diet, adequate fluids, polyethylene glycol (Miralax), scheduled toilet sitting.<br /></br>3. Education: discourage stool withholding, positive reinforcement.<br /></br>4. Evaluate further ONLY if prolapse persists after constipation is controlled or if red-flag features (poor weight gain, foul diarrhea, recurrent respiratory infections) emerge—then test for CF (sweat chloride) or celiac (tTG-IgA).  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“PROLAPSE” mnemonic for kids:<br /></br>• P = PainLESS protrusion<br /></br>• R = Retracts on its own<br /></br>• O = Occurs with hard stools<br /></br>• L = Look for Lack of diarrhea<br /></br>• A = Age 1–3 yrs common<br /></br>• P = Pinworms itch, not prolapse<br /></br>• S = Steatorrhea/Small stature → think CF/Celiac (not present)<br /></br>• E = Evaluate constipation first  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Celiac disease: Would give chronic bulky, foul, or watery stools, distention, irritability, iron-deficiency anemia, and poor growth. Hard stools and normal growth make this unlikely.<br /></br>b. Cystic fibrosis: Rectal prolapse can be a presenting sign, but CF kids typically have recurrent respiratory infections, greasy stools, or meconium ileus history. None are present here.<br /></br>d. Pinworms (Enterobius): Cause nocturnal perianal pruritus, not prolapse; stools are otherwise normal.<br /></br>e. Rectal polyps: Juvenile polyps present with painless rectal bleeding or a “grape-like” mass that prolapses and may require manual reduction; bleeding—not constipation—is the hallmark.  </p></br><p>Bottom line: In a healthy toddler whose prolapse occurs only with straining at hard stools, functional constipation is by far the most common and most likely cause.</p>
41. A 32-year-old man comes to the physician for a follow-up examination. He was admitted to the hospital 2 months ago for treatment of Pneumocystis jirovecii pneumonia and received the diagnosis of HIV infection at that time. After discharge from the hospital, he completed his antibiotic course but missed scheduled follow-up appointments. He currently takes no medications. He says he feels well. Examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Referral to another physician due to noncompliance with medical therapy</br>b. Acyclovir therapy</br>c. Trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole prophylaxis</br>d. Varicella vaccine</br>e. Zidovudine (AZT) therapy</br></br></br><p>I want to be sure I give you the most accurate, high-yield explanation possible.  </p></br><p>For a newly-diagnosed HIV-positive patient who just completed treatment for Pneumocystis jirovecii pneumonia, current guidelines recommend (1) immediate initiation of antiretroviral therapy and (2) trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole prophylaxis until the CD4 count is ≥ 200 cells/µL for ≥ 3 months. “Referral to another physician for non-compliance” would generally be considered inappropriate and unethical unless there are extenuating circumstances such as dangerous or threatening behavior toward clinic staff.</p></br><p>Because the keyed answer you supplied (“Referral to another physician due to noncompliance with medical therapy”) conflicts with standard HIV management and NBME/Step 2 CK expectations, could you please double-check that this is indeed the intended correct answer?  </p></br><p>If the correct answer is actually “Trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole prophylaxis,” everything else in the question stem would align well with current best practice, and I can proceed with a high-yield explanation based on that.</p>
42. A 37-year-old woman comes to the physician for evaluation of malnutrition secondary to systemic sclerosis (scleroderma). Six months ago, she had an elective abortion because of her poor medical condition and has had a 9-kg (20-lb weight loss since that time. She says she has difficulty falling asleep; she awakens daily at 4 AM and cannot go back to sleep. She is 173 cm (5 ft 8 in) tall and weighs 48 kg, or 105lb; BMI is 16 kg/m². Physical examination shows no other abnormalities. On mental status examination, she is sad and tearful and reports feeling hopeless. Her serum albumin concentration is 2.2 g/dL. In addition to aggressive nutritional support, which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Buspirone</br>b. Dronabinol</br>c. Megestrol</br>d. Olanzapine</br>e. Sertraline</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Sertraline (selective serotonin-reuptake inhibitor, SSRI)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – “Difficulty falling asleep … awakens daily at 4 AM and cannot go back to sleep” → classic early-morning awakening (terminal insomnia) of major depressive disorder (MDD).<br /></br>  – “Sad and tearful … feeling hopeless” → low mood + hopelessness &gt; 2 weeks.<br /></br>  – Marked weight loss, but systemic sclerosis alone does not explain insomnia, hopelessness, anhedonia.<br /></br>  – No psychosis, mania, or substance use reported.  </p></br><p>• Pertinent positives → depressed mood, neurovegetative changes (insomnia, weight change), hopelessness.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives → no panic attacks (rules out primary anxiety disorder), no delusions/hallucinations (rules out psychotic disorder), no mania/hypomania (rules out bipolar).<br /></br>Pattern = unipolar major depression complicating a chronic medical illness and malnutrition.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Establish diagnosis: meets ≥ 5/9 SIGECAPS criteria (Sleep disturbance, Interest ↓, Guilt/hopelessness, Energy ↓, Concentration ↓, Appetite/weight ↓, Psychomotor ±, Suicidal ideation) for ≥ 2 weeks.<br /></br>2. First-line treatment for non-psychotic MDD = SSRI or SNRI +/- psychotherapy.<br /></br>3. No contraindication to SSRI (no MAOI use, no bipolar). Malnutrition does not preclude SSRI; start low dose and titrate.<br /></br>4. Other listed drugs treat anxiety or cachexia but do not address core depressive pathology.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In medically ill patients, always treat comorbid major depression with a first-line antidepressant (SSRI) unless contraindicated.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Chronic disease (systemic sclerosis), recent pregnancy termination, and severe weight loss are major psychosocial stressors → ↑risk of MDD.<br /></br>• Neurobiology: ↓central serotonergic transmission, dysregulated HPA axis → sleep disturbance (↓slow-wave sleep, early REM onset), appetite changes, hopelessness.<br /></br>• Treat by restoring serotonin via reuptake inhibition (sertraline) to improve mood, sleep, appetite.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line drugs for MDD<br /></br>• SSRIs: sertraline, fluoxetine, escitalopram, paroxetine, citalopram.<br /></br>• Start sertraline 25–50 mg daily; monitor for GI upset, sexual dysfunction, hyponatremia (especially in malnourished/low albumin patients).<br /></br>• Benefit appears in 4–6 weeks; continue ≥ 6 months after remission.</p></br><p>When might the other choices be used?<br /></br>• Megestrol or dronabinol: cancer/HIV cachexia when weight gain is primary goal.<br /></br>• Olanzapine: psychotic depression or bipolar disorder; risk of metabolic syndrome.<br /></br>• Buspirone: generalized anxiety disorder; no antidepressant efficacy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>High-Yield Memory Aids<br /></br>“SIG-E-CAPS” = criteria for major depression (Sleep, Interest, Guilt, Energy, Concentration, Appetite, Psychomotor, Suicidal).<br /></br>Think “S = SSRI” → First S in SIG-E-CAPS reminds you that SSRIs (e.g., Sertraline) are first-line.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Rundown<br /></br>a. Buspirone – 5-HT1A partial agonist used for chronic generalized anxiety. Does not treat depressive core symptoms; no sleep or appetite benefit.<br /></br>b. Dronabinol – Synthetic THC; appetite stimulant for AIDS- or chemo-related anorexia. No antidepressant efficacy; risks tachycardia, dysphoria.<br /></br>c. Megestrol – Appetite stimulant/progestin for cancer or AIDS cachexia; can cause thromboembolism, adrenal suppression; does not address mood.<br /></br>d. Olanzapine – Atypical antipsychotic; may cause weight gain, but indicated for schizophrenia, bipolar, or depression with psychotic features in combination with fluoxetine; metabolic adverse effects outweigh benefits here.<br /></br>e. Sertraline – Correct: first-line SSRI for major depression, improves sleep, mood, and appetite with favorable safety profile.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>The patient meets criteria for major depressive disorder superimposed on systemic sclerosis–related malnutrition. First-line pharmacotherapy is an SSRI such as sertraline, making choice (e) the most appropriate drug.</p>
43. A 72-year-old man comes to the office for a routine examination. He underwent carotid endarterectomy and five-vessel coronary artery bypass grafting 1 and 5 years ago, respectively. He has hypertension, hyperlipidemia, chronic kidney disease, and gastroesophageal reflux disease. Medications are clopidogrel, hydrochlorothiazide, metoprolol, atorvastatin, famotidine, and aspirin. He has smoked two packs of cigarettes daily for 50 years. Temperature is 37.0 °C (98.6 °F), pulse is 62/min, respirations are 12/min, and blood pressure is 184/96 mm Hg. Examination shows no other abnormalities. Results of laboratory studies are shown:</br>Hemoglobin  13.6 g/dL</br>Hematocrit 44%</br>Leukocyte count   8500/mm³</br>Serum</br>Na⁺   134 mEq/L</br>K⁺   4.1 mEq/L</br>Cl⁻   98 mEq/L</br>HCO₃⁻   24 mEq/L</br>Urea nitrogen  22 mg/dL</br>Creatinine 1.5 mg/dL</br>Lisinopril is added to the medication regimen. Two weeks later, the patient returns to the physician for a follow-up visit. Pulse is 64/min, and blood pressure is 136/84 mm Hg. Examination is otherwise unchanged. Results of laboratory studies are shown:</br>Serum</br>Urea nitrogen  46 mg/dL</br>Creatinine 3.6 mg/dL</br>Urine</br>Blood   none</br>Proteinnone</br>Hyaline casts  0–1/hpf</br>Which of the following is the most likely cause of the change in renal function in this patient?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Acute interstitial nephritis</br>b. Acute tubular necrosis</br>c. Bilateral renal artery stenosis</br>d. Renal vein thrombosis</br>e. Unilateral renal artery stenosis</br></br></br><p>⚠️  I believe there is a mismatch between the answer key you provided (“Acute interstitial nephritis”) and the information in the vignette.<br /></br>When the clinical data are analyzed step-by-step, the findings are classic for bilateral renal artery stenosis precipitating an ACE-inhibitor–induced drop in GFR, not for acute interstitial nephritis. I have laid out the full reasoning below so you can see how the conclusion is reached.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Bilateral renal artery stenosis (RAS)</p></br><hr /></br><p>Vignette Analysis – Key Clues<br /></br>1. Very large jump in creatinine after starting an ACE inhibitor<br /></br>   • Baseline Cr 1.5 → 3.6 mg/dL (≈140% rise) within 2 weeks.<br /></br>2. Benign urinalysis<br /></br>   • No blood, no protein, 0–1 hyaline casts ➔ argues against intrinsic renal damage.<br /></br>3. Highly atherosclerotic patient<br /></br>   • 72 y/o male, 100-pack-year smoker, CAD → 5-vessel CABG, carotid endarterectomy, long-standing HTN, HLD.<br /></br>4. Blood pressure falls substantially after ACE I<br /></br>   • 184/96 → 136/84: confirms systemic vasodilatory effect and ↓glomerular perfusion pressure.</p></br><p>Pattern to recognize: “Large creatinine rise (&gt;30%) soon after ACE I/ARB + extensively atherosclerotic patient + bland urine” → think bilateral renal artery stenosis or stenosis in a solitary kidney.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. ACE inhibitors dilate the efferent arteriole.<br /></br>2. In bilateral RAS, each kidney is already relying on angiotensin II–mediated efferent constriction to preserve GFR.<br /></br>3. Removing that constriction causes precipitous fall in intraglomerular pressure → acute rise in serum creatinine/BUN with otherwise benign urine.<br /></br>4. Unilateral RAS would not do this because the contralateral kidney compensates.</p></br><p>Take-home message: A &gt;30% bump in creatinine soon after starting an ACE I/ARB mandates evaluation for bilateral renal artery stenosis.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Renal artery stenosis (usually due to atherosclerosis in older men, fibromuscular dysplasia in young women) limits perfusion pressure.<br /></br>• Kidneys up-regulate renin → more ANG II → preferential efferent constriction → maintain GFR.<br /></br>• ACE I/ARB removes ANG II effect → ↓efferent tone → big GFR drop if both kidneys depend on it.  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>1. Stop the ACE inhibitor if creatinine ↑ &gt;30%.<br /></br>2. Duplex Doppler ultrasound → first-line, or CT/MR angiography to confirm RAS.<br /></br>3. Definitive therapy: angioplasty ± stent (selected cases) or surgical revascularization; aggressive risk-factor control (statin, antiplatelet, smoking cessation).<br /></br>4. If only unilateral RAS and renal function stable, ACE I/ARB is beneficial.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ACE-I + Atherosclerotic Old Man → Check Cr within a month”<br /></br>Mnemonic: “CAP”<br /></br>• Creatinine rise &gt;30%<br /></br>• Atherosclerosis history<br /></br>• Plain urine (bland UA)</p></br><hr /></br><p>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Acute interstitial nephritis<br /></br>   • Usually drug-induced (β-lactams, PPIs, NSAIDs, diuretics) or infections; classically presents with fever, rash, eosinophilia, WBC/WBC-cast urine ± microscopic hematuria. None of these are present; UA is bland. ACE I are not typical culprits.<br /></br>b. Acute tubular necrosis<br /></br>   • Would show muddy brown granular casts, possibly fractional excretion of Na⁺ &gt;2%, often occurs after ischemia or nephrotoxins. Not seen here.<br /></br>d. Renal vein thrombosis<br /></br>   • Presents with sudden flank pain, hematuria, enlarged kidney, possibly nephrotic-range proteinuria. Not consistent with bland UA or absence of pain.<br /></br>e. Unilateral renal artery stenosis<br /></br>   • Contralateral kidney keeps overall GFR stable; creatinine rise is minimal (&lt;30%) after ACE I. A 140% rise indicates both kidneys are flow-limited.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Bottom line: In an older, heavily atherosclerotic patient, a marked creatinine increase shortly after initiating an ACE inhibitor with a benign urinalysis is virtually diagnostic of bilateral renal artery stenosis, not acute interstitial nephritis.</p>
44. A 62-year-old woman, gravida 3, para 3, comes to the physician because of a 3-month history of a painless mass protruding from her vagina with bowel movements. She has the sensation of incomplete emptying of the bladder unless she pushes the mass back into her vagina. During the past 5 years, she has had increased pelvic pressure with prolonged standing or lifting. She does not have urine loss when coughing or laughing. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. All her pregnancies ended in uncomplicated vaginal deliveries. The patient is 160 cm (5 ft 3 in) tall and weighs 50 kg (110 lb); BMI is 20 kg/m². Vital signs are within normal limits. A photograph of the mass is shown. Examination of the mass shows no other abnormalities. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Bartholin gland cyst</br>b. Cystocele</br>c. Leiomyoma uteri</br>d. Urethral diverticulum</br>e. Uterine sarcoma</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Cystocele (anterior vaginal wall prolapse of the bladder)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Post-menopausal woman (62 y) with 3 mo history of a “painless mass protruding from her vagina with bowel movements” → classic description of a vaginal wall prolapse that becomes evident with Valsalva/straining.<br /></br>• “Sensation of incomplete emptying of the bladder unless she pushes the mass back” → the bladder itself is contained in the prolapse; manual reduction (splinting) permits complete voiding.<br /></br>• Chronic pelvic pressure that worsens “with prolonged standing or lifting” → increased intra-abdominal pressure stretches pelvic‐floor support.<br /></br>• No stress urinary incontinence (no loss with coughing/laughing) → favors cystocele over urethral sphincter incompetence; stress incontinence often coexists but is not required.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no pain, no infectious symptoms, no suprapubic mass on bimanual exam, and the protrusion is at the introitus (not a posterior perineal lesion). These findings collectively rule in an anterior wall prolapse (cystocele) and rule out Bartholin cyst, leiomyoma, urethral diverticulum, and uterine malignancy.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify compartment: Is the bulge anterior (bladder), posterior (rectum/enterocele), or apical (uterus)?<br /></br>2. Check associated urinary symptoms → sense of incomplete emptying that improves with reduction = bladder involvement.<br /></br>3. Risk factors: multiparity, chronic ↑ intra-abdominal pressure, menopause (estrogen deficiency), advancing age.<br /></br>4. Conclusion: anterior vaginal wall prolapse = cystocele.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: A cystocele presents as an anterior vaginal wall bulge that worsens with Valsalva and may require manual reduction to void.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Support of the anterior vaginal wall depends on endopelvic fascia + pubocervical fascia + levator ani muscles.<br /></br>• Childbirth, chronic straining, obesity, menopause → weakening &amp; stretching → bladder herniates through vaginal introitus.<br /></br>Clinical manifestations<br /></br>– Bulge/pressure sensation, worsens late in day or with standing<br /></br>– Voiding dysfunction: urinary retention, frequency, incomplete emptying, need to splint<br /></br>– +/- stress incontinence</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnosis &amp; Management<br /></br>• Physical exam with patient straining confirms diagnosis (no imaging needed unless surgical planning).<br /></br>• First-line treatment:<br /></br>  – Pelvic-floor muscle (Kegel) exercises<br /></br>  – Pessary (support device) for symptomatic patients who desire non-surgical therapy or are poor surgical candidates<br /></br>• Definitive: anterior colporrhaphy (surgical repair of pubocervical fascia) ± concomitant mid-urethral sling if stress incontinence present.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Cysto-SEAL” mnemonic<br /></br>C – Chronic pressure/childbirth<br /></br>Y – You push it back to p- void (splint)<br /></br>S – Straining makes it Show<br /></br>T – Trouble emptying bladder<br /></br>O – Occurs anteriorly<br /></br>SEAL – SEAL = bulge / prolapse</p></br><p>Visual: Think of the bladder as a water balloon pushing through a weak canvas (vaginal wall) whenever you bear down.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Bartholin gland cyst<br /></br>• Location: posterolateral introitus (4 &amp; 8 o’clock).<br /></br>• Presents as single, well-circumscribed fluctuant mass at vestibule, often tender if infected.<br /></br>• No voiding problems; does not enlarge with Valsalva.  </p></br><p>c. Leiomyoma uteri<br /></br>• Benign smooth-muscle tumor within uterine body; produces heavy menses, pelvic fullness, or reproductive issues, not a protruding vaginal mass.<br /></br>• Speculum exam shows normal introitus; bimanual exam reveals enlarged irregular uterus.  </p></br><p>d. Urethral diverticulum<br /></br>• Outpouching of urethral mucosa into anterior vaginal wall; classically causes post-void dribbling, dysuria, dyspareunia; small tender mass along urethra.<br /></br>• Does not produce a large prolapsing mass requiring manual reduction, and rarely changes with Valsalva.  </p></br><p>e. Uterine sarcoma<br /></br>• Malignant uterine soft-tissue tumor; presents with rapidly enlarging, often painful pelvic mass, abnormal bleeding.<br /></br>• Remains in pelvis; does not prolapse externally through vagina.  </p></br><p>Common pitfall: Confusing cystocele with Bartholin cyst—remember cystocele is an anterior wall bulge linked to urinary complaints; Bartholin lesions sit low and lateral at the vestibule and do not affect micturition.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>High-Yield Bite<br /></br>Anterior vaginal wall bulge + voiding difficulty relieved by manual reduction = cystocele. Past multiparity + menopause = classic risk profile.</p>
45. A 1-day-old male newborn is evaluated in the hospital nursery. He was born at term to a 23-year-old woman, gravida 1, para 1, following an uncomplicated pregnancy and spontaneous vaginal delivery. Apgar scores were 7 and 8 at 1 and 5 minutes, respectively. He has been bottle-fed but feeds slowly. On examination of the patient’s mother, grip strength is 4/5, and she has difficulty releasing her grip. The mother is adopted; her and the father’s family medical histories are unknown. The patient does not appear to be in distress. He is at the 45th percentile for length, 75th percentile for weight, and 50th percentile for head circumference. His temperature is 35.7°C (96.2°F), pulse is 132/min, respirations are 36/min, and blood pressure is 82/45 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 98%. He does not have a vigorous suck or loud cry. On passive range of motion, muscle tone is decreased throughout. Spontaneous movements are decreased when the patient is awake. Moro reflex is poor. Deep tendon reflexes are absent. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Congenital muscular dystrophy</br>b. Duchenne muscular dystrophy</br>c. Myotonic dystrophy</br>d. Myotubular myopathy</br>e. Nemaline myopathy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>C. Myotonic dystrophy (congenital form)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key maternal clue – “grip strength 4/5 and difficulty releasing her grip.”  ➜ Classic description of myotonia (delayed relaxation after contraction).<br /></br>• Autosomal-dominant disease with anticipation; larger CTG-repeat expansions passed from an affected mother often present as the severe “congenital” form in the neonate.<br /></br>• Neonatal findings – hypotonia (“floppy baby”), weak cry, poor suck, ↓ spontaneous movement, absent DTRs, normal facial/limb anatomy.<br /></br>• Normal vitals/oxygenation and no structural anomalies ⇒ points toward a primary myopathic (not neurologic, metabolic, or cardiopulmonary) etiology.<br /></br>These maternal + neonatal clues together essentially pathognomonic for congenital myotonic dystrophy.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Stepwise)<br /></br>1. Floppy neonate differential: sepsis, CNS injury, chromosomal d/o (Down, Prader-Willi), SMA type 1, congenital muscular dystrophies/myopathies.<br /></br>2. Maternal myotonia immediately narrows list to myotonic dystrophy (AD, CTG repeat).<br /></br>3. Duchenne and most congenital myopathies lack maternal findings because they are usually X-linked recessive or de novo.<br /></br>4. The pattern (affected mother ➜ much sicker newborn) = anticipation, hallmark of myotonic dystrophy.</p></br><p>Take-home core concept: Maternal grip myotonia + floppy newborn = congenital myotonic dystrophy due to CTG-repeat expansion in the DMPK gene.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Gene: DMPK (chromosome 19) with CTG trinucleotide repeat expansion.<br /></br>• RNA toxic gain-of-function → abnormal splicing of chloride &amp; insulin receptor mRNA → myotonia, muscle wasting, endocrine issues.<br /></br>• Anticipation: repeat length ↑ in successive generations, particularly when transmitted by the mother; &gt;1000 repeats → congenital form.<br /></br>• Neonatal manifestations: severe generalized hypotonia, respiratory insufficiency, feeding difficulty, arthrogryposis, facial diplegia.<br /></br>• Later complications (survivors): intellectual disability, cardiac conduction defects.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Notes<br /></br>Neonate:<br /></br>• Supportive—maintain airway (often need ventilation), nasogastric or gastrostomy feeds, PT/OT.<br /></br>Mother/family:<br /></br>• Confirm with molecular genetics; offer genetic counseling.<br /></br>• Maternal myotonia can respond to mexiletine; screen adults for arrhythmias (ECG).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“CTG = Can’t Turn Grip”<br /></br>• CTG repeat → Can’t Turn Grip (delayed release).<br /></br>• Picture mother stuck in a handshake passing a long CTG ribbon to her floppy baby in a sling.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>A. Congenital muscular dystrophy (e.g., merosin-deficient)<br /></br>• Presents with hypotonia &amp; weakness, BUT no maternal myotonia; family history usually AR. Often see contractures, elevated CK, and white-matter changes on MRI—none in vignette.</p></br><p>B. Duchenne muscular dystrophy<br /></br>• X-linked; onset 2–3 yr with Gower sign and calf pseudohypertrophy, NOT in a 1-day-old. Mothers typically asymptomatic carriers (no grip myotonia).</p></br><p>D. Myotubular (centronuclear) myopathy<br /></br>• X-linked; severe hypotonia at birth similar to vignette, but mothers are just carriers without myotonia. Muscle biopsy shows centrally placed nuclei (diagnostic but not suggested here).</p></br><p>E. Nemaline myopathy<br /></br>• Floppy infant with respiratory weakness; biopsy: nemaline rods. Again, no maternal grip myotonia; usually sporadic or AR.</p></br><p>Key discriminating feature across all distractors: only myotonic dystrophy links maternal myotonia + neonatal hypotonia via anticipation.</p>
46. An 88-year-old woman remains hospitalized 1 day after undergoing an 8-hour total hip replacement. She is 163 cm (5 ft 4 in) tall and weighs 77 kg, or 169lb; BMI is 29 kg/m². Her temperature is 36.8 °C (98.2 °F), pulse is 73/min, respirations are 12/min, and blood pressure is 152/89 mm Hg. Examination shows edema of the left gluteus maximus and palsy of the sciatic nerve. A urinary catheter is in place and contains red-colored urine. Laboratory studies show:</br>Serum</br>Na⁺ 138 mEq/L</br>K⁺ 6 mEq/L</br>Cl⁻ 98 mEq/L</br>HCO₃⁻ 20 mEq/L</br>Ca²⁺ 8 mg/dL</br>Creatinine 2 mg/dL</br>Creatine kinase 20,000 U/L</br>Phosphorus 4.0 mg/dL</br>Alkaline phosphatase 110 U/L</br>Urine</br>Blood 2+</br>Protein 2+</br>RBC 4/hpf</br>WBC 0/hpf</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Administration of furosemide</br>b. Administration of intravenous 0.9% saline</br>c. Administration of lisinopril</br>d. Administration of mannitol</br>e. Elevation of the left lower extremity</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>Administration of intravenous 0.9 % saline  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “8-hour total hip replacement,” “edema of the left gluteus maximus,” and “sciatic nerve palsy”  → prolonged external compression/ischemia of the gluteal musculature.<br /></br>• “Red-colored urine,” “urine dipstick blood 2+ but only 4 RBC/hpf,” CK 20 000 U/L → myoglobinuria from rhabdomyolysis (dipstick detects heme pigment in myoglobin even when microscopic RBCs are absent).<br /></br>• Lab triad typical for rhabdo-induced acute kidney injury (AKI):<br /></br>  – Hyperkalemia (K⁺ 6 mEq/L)<br /></br>  – Mild metabolic acidosis (HCO₃⁻ 20 mEq/L)<br /></br>  – Rising creatinine (2 mg/dL)<br /></br>• Elderly postoperative patient with BMI 29 → high risk of pressure-induced muscle necrosis.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Core step-wise approach to rhabdomyolysis:<br /></br>1. Recognize the pattern: muscle injury + very high CK + myoglobin-positive urine with few RBCs.<br /></br>2. Prevent &amp; treat AKI: rapidly flush nephrotoxic myoglobin out of the renal tubules with large-volume isotonic crystalloid.<br /></br>3. Correct life-threatening electrolyte disturbances (hyperkalemia) once circulating volume is restored.  </p></br><p>Take-home message: The single most important initial therapy in rhabdomyolysis is aggressive isotonic saline hydration.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Muscle cell breakdown → release of myoglobin, K⁺, phosphate, purines. Myoglobin precipitates in the renal tubules, especially in an acidic milieu, causing acute tubular necrosis. Volume depletion peri-operatively worsens renal vasoconstriction and pigment nephropathy. Early saline expansion increases renal perfusion and urine flow, dilutes myoglobin concentration, alkali administration can follow to keep urine pH &gt; 6.5.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>First-line:<br /></br>• 0.9 % saline bolus then high-rate infusion (e.g., 200–500 mL/h, titrate to urine output ≥200 mL/h).<br /></br>Adjuncts (after adequate hydration, individualized):<br /></br>• IV sodium bicarbonate if severe acidosis or to alkalinize urine.<br /></br>• Mannitol (osmotic diuretic) sometimes used to increase flow, but evidence mixed; never before fluids.<br /></br>• Calcium gluconate, insulin/glucose, or albuterol for emergent hyperkalemia management if ECG changes arise.<br /></br>Monitoring: serial CK, creatinine, electrolytes, urine output.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“HEM” for rhabdo:<br /></br>H – Heme-positive urine with no RBCs<br /></br>E – Elevated CK (&gt;5,000)<br /></br>M – Myoglobin harms kidneys → give Massive fluids first.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Furosemide: Loop diuretics may increase urine flow but worsen intravascular volume depletion if given before fluids. Not first-line and can aggravate AKI.<br /></br>c. Lisinopril: ACE inhibitors decrease GFR and raise K⁺—contraindicated in acute renal injury with existing hyperkalemia.<br /></br>d. Mannitol: Can be used as an adjunct osmotic diuretic after rehydration; giving it without prior fluid resuscitation risks volume depletion and renal hypoperfusion.<br /></br>e. Elevation of the left lower extremity: Helpful for edema and compartment pressure reduction but does nothing for systemic myoglobin-induced renal injury, which is the urgent threat.</p></br><p>Bottom Line: Identify rhabdomyolysis by CK + “blood but no RBCs,” treat immediately with aggressive isotonic saline to protect the kidneys.</p>
47. A 67-year-old man is brought to the emergency department because of a 2-day history of confusion. His wife says he is forgetful and disoriented to time and place. He has small cell carcinoma of the lung, type 2 diabetes mellitus, and hypertension. His medications are lisinopril and metformin. He smoked one and one-half packs of cigarettes daily for 45 years but quit 5 years ago. He drinks six to seven 12-oz beers weekly. On arrival, he has a generalized tonic-clonic seizure. He is unarousable. His blood pressure is 136/76 mm Hg; other vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows normal skin turgor; there is no edema. Neurologic examination shows no focal findings. Laboratory studies show:</br>Serum</br>Na⁺  110 mEq/L</br>K⁺   3.5 mEq/L</br>Urea nitrogen  10 mg/dL</br>Glucose  220 mg/dL</br>Creatinine  0.8 mg/dL</br>Uric acid  3.6 mg/dL</br>Urine</br>Sodium 60 mEq/L</br>Osmolality 650 mOsmol/kg H₂O</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Intravenous administration of 0.45% saline</br>b. Intravenous administration of 0.9% saline</br>c. Intravenous administration of 3% saline</br>d. Intravenous insulin therapy</br>e. Oral sodium tablet therapy</br>f. Water restriction</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Intravenous administration of 3% (hypertonic) saline  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Small-cell carcinoma of the lung” → classic paraneoplastic cause of SIADH (syndrome of inappropriate antidiuretic hormone secretion).<br /></br>• Serum Na⁺ 110 mEq/L (severe hyponatremia).<br /></br>• Urine Na⁺ 60 mEq/L and urine osmolality 650 mOsm/kg → inappropriately concentrated urine despite hyponatremia, confirming SIADH.<br /></br>• Normal skin turgor, no edema, normal BP → clinically euvolemic.<br /></br>• Neurologic manifestations: confusion progressing to generalized tonic-clonic seizure → life-threatening, symptomatic hyponatremia.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Determine tonicity: calculated serum Osm ≈ 236 mOsm/kg (low) → hypo-osmolar.<br /></br>2. Determine volume status: euvolemic (SIADH pattern).<br /></br>3. Determine symptom severity: seizures = severe neurologic symptoms.<br /></br>4. Acute, severe, symptomatic hypo-natremia → emergent therapy with hypertonic (3%) saline to rapidly raise serum Na⁺ by 4–6 mEq/L in the first few hours, stopping seizures and preventing cerebral edema.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• SIADH: ectopic ADH from small-cell lung carcinoma → ↑ water reabsorption in collecting ducts → dilutional hyponatremia, ↓ serum osmolality, ↑ urine osmolality/Na⁺ (kidney can still dump Na⁺).<br /></br>• Rapid fall in Na⁺ causes cerebral edema → confusion, seizures, coma.<br /></br>• Therapy goal: controlled, modest ↑ in Na⁺ to relieve cerebral edema without overshoot (≤8–10 mEq/L in first 24 h) to avoid osmotic demyelination syndrome.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Initial (emergent)<br /></br>• 3% saline IV bolus 100 mL over 10 min, repeat up to 3 times as needed to abort seizures.<br /></br>• Check serum Na⁺ every 1–2 h; once symptoms stop and Na⁺ &gt; 120 mEq/L, slow the correction (≈0.5 mEq/L/h).<br /></br>Subsequent / chronic management<br /></br>• Fluid restriction (&lt;1 L/day).<br /></br>• Salt tablets + loop diuretic or oral urea.<br /></br>• Vasopressin receptor antagonists (tolvaptan, conivaptan) or demeclocycline for refractory cases.<br /></br>• Treat underlying tumor (chemotherapy, radiation).  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Seizure or coma with SIADH? Think ‘3’ for 3 %.”<br /></br>S – Small cell<br /></br>I – Inappropriate ADH<br /></br>A – Acute neuro symptoms<br /></br>D – 3% saline (three letters after S)<br /></br>H – Hypertonic  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. 0.45 % saline (½-NS) – hypotonic; would deliver more free water than sodium, worsening hyponatremia and cerebral edema.<br /></br>b. 0.9 % saline – isotonic; in SIADH the kidney excretes the Na⁺ but retains the water (urine osm &gt; plasma), so isotonic saline can paradoxically lower serum Na⁺ further.<br /></br>d. Intravenous insulin – appropriate for hyperglycemia or DKA/HHS; glucose 220 mg/dL is only mildly elevated and not the cause of hyponatremia.<br /></br>e. Oral sodium tablets – used for chronic, asymptomatic SIADH, not rapid enough for seizures.<br /></br>f. Water restriction – first-line for mild/moderate and chronic SIADH; inadequate in acute symptomatic cases.  </p></br><p>Take-home Message<br /></br>Severe symptomatic hyponatremia (seizures, coma) demands rapid but controlled correction with hypertonic 3 % saline, regardless of the underlying cause. Overshooting or using hypotonic solutions can be fatal.</p>
48. A 67-year-old woman is admitted to the hospital because of severe shortness of breath. She has a 15-year history of emphysema. Her written advance directive states that she does not want mechanical ventilation in the event of respiratory failure. She now states that she has changed her mind and asks the physician to “do everything” for her, including mechanical ventilation if necessary for survival. Her husband states that she never wanted to “live on a machine.” She is oriented to person, place, time, and situation. Her pulse is 110/min, respirations are 28/min and labored, and blood pressure is 90/60 mm Hg. Examination shows diffuse expiratory wheezing. Inspiratory crackles are heard over the right upper and lower lung fields. Arterial blood gas analysis on 100% oxygen by face mask shows:</br>pH 7.19</br>PCO₂ 70 mm Hg</br>PO₂ 60 mm Hg</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in patient care?	Correct answer: F</br></br>a. Consult the hospital ethics committee</br>b. Consult the patient’s attorney to revise the advance directive</br>c. Request that the husband decide whether to use mechanical ventilation</br>d. Perform psychiatric assessment</br>e. Continue oxygen by face mask and administer intravenous morphine for comfort</br>f. Intubate and begin mechanical ventilation</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>f. Intubate and begin mechanical ventilation  </p></br><p>===============================<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Written advance directive … does not want mechanical ventilation” – establishes a prior instruction.<br /></br>• “She now states that she has changed her mind and asks the physician to ‘do everything’” – NEW, explicit request.<br /></br>• “She is oriented to person, place, time, and situation.” – Demonstrates intact decision-making capacity (competence).<br /></br>• ABG on 100 % O₂: pH 7.19, PCO₂ 70, PO₂ 60 + labored respirations, hypotension → impending respiratory failure; urgent intervention needed.<br /></br>Key pattern: Competent patient rescinds previous advance directive + emergent need for ventilatory support.</p></br><p>===============================<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Does the patient currently have decision-making capacity? Yes (oriented, coherent).<br /></br>2. A capacitated patient’s contemporaneous wishes ALWAYS supersede:<br /></br>   • Prior written advance directives<br /></br>   • Surrogate / family opinions<br /></br>3. She requests full life-sustaining treatment.<br /></br>4. Clinical picture warrants immediate airway protection and ventilatory support.<br /></br>=&gt; Intubate now.</p></br><p>Take-home message: In ethics, the hierarchy is (highest to lowest) — Current competent patient &gt; advance directive &gt; surrogate &gt; best-interest standard.</p></br><p>===============================<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (concise)<br /></br>Emphysema (COPD) exacerbation + superimposed pneumonia (crackles RUL/RLL) → V/Q mismatch, CO₂ retention → acute on chronic hypercapnic respiratory failure (respiratory acidosis). Without ventilation she is at high risk for arrest.</p></br><p>===============================<br /></br>Treatments / Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Immediate:<br /></br>• Endotracheal intubation + mechanical ventilation (assist-control or bilevel as appropriate).<br /></br>• Broad-spectrum antibiotics for pneumonia (e.g., ceftriaxone + azithromycin).<br /></br>• Systemic corticosteroids for COPD exacerbation (IV methylprednisolone).<br /></br>• Nebulized albuterol/ipratropium.<br /></br>• Treat hypotension (IV fluids ± vasopressors).<br /></br>Diagnostics after stabilization: CXR, sputum cultures, CBC, CMP.</p></br><p>===============================<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“CAPS” for capacity hierarchy:<br /></br>C – Competent patient (top)<br /></br>A – Advance directive (if patient lacks capacity)<br /></br>P – Proxy / surrogate<br /></br>S – Substituted best-interest  </p></br><p>Imagine CAP (patient) sitting on top of a stack of papers (Advance Directive) held by a Proxy; knock off the CAP and everything else falls.</p></br><p>===============================<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Consult the hospital ethics committee – Not needed; no ethical conflict once the competent patient’s wishes are clear. Ethics consults are for TRUE conflicts (e.g., surrogate vs incapacitated patient).<br /></br>b. Consult the patient’s attorney to revise the advance directive – Legal redrafting is unnecessary in an emergency; verbal revocation by a competent patient is immediately valid.<br /></br>c. Request that the husband decide – Surrogates come into play ONLY when the patient lacks decision-making capacity. Here she is competent.<br /></br>d. Perform psychiatric assessment – No evidence of delirium, psychosis, or depression impairing capacity; delaying lifesaving therapy for psych evaluation would be inappropriate.<br /></br>e. Continue oxygen by face mask and give morphine for comfort – Palliative approach contrary to the patient’s expressed desire for full treatment; also inadequate for life-threatening hypercapnic failure.</p></br><p>===============================<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>A competent patient can change her advance directive at any time. When she requests full treatment in the face of impending respiratory failure, immediate intubation and mechanical ventilation is ethically and clinically obligatory.</p>
49. Six hours after undergoing esophagectomy for esophageal cancer, a 52-year-old man’s blood pressure increases from 130/86 mm Hg to 180/100 mm Hg. Despite receiving an epidural infusion of fentanyl, he rates incisional pain as an 8 on a 10-point scale. He is receiving 5% dextrose in 0.45% saline (125 mL/h); including fluids administered intraoperatively, his fluid balance is +4 L. His postoperative urine output is 50 mL. His pulse is 110/min, and respirations are 18/min. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 100%. Examination shows a dry, intact incision site and a patent nasogastric tube that irrigates clear fluid. The lungs are clear to auscultation. The abdomen is flat, and there is exquisite incisional tenderness. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. His hemoglobin concentration is 10.5 g/dL, and hematocrit is 32%. The most appropriate next step in management of this patient’s increased blood pressure is intravenous administration of a medication from which of the following drug classes?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. ACE inhibitor</br>b. β Adrenergic blocker</br>c. Calcium-channel blocker</br>d. Diuretic</br>e. Narcotic</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Narcotic (e.g., an IV opioid such as morphine or hydromorphone)</p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Sudden postoperative hypertension: BP rose from 130/86 → 180/100 mm Hg within 6 h.<br /></br>• Severe, uncontrolled incisional pain: 8/10 despite an epidural infusion. Pain is a potent sympathetic stimulator.<br /></br>• Normal oxygenation (SaO₂ 100 %), clear lungs, flat abdomen: rules out hypoxia, abdominal compartment, or pulmonary cause.<br /></br>• +4 L fluid balance with low urine output (50 mL): worrisome but occurs early after major surgery; no pulmonary crackles, no peripheral edema to suggest acute volume-overload–induced pulmonary congestion.<br /></br>• No neurologic signs of stroke, no chest pain to suggest MI, no evidence of pheochromocytoma or thyroid storm.<br /></br>Key takeaway: The only obvious trigger for catecholamine surge is inadequately treated postoperative pain.</p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Identify reversible sympathetic triggers. Postop HTN most commonly results from pain, agitation, bladder distention, or hypoxia.<br /></br>Step 2 – Address the underlying cause first, before reflexively adding chronic antihypertensives.<br /></br>Step 3 – Adequate IV analgesia (narcotic) directly treats the stimulus and secondarily lowers BP. Using β-blockers, ACE inhibitors, CCBs, or diuretics treats the number, not the cause, and can mask inadequate pain control.</p></br><p>Core concept: Treat the precipitating stimulus (pain) rather than the hypertension itself in the immediate postop period.</p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Tissue injury → nociceptive afferents → hypothalamic–pituitary–adrenal (HPA) axis and sympathetic nervous system → ↑catecholamines (epi/norepi) → peripheral vasoconstriction, ↑HR, ↑BP.<br /></br>• Opioids (µ-receptor agonists) blunt nociceptive transmission in the dorsal horn and activate descending inhibitory pathways, quickly reducing sympathetic outflow.</p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>First-line: IV opioid (e.g., 2–4 mg morphine, 0.5–1 mg hydromorphone) with reassessment of pain/BP in 10–15 min.<br /></br>Second-line/adjuncts:<br /></br>• Re-evaluate epidural catheter placement/dose.<br /></br>• Non-opioid adjuncts (ketorolac, acetaminophen) once bleeding risk addressed.<br /></br>Diagnostics: none immediately required; monitor urine output and consider bladder scan, CMP if oliguria persists.</p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“PAIN = PRESSURE” – Uncontrolled Pain After Immediate (Narcotic) deficiency Elevates BP.<br /></br>Mnemonic for acute postop HTN work-up: “PAIN-BAD”<br /></br>P – Pain<br /></br>A – Anxiety/Agitation<br /></br>I – Increased ICP<br /></br>N – Nicotine withdrawal<br /></br>B – Bladder distention<br /></br>A – Arterial hypoxemia<br /></br>D – Drugs (withdrawal)</p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. ACE inhibitor – Appropriate for chronic essential HTN or heart failure; slow onset IV forms limited. Does not address acute sympathetic surge; risk of postoperative hypotension and renal hypoperfusion.<br /></br>b. β-Adrenergic blocker – Esmolol/labetalol can rapidly lower BP but mask pain, may precipitate bradycardia, bronchospasm; still leaves patient in significant pain. Use only if pain adequately controlled and BP remains high.<br /></br>c. Calcium-channel blocker – IV nicardipine useful for refractory hypertensive emergencies but again treats the number, not the cause, and may cause reflex tachycardia.<br /></br>d. Diuretic – Postop oliguria is multifactorial (third spacing, ADH surge). Loop diuretic without evaluating volume status can worsen renal perfusion; diuretics do not acutely lower BP driven by catecholamines.<br /></br>e. Narcotic – Correct: removes primary stimulus (pain) → decreases sympathetic tone → normalizes BP.</p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In the immediate postoperative period, new-onset hypertension is most commonly catecholamine-mediated from unrelieved pain. Give an IV narcotic to treat the pain; the blood pressure will usually normalize without additional antihypertensives.</p>
50. A 1000-g (2-lb 3-oz) newborn has no spontaneous breathing following delivery at 34 weeks’ gestation. He is intubated and mechanically ventilated, and he is admitted to the neonatal intensive care unit. Pregnancy was complicated by marked oligohydramnios. His temperature is 36.5 °C (97.7 °F), and pulse is 160/min. Examination shows flat facies. Breath sounds are decreased. Heart sounds are normal. There are no abdominal masses. Which of the following is the most likely cause of these findings?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Choanal atresia</br>b. Congenital pneumonia</br>c. Pneumothorax</br>d. Pulmonary hypoplasia</br>e. Tracheoesophageal fistula</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d.
