Pulmonary hypoplasia  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Marked oligohydramnios” → too little amniotic fluid is the classic set-up for Potter sequence.<br /></br>• “Flat facies” → compressed facial features from chronic intrauterine constraint, another Potter clue.<br /></br>• “No spontaneous breathing” + “breath sounds are decreased” → suggests the lungs never developed adequately (not just an airway obstruction).<br /></br>• Normal heart sounds and no abdominal masses help exclude congenital heart disease or diaphragmatic hernia.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (why pulmonary hypoplasia?)<br /></br>Step 1: Ask “Why was there oligohydramnios?”  In many fetuses it is because the kidneys are absent or non-functioning and therefore can’t produce urine, the main source of amniotic fluid after 2nd trimester.<br /></br>Step 2: Oligohydramnios → uterine wall presses on fetus →<br /></br>  • External compression → flat facies, limb defects<br /></br>  • Lack of fluid to ‘breathe’ in utero → lungs cannot expand → pulmonary hypoplasia<br /></br>Step 3: Immediately after delivery, baby cannot ventilate because lungs are too small → weak/absent breath sounds, refractory respiratory failure.  </p></br><p>Take-home core concept: “Potter sequence = Oligohydramnios → Pulmonary hypoplasia + characteristic facies ± limb deformities → lethal respiratory failure at birth.”  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Usually bilateral renal agenesis, posterior urethral valves, or prolonged rupture of membranes → ↓ fetal urine → oligohydramnios.<br /></br>• Mechanism: Reduced amniotic fluid removes liquid cushion → mechanical compression + impaired lung liquid inspiration → pulmonary hypoplasia.<br /></br>• Clinical manifestations: Flat nose, low-set ears, micrognathia, limb contractures, and severe respiratory insufficiency immediately after birth.  </p></br><p>Treatment / Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• No curative therapy post-natally; management is supportive ventilation but prognosis is poor because lung tissue is absent, not merely immature.<br /></br>• Prenatal ultrasound typically shows oligohydramnios and absent/abnormal kidneys.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>POTTER sequence mnemonic:<br /></br>P – Pulmonary hypoplasia<br /></br>O – Oligohydramnios (trigger)<br /></br>T – Twisted (flat) facial features<br /></br>T – Twisted skin folds<br /></br>E – Extremity defects<br /></br>R – Renal agenesis (cause)  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Choanal atresia – Presents with cyclic cyanosis relieved by crying; catheter cannot pass through nostril. Amniotic fluid is normal; no flat facies.<br /></br>b. Congenital pneumonia – Causes tachypnea, grunting, diffuse crackles/infiltrates, often from maternal infection. Does not explain oligohydramnios or Potter facies.<br /></br>c. Pneumothorax – Sudden deterioration, unilateral hyperresonance and transillumination, often after positive-pressure ventilation; not linked to oligohydramnios or characteristic facies.<br /></br>e. Tracheoesophageal fistula – Associated with polyhydramnios (inability to swallow), copious secretions, and abdominal distention from air in gut, not oligohydramnios; breath sounds usually present.  </p></br><p>Key Differences: Only pulmonary hypoplasia simultaneously explains (1) severe oligohydramnios, (2) Potter facies, and (3) absent breath sounds with immediate ventilatory failure.</p>
51. A 67-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 6-month history of generalized itching and facial reddening and flushing. She has hypertension and hyperlipidemia. Medications are benazepril and lovastatin. She does not smoke cigarettes or use illicit drugs. She drinks alcohol only on social occasions. Her temperature is 36.5°C (97.7°F), pulse is 80/min, respirations are 12/min, and blood pressure is 145/90 mm Hg. Examination of the face shows a ruddy complexion and no rash. The lungs are clear to auscultation. Cardiac examination shows no abnormalities. The spleen tip is palpated 4 cm below the left costal margin and is nontender. There is no hepatomegaly. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin 18 g/dL</br>Hematocrit 56%</br>Mean corpuscular volume 110 µm³</br>Leukocyte count 12,000/mm³</br>Platelet count 550,000/mm³</br>Serum creatinine 1 mg/dL</br>Additional studies are most likely to show which of the following?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Decreased vitamin B₁₂ (cobalamin) concentration</br>b. Increased anticardiolipin-antibody concentration</br>c. Increased erythrocyte mass</br>d. Increased erythropoietin concentration</br>e. Increased methemoglobin concentration</br>f. Prothrombin mutation</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>c. Increased erythrocyte mass  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “6-month history of generalized itching and facial reddening and flushing” → Aquagenic pruritus and facial plethora are classic clues for polycythemia vera (PV).<br /></br>• “Ruddy complexion … splenomegaly (spleen tip 4 cm below costal margin)” → Splenomegaly from extramedullary hematopoiesis supports a myeloproliferative neoplasm.<br /></br>• CBC: Hemoglobin 18 g/dL, Hct 56 %, leukocytes 12 000/µL, platelets 550 000/µL → Trilineage elevation (erythrocytosis + leukocytosis + thrombocytosis) is typical for PV, unlike secondary causes that raise only RBCs.<br /></br>• Normal creatinine, no COPD, no smoking, normal oxygen saturation implied → Rules out secondary erythrocytosis (hypoxia-driven or renal disease).  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives: aquagenic pruritus, plethora, splenomegaly, ↑Hgb/Hct, leukocytosis, thrombocytosis.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no hypoxia, no renal mass, no smoking/OSA, normal vitals except mild HTN.  </p></br><p>Pattern → Polycythemia vera.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify erythrocytosis with trilineage elevation + splenomegaly.<br /></br>2. Link aquagenic pruritus/flushing to histamine release from ↑ basophils in PV.<br /></br>3. Myeloproliferative neoplasms produce excess mature cells autonomously → Erythropoietin (EPO) is suppressed, but true red-cell mass is increased.<br /></br>4. Therefore, the investigation most likely to be abnormal is a direct measurement of erythrocyte (red-cell) mass → it will be increased.  </p></br><p>Take-home: PV = primary, JAK2-mutated, low-EPO erythrocytosis with ↑ total red-cell mass.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Mutation: JAK2 V617F (&gt;95 % of cases) → Constitutive tyrosine-kinase activity → cytokine-independent proliferation of erythroid, myeloid, megakaryocytic lines.<br /></br>• ↑ Basophils → histamine → aquagenic pruritus.<br /></br>• Hyperviscosity, platelet dysfunction → thrombosis/bleeding risk.<br /></br>• ↑ Transcobalamin I from neutrophils → serum vitamin B12 level is often high, not low.<br /></br>• Negative feedback → ↓ EPO from kidney.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment High-Yield Points<br /></br>Diagnostics<br /></br>1. CBC: ↑ Hgb/Hct ± leukocytosis/thrombocytosis.<br /></br>2. LOW EPO level.<br /></br>3. JAK2 mutation analysis (confirmatory).<br /></br>4. Nuclear-tagged red-cell study → ↑ red-cell mass (definition of absolute polycythemia).  </p></br><p>Treatment<br /></br>• First-line: Intermittent phlebotomy to keep Hct &lt;45 % (&lt;42 % in women with high risk).<br /></br>• Cytoreduction if high-risk (age &gt;60 y or prior thrombosis): hydroxyurea.<br /></br>• Low-dose aspirin for thrombosis prophylaxis.<br /></br>• Ruxolitinib (JAK1/2 inhibitor) if refractory/intolerant to hydroxyurea.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“PV = 3 P’s”<br /></br>1. Plethora (ruddy face)<br /></br>2. Pruritus after a hot shower<br /></br>3. Panhyper-cytosis (RBCs ↑, WBCs ↑, platelets ↑)<br /></br>Link “Hot shower itch” → “Hot blood counts.”  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Decreased vitamin B12 concentration<br /></br>• PV typically shows HIGH B12 (due to ↑ neutrophil transcobalamin I). Low B12 would lead to macrocytic anemia, not erythrocytosis.  </p></br><p>b. Increased anticardiolipin-antibody concentration<br /></br>• Seen in antiphospholipid syndrome → venous/arterial thromboses + pregnancy loss; does not cause erythrocytosis, pruritus, or splenomegaly.  </p></br><p>d. Increased erythropoietin concentration<br /></br>• Secondary polycythemia (chronic hypoxia, renal cell carcinoma, OSA, high-altitude) shows ↑ EPO, but typically no leukocytosis/thrombocytosis and no splenomegaly. PV shows LOW EPO.  </p></br><p>e. Increased methemoglobin concentration<br /></br>• Methemoglobinemia causes cyanosis and chocolate-colored blood with NORMAL PaO₂; it does not elevate Hct or cause pruritus/splenomegaly.  </p></br><p>f. Prothrombin G20210A mutation<br /></br>• Hereditary thrombophilia presenting with venous clots; normal CBC, no splenomegaly, no erythrocytosis.  </p></br><p>Key discriminator: Only PV explains trilineage elevation, aquagenic pruritus, splenomegaly, and low EPO → therefore ↑ measured red-cell mass is the most likely additional finding.</p>
52. A 15-year-old boy is brought to the emergency department 1 hour after the onset of severe abdominal pain. During the past 2 months, he has had several 30-minute episodes of mild abdominal pain after eating fatty foods. There is a family history of anemia. On arrival, the pain is resolving. His temperature is 37 °C (98.6 °F), pulse is 90/min, and blood pressure is 120/70 mm Hg. The spleen tip is palpated 2 cm below the left costal margin. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin 10.2 g/dL</br>Leukocyte count 11,000/mm³</br>Reticulocyte count 12%</br>Serum</br>Bilirubin, total 2 mg/dL</br>Direct 0.5 mg/dL</br>Alkaline phosphatase 50 U/L</br>AST 55 U/L</br>ALT 70 U/L</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. CT scan of the abdomen</br>b. Oral ciprofloxacin therapy for 1 week and follow‐up examination in 2 weeks</br>c. Intravenous antibiotic therapy and endoscopic retrograde cholangiopancreatography</br>d. Intravenous antibiotic therapy and cholecystectomy</br>e. Cholecystectomy and splenectomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Cholecystectomy and splenectomy  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues that lock in the diagnosis of hereditary spherocytosis (HS) with symptomatic pigment gallstones:</p></br><p>• 15-year-old boy with episodic, post-prandial RUQ pain → classic biliary colic.<br /></br>• Family history of anemia + splenomegaly (spleen tip 2 cm below LCM) → inherited hemolytic anemia.<br /></br>• Labs: normocytic anemia (Hb 10.2 g/dL), markedly ↑ reticulocytes (12 %), isolated ↑ indirect bilirubin (total 2.0, direct 0.5 mg/dL). These are the laboratory hallmarks of ongoing extravascular hemolysis.<br /></br>• Normal/low alkaline phosphatase and only mild AST/ALT bump → no acute cholecystitis or choledocholithiasis.<br /></br>Pattern recognition: Teen + mild anemia + splenomegaly + indirect hyperbilirubinemia + biliary colic = hereditary spherocytosis complicated by black pigment gallstones.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning  </p></br><p>Step 1 – Identify the underlying disorder: HS (defect in spectrin/ankyrin → spherocytes → splenic destruction).<br /></br>Step 2 – Recognize its two major long-term complications:<br /></br>  a. Pigment gallstones (from chronic hemolysis) → symptomatic here.<br /></br>  b. Progressive anemia/splenomegaly.<br /></br>Step 3 – Definitive therapy:<br /></br>  • Splenectomy abolishes hemolysis (no more splenic sequestration) → corrects anemia &amp; hyperbilirubinemia.<br /></br>  • Because gallstones are already present and will not clear with splenectomy alone, elective cholecystectomy is performed at the same operation to prevent recurrent biliary colic or acute cholecystitis.<br /></br>Therefore the single best next step is combined cholecystectomy + splenectomy (answer e).  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In hereditary spherocytosis, symptomatic gallstones plus evidence of hemolysis warrants simultaneous elective cholecystectomy and splenectomy once the child is ≥5 y.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)  </p></br><p>• Etiology: Autosomal dominant defects in RBC membrane proteins (spectrin &gt; ankyrin &gt; Band 3).<br /></br>• Mechanism: ↓ membrane surface area → spherocytes get trapped and destroyed in splenic cords.<br /></br>• Labs: ↑ MCHC, ↑ retics, ↑ indirect bilirubin, negative Coombs. Osmotic fragility and EMA binding tests confirm the diagnosis.<br /></br>• Complications: aplastic crises (parvovirus B19), hemolytic crises, pigment gallstones, folate deficiency, leg ulcers.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review  </p></br><ol></br><li>Folic acid supplementation for life (↑ RBC production).  </li></br><li>Splenectomy (after age 5 to allow immune maturation) = curative for hemolysis.<br /></br>   • Pre-op vaccines: PPSV23, PCV13, Hib, meningococcal.  </li></br><li>Cholecystectomy if gallstones present—ideally performed during the same surgery.  </li></br><li>No additional imaging is needed now; ultrasound already demonstrated (clinically) biliary colic. CT adds radiation and is less sensitive for pigment stones.  </li></br></ol></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“SPHERE” for Hereditary SPHERocytosis:<br /></br>S – Splenomegaly<br /></br>P – Pigment gallstones<br /></br>H – Hemolysis (↑ retics/↑ indirect bili)<br /></br>E – Elevated MCHC<br /></br>R – Reticulocytosis<br /></br>E – Elective splenectomy + cholecystectomy  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. CT scan of the abdomen<br /></br>   • Gallstones are best seen on ultrasound; CT misses most pigment stones and adds radiation. The patient already has a clear clinical picture—imaging would not change management.  </p></br><p>b. Oral ciprofloxacin for 1 week<br /></br>   • Treats bacterial enteritis/UTI, not hemolysis or gallstones. No infectious signs (afebrile, normal WBC).  </p></br><p>c. IV antibiotics + ERCP<br /></br>   • Appropriate for obstructive choledocholithiasis with cholangitis (fever, jaundice, ↑ ALP/Tbili) – absent here.  </p></br><p>d. IV antibiotics + cholecystectomy<br /></br>   • IV antibiotics indicate acute cholecystitis (Murphy sign, fever, leukocytosis), none of which are present. Also leaves the spleen in place, so hemolysis would persist.  </p></br><p>e. Cholecystectomy and splenectomy (Correct)<br /></br>   • Definitive, addresses both the source of hemolysis and the symptomatic gallstones.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line  </p></br><p>In a teenager with hereditary spherocytosis who develops symptomatic pigment gallstones, the next step is an elective, same-sitting cholecystectomy and splenectomy once appropriately vaccinated.</p>
53. A 17-year-old primigravid patient at 38 weeks’ gestation is admitted to the hospital in labor. Contractions have occurred for 6 hours every 2 to 3 minutes. Her pregnancy has been uncomplicated. A fetal heart tracing is shown. The cervix is 2 cm dilated and 90% effaced; the vertex is at 0 station. Which of the following is most consistent with these heart tracing findings?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Acidemia</br>b. Hypoxia</br>c. Labor intolerance</br>d. Umbilical cord compression</br>e. Normal labor</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Acidemia  </p></br><p>─────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Contractions every 2–3 min” tells you the tracing should show frequent uterine activity.<br /></br>• The question gives no other fetal data (no baseline rate, no variability) but still asks what the tracing “is most consistent with,” implying the picture shows a pathologic deceleration pattern the examinee is expected to recognize.<br /></br>• On NBME/Step 2 questions, the one pattern that is consistently linked to fetal ACIDEMIA is recurrent LATE decelerations (nadir after the peak of each contraction).<br /></br>• Variable decelerations → cord compression; Early decelerations → benign head compression; a normal, “Category I” tracing would never be described in terms of metabolic status.<br /></br>Bottom line: the only answer choice that matches recurrent late decelerations is fetal acidemia.</p></br><p>─────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify type of decel: “Late” = begins after contraction starts, nadir after contraction peak, returns to baseline after contraction ends.<br /></br>2. Associate pathophysiology: uteroplacental insufficiency → progressive fetal hypoxemia → anaerobic metabolism → lactic acid build-up → metabolic acidosis.<br /></br>3. Translate to answer list: acidemia is the downstream consequence we worry about → pick “Acidemia.”</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Recurrent late decelerations on fetal heart tracing = uteroplacental insufficiency &amp; fetal metabolic acidemia unless corrected.</p></br><p>─────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Uteroplacental insufficiency (e.g., maternal hypotension, tachysystole, preeclampsia, post-dates placenta) decreases fetal oxygen delivery during contractions.<br /></br>• The fetus switches to anaerobic metabolism → lactic acidosis → pH &lt; 7.2 (acidemia).<br /></br>• Late decels are therefore a surrogate marker of fetal metabolic compromise.</p></br><p>─────────────────</br>Management Pearls<br /></br>1. Immediate intra-uterine resuscitation: maternal left lateral positioning, O₂ via face mask, IV fluid bolus, stop oxytocin, treat maternal hypotension.<br /></br>2. Persistent category III tracing (recurrent late decels + absent variability) after interventions → expedited delivery (operative vaginal or emergent C-section).</p></br><p>─────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“VEAL CHOP” mnemonic:<br /></br>• Variable = Cord compression<br /></br>• Early = Head compression<br /></br>• Acceleration = Okay<br /></br>• Late = Placental insufficiency → Acidemia<br /></br>Visual: imagine the “Late” train arrives after the contraction peak and unloads a cargo of lactic acid.</p></br><p>─────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Hypoxia<br /></br>• Late decels do indicate hypoxemia, but NBME prefers the more severe metabolic consequence (acidemia). When both “hypoxia” and “acidemia” appear, pick acidemia for late decels.  </p></br><p>c. Labor intolerance<br /></br>• Non-standard term. Would imply maternal exhaustion or failure to progress, which isn’t diagnosed by fetal heart tracing.  </p></br><p>d. Umbilical cord compression<br /></br>• Produces VARIABLE decelerations: abrupt drop, V- or U-shaped, unrelated to contraction timing, often with “shoulders.” Not described/depicted here.  </p></br><p>e. Normal labor<br /></br>• A normal (Category I) tracing has baseline 110-160, moderate variability, no decels. Late decels are never “normal.”  </p></br><p>Key distinction: Only recurrent LATE decelerations forecast fetal metabolic acidemia requiring intervention.</p>
54. A 3-year-old girl is brought to the office by her mother 2 days after she suddenly stopped talking. The mother says her daughter has not spoken since returning from a weekend visit at her father’s house. Previously, she spoke in short sentences. The patient’s parents are divorced, and her father has a new girlfriend who has a 4-year-old son. The patient has no history of serious illness and receives no medications. In the examination room, she looks at books and plays alone quietly. Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. The patient looks in the direction of a dropped object that made a loud sound when it hit the ground. She nods or shakes her head in response to questions but does not answer open-ended questions.</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate initial step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. CT scan of the head</br>b. Interviewing the patient’s father</br>c. Psychological testing</br>d. Speech pathology evaluation</br>e. Reassurance that this is an age-appropriate phase</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. CT scan of the head  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues that point to an acquired neurologic deficit rather than a behavioral problem:<br /></br>• “Suddenly stopped talking” – acute onset, not a gradual or situational change.<br /></br>• “Previously, she spoke in short sentences” – establishes a baseline of normal language.<br /></br>• She nods/shakes her head appropriately and turns toward sound – hearing and language comprehension are intact; only verbal expression is absent.<br /></br>• No fever, no toxic appearance, normal vitals – rules against infectious encephalopathy.<br /></br>• Physical examination otherwise normal – but language production is missing, which in neurology = a focal deficit until proved otherwise.  </p></br><p>Pattern: Sudden loss of expressive language with preserved comprehension = classic for an acute Broca-area (dominant inferior frontal gyrus) lesion, most commonly ischemic or hemorrhagic stroke in the MCA territory, seizure with post-ictal aphasia, or less commonly an intracranial mass with hemorrhage. Any acute focal neurologic change in a child warrants emergent neuro-imaging.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)  </p></br><ol></br><li>Identify that the deficit is focal (speech only).  </li></br><li>Determine if onset is acute (yes → possible stroke/bleed).  </li></br><li>Before labeling a 3-year-old with a psychiatric diagnosis—or assuming family dynamics—first exclude life-threatening organic disease.  </li></br><li>The fastest readily available modality to rule out intracranial hemorrhage or large infarct is a non-contrast CT of the head.  </li></br></ol></br><p>Core takeaway: Always rule out an acute neurologic lesion with imaging when a child presents with a sudden, focal loss of function.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)  </p></br><p>• Broca (expressive) aphasia = lesion in dominant inferior frontal gyrus.<br /></br>• Etiologies in children: ischemic stroke (e.g., congenital heart disease, sickle cell, hypercoagulability), intracerebral hemorrhage (AVM, trauma), focal seizure with Todd paralysis/aphasia, brain tumor with acute bleed.<br /></br>• Comprehension spared → only expressive circuit affected.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Pearls  </p></br><ol></br><li></br><p>Imaging:<br /></br>   • First: Non-contrast CT head → rules in/out hemorrhage, large ischemia, mass with acute bleed.<br /></br>   • If CT negative but suspicion remains, follow with MRI/MRA.  </p></br></li></br><li></br><p>Management depends on findings:<br /></br>   • Ischemic stroke → thrombolysis (if criteria met), anticoagulation for venous sinus thrombosis, underlying cause work-up.<br /></br>   • Hemorrhage → neurosurgery, blood pressure control.<br /></br>   • Seizure → EEG, antiepileptics.  </p></br></li></br></ol></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“FAST rule for sudden speech loss”<br /></br>F = Face droop?<br /></br>A = Arm drift?<br /></br>S = Speech gone?<br /></br>T = Time → get a CT on time!<br /></br>Any one positive → image first.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>b. Interviewing the patient’s father<br /></br>• Possible psychosocial stress or abuse is important, but only after urgent organic causes are excluded. Delays potentially life-saving imaging.  </p></br><p>c. Psychological testing<br /></br>• Appropriate only after medical causes ruled out; psych testing is not emergent and would miss an acute stroke/bleed.  </p></br><p>d. Speech pathology evaluation<br /></br>• Speech therapy evaluates chronic language disorders, not acute loss. The therapist would also insist on neuro-imaging first.  </p></br><p>e. Reassurance that this is an age-appropriate phase<br /></br>• Normal “selective mutism” is situation-specific (e.g., only at school) and NOT sudden. Reassurance without work-up risks missing a catastrophic neurologic event.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Bottom Line  </p></br><p>Sudden, focal neurologic deficits—no matter how subtle—in any child require immediate brain imaging. Always prove it is NOT a stroke or intracranial bleed before considering psychiatric or developmental explanations.</p>
55. A 23-year-old man comes to the office because of a 4-week history of an itchy rash on his torso. He is otherwise asymptomatic. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Vital signs are within normal limits. A photograph of the torso is shown. Cardiopulmonary and abdominal examinations show no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Coal tar therapy</br>b. Fluconazole therapy</br>c. Methotrexate therapy</br>d. Penicillin therapy</br>e. Observation only</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Observation only  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><ol></br><li></br><p>Key phrases/clues<br /></br>   • 23-year-old otherwise healthy man<br /></br>   • 4-week history of “itchy” rash limited to torso<br /></br>   • Photograph (NBME usually shows multiple oval, salmon-colored plaques with fine peripheral scale aligned along Langer’s lines = “Christmas-tree” pattern)<br /></br>   • Normal vital signs and systemic examinations (no lymphadenopathy, no mucosal lesions, no palm/sole involvement)  </p></br></li></br><li></br><p>Pertinent positives<br /></br>   • Young adult, mild pruritus, truncal distribution, several weeks in duration → classic for pityriasis rosea (PR).<br /></br>   • Rash began with a single larger lesion (herald patch) followed 1–2 weeks later by many smaller lesions (picture).  </p></br></li></br><li></br><p>Pertinent negatives<br /></br>   • No palms/soles, no oral lesions → argues against secondary syphilis (would need RPR/VDRL + penicillin).<br /></br>   • No greasy scale on face/chest, no hypopigmented macules → argues against tinea versicolor (would need azole).<br /></br>   • No thick silvery scale on extensor surfaces/scalp, no nail pitting, arthritis → argues against psoriasis (coal tar, methotrexate).  </p></br></li></br></ol></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Stepwise)  </p></br><ol></br><li>Identify pattern: Herald patch → widespread oval lesions on trunk/“Christmas-tree” = pityriasis rosea.  </li></br><li>Natural history: Benign, self-limited; 80–90 % clear spontaneously within 6–8 weeks.  </li></br><li>Management: Reassurance ± topical antipruritics (emollients, low-potency steroid, oral antihistamine) if itching is troublesome. No antifungals, immunosuppressants, or antibiotics are needed.  </li></br><li>Therefore the most appropriate next step = Observation only.  </li></br></ol></br><p>Core takeaway: Pityriasis rosea = self-limited exanthem of adolescents/young adults → mainstay is reassurance.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>• Etiology: Likely viral reactivation (HHV-6/7).<br /></br>• Sequence: Herald patch (2–5 cm), then diffuse eruption along skin tension lines.<br /></br>• Course: Resolves without scarring; recurrence rare.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests  </p></br><p>First-line<br /></br>• None required; reassure.<br /></br>Symptomatic relief<br /></br>• Topical emollients, low-potency corticosteroids, oral antihistamines, narrow-band UVB if severe itching.<br /></br>Diagnostics<br /></br>• None unless atypical features (palms/soles, mucosa, systemic symptoms) → then rule out syphilis with RPR.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“Rosea = rosy Christmas tree that drops its needles after the holidays.”<br /></br>• Herald patch = star on top.<br /></br>• Tree disappears in ~6 weeks (self-limited).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Coal tar therapy<br /></br>   • Used for chronic plaque psoriasis or atopic dermatitis. Psoriasis shows well-demarcated erythematous plaques with thick silvery scale on extensor surfaces, not the fine collarette scale of PR.  </p></br><p>b. Fluconazole therapy<br /></br>   • Systemic azole for tinea versicolor or candidiasis. Tinea versicolor shows hypo/hyper-pigmented macules with fine scale, KOH: “spaghetti &amp; meatballs.” Not the presentation here.  </p></br><p>c. Methotrexate therapy<br /></br>   • Systemic immunosuppressant for severe psoriasis, psoriatic arthritis, or refractory eczema. Over-treatment for a benign, self-limited exanthem.  </p></br><p>d. Penicillin therapy<br /></br>   • Benzathine penicillin G is first-line for secondary syphilis (maculopapular rash including palms &amp; soles + mucous patches, condyloma lata). Those features are absent; RPR not indicated unless atypical.  </p></br><p>e. Observation only<br /></br>   • Matches natural history of pityriasis rosea → correct.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line: In a healthy young adult with a herald patch followed by a pruritic “Christmas-tree” truncal eruption, pityriasis rosea is diagnosed clinically and resolves spontaneously; provide reassurance and symptomatic care only.</p>
56. An 82-year-old woman is brought to the physician by her daughter because she has not had a bowel movement for 2 weeks and has had loss of appetite for 3 days. There is no history of rectal bleeding or change in stool color or caliber. One year ago, test of the stool for occult blood was negative. Examination shows a large, exophytic, cauliflower-like lesion between the vagina and rectum. Digital rectal examination shows fecal impaction. A biopsy specimen of the lesion is most likely to show which of the following?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Ependymoma</br>b. Hyperplastic dystrophy</br>c. Lichen sclerosus</br>d. Signet-ring cells</br>e. Squamous cell carcinoma</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>e. Squamous cell carcinoma</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Location: “Between the vagina and rectum” = posterior fourchette/perineal‐vulvar region.<br /></br>• Morphology: “Large, exophytic, cauliflower-like lesion” → classic buzz-phrase for an invasive squamous neoplasm (same descriptor used for HPV-related lesions of cervix, vulva, anus, larynx).<br /></br>• Patient profile: 82-year-old post-menopausal woman. The most common malignancy of the vulva/perineum in elderly women is keratinizing squamous cell carcinoma, often arising on a background of chronic inflammatory dermatoses (e.g., lichen sclerosus).<br /></br>• No systemic or GI “signet-ring” symptoms; lesion is external, not CNS (rules out ependymoma).  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1: An elderly woman with a solitary, bulky, verrucous mass on the vulva/perineum → think squamous malignancy.<br /></br>Step 2: Rule out distractors that do not produce cauliflower-like masses in this anatomic site (CNS tumor, benign dystrophies, mucinous adenocarcinoma).<br /></br>Core takeaway: A warty, exophytic mass on anogenital skin = squamous cell carcinoma until proven otherwise.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>Two pathways to vulvar SCC:</br>1. HPV-related (types 16, 18, 31, 33) → younger patients, basaloid/warty SCC.<br /></br>2. Non-HPV, chronic inflammation (lichen sclerosus, lichen simplex chronicus) → elderly, keratinizing SCC (this case).<br /></br>Histology: Invasive nests/cords of atypical keratinizing squamous cells ± keratin pearls, intercellular bridges.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests</br>• Diagnosis: Punch or excisional biopsy (already planned in vignette).<br /></br>• Staging: Pelvic MRI or CT, possible sentinel lymph node biopsy (inguinofemoral nodes).<br /></br>• Management: Wide local excision or radical vulvectomy ± lymphadenectomy; consider radiation/chemo for nodal or bulky disease.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“Squishy, scaly, cauliflower = Squamous.”<br /></br>Older vulva + lichen sclerosus history → think keratinizing SCC.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Ependymoma – Tumor of ependymal cells lining ventricles/spinal canal; presents with hydrocephalus or cord compression, not a perineal mass.<br /></br>b. Hyperplastic dystrophy (lichen simplex chronicus) – Thickened pruritic white plaque; flat, not exophytic; shows acanthosis and hyperkeratosis without cellular atypia.<br /></br>c. Lichen sclerosus – Thin, parchment-like white patches; atrophy, dermal homogenization, no malignant cells (although a risk factor for SCC).<br /></br>d. Signet-ring cells – Mucin-laden adenocarcinoma (e.g., gastric, lobular breast, Krukenberg ovarian metastasis); histology and site do not match an exophytic vulvar lesion.  </p></br><p>Key Pitfall</br>Students often pick lichen sclerosus because it is common in elderly women, but remember: LS itself is flat, ivory-white, and pruritic. Once the lesion becomes large, raised, and “cauliflower-like,” malignant transformation to squamous cell carcinoma has likely occurred.</p>
57. An 80-year-old woman with hypertension and dementia, Alzheimer type, is brought to the emergency department by paramedics from the nursing care facility where she resides because of a 1-day history of vaginal bleeding. The patient is nonverbal. The staff member who accompanied the patient reports that the patient fell 2 nights ago while moving from the shower chair to the bathtub. The bleeding was noted today while a nurse’s aide changed the patient’s absorbent pad this morning. Her medications are donepezil, amlodipine, and aspirin. She is 163 cm (5 ft 4 in) tall and weighs 48 kg, or 105lb; BMI is 18 kg/m2. Vital signs and oxygen saturation are within normal limits. Examination shows ecchymoses over the upper extremities and thighs; the ecchymoses are more severe on the right thigh than the left. Genitourinary examination shows ecchymoses over the labia majora and torn labia minora at the 7 o’clock position with minimal bleeding. Her hemoglobin concentration is 12 g/dL and platelet count is 150,000/mm3. Which of the following is the most likely explanation for these findings?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Elder abuse</br>b. Lichen sclerosus</br>c. Straddle injury</br>d. Vaginal cancer</br>e. von Willebrand disease</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>A. Elder abuse (sexual/physical)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Non-verbal, cognitively impaired elder in a nursing facility → high-risk group for abuse.<br /></br>• New vaginal bleeding discovered by staff; no prior gynecologic complaints.<br /></br>• Physical findings</br>  – Ecchymoses on multiple extremities in various stages, worse on one thigh → patterned, non-cohesive with a single mechanical fall.<br /></br>  – Genital trauma: torn labia minora at the 7-o’clock position (posterior vestibule) + surrounding bruising. This site is classic for penetrative/sexual trauma, not for accidental “straddle” or “slip-in-the-tub” injuries, which typically involve the anterior vulva.<br /></br>• Normal Hgb, platelet count, and only aspirin as an antiplatelet → bleeding is due to tissue injury, not a coagulopathy.<br /></br>• The history supplied by caretakers (“she fell two nights ago”) does not plausibly explain deep posterior genital lacerations today.</p></br><p>Key pattern = cognitively impaired elder + inconsistent injury pattern + genital laceration → think abuse.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify true bleeding source: lacerated labia minora, minimal bleeding now.<br /></br>2. Ask “What mechanism causes posterior vestibular tears?” → forced spreading/penetration.<br /></br>3. Evaluate provided mechanism (simple bathroom fall). A fall produces anterior vulvar/inner-thigh bruises, not posterior vestibular tears.<br /></br>4. Multiple bruises in different locations &amp; severity further contradict a single fall.<br /></br>5. Therefore, most consistent explanation = elder (sexual) abuse.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Genital injury in a vulnerable elder that cannot be explained by the reported mechanism mandates suspicion for abuse.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Elder abuse may be physical, psychological, financial, or sexual. Risk factors: advanced age, dependency, dementia, caregiver stress, institutionalization. Sexual abuse produces:</br>• Tears of labia minora/posterior fourchette/hymen</br>• Perineal ecchymoses</br>• Frequently minimal active bleeding but significant bruising.</p></br><p>Clinicians are mandatory reporters; proper management involves ensuring patient safety, forensic evidence collection, social work involvement, and notification of Adult Protective Services.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostics &amp; Management<br /></br>1. Stabilize &amp; treat injuries (e.g., topical hemostasis, suturing if needed).<br /></br>2. Forensic sexual assault kit if &lt;72 h or as jurisdiction dictates.<br /></br>3. Full skin survey &amp; dating of bruises, photography.<br /></br>4. Labs only if clinically indicated (CBC, coags in heavy bleeding).<br /></br>5. Notify APS/law enforcement; interdisciplinary elder-abuse team.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“BACK 7” for elder sexual abuse clues:<br /></br>B – Bruises in various stages<br /></br>A – Anatomic site inconsistent with fall (posterior vestibule)<br /></br>C – Cognitively impaired/Communicatively limited patient<br /></br>K – Known caregiver explanation doesn’t fit<br /></br>7 – Tears at 3, 6, or 7-o’clock positions of hymen/labia</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>B. Lichen sclerosus<br /></br>• Chronic inflammatory dermatosis → pruritic, thin white “parchment” plaques, chronic vulvar discomfort, not an acute tear or ecchymoses.<br /></br>• Would not cause isolated posterior labial laceration or multiple fresh bruises.</p></br><p>C. Straddle injury<br /></br>• Occurs when a person lands with legs astride an object (e.g., bicycle bar).<br /></br>• Typically injures anterior labia majora/minora or clitoral hood, spares posterior fourchette/hymen.<br /></br>• Explanation requires credible straddle mechanism; a bathroom slip does not generate a focused posterior laceration at 7-o’clock.</p></br><p>D. Vaginal cancer<br /></br>• Presents with chronic spotting, malodorous discharge, or mass, not acute laceration with minimal bleeding.<br /></br>• No mass, friable lesion, or chronic symptoms described.</p></br><p>E. von Willebrand disease<br /></br>• Lifelong bleeding tendency: menorrhagia, epistaxis, surgical bleeding.<br /></br>• Would cause prolonged bleeding, petechiae, and low vWF activity—not a discrete posterior tear with otherwise normal hemostasis (Hgb 12 g/dL, normal platelets).<br /></br>• Aspirin use alone does not create deep genital lacerations.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line: In any vulnerable elder, genital injury without a plausible accidental mechanism should immediately raise suspicion for sexual abuse and prompt mandatory protective actions.</p>
58. A 67-year-old man comes to the physician for a second measurement of his blood pressure. He had a blood pressure of 165/105 mm Hg in his right arm 9 days ago. He takes no medications. He has smoked two packs of cigarettes daily for 35 years. Today, his blood pressure is 160/100 mm Hg in the right arm and 105/65 mm Hg in the left arm. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most likely cause of these blood pressure findings?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Coarctation of the aorta</br>b. Error during auscultation of blood pressure</br>c. Pheochromocytoma</br>d. Subclavian artery atherosclerosis</br>e. White-coat hypertension</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Coarctation of the aorta  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues<br /></br>• Repeatedly elevated right-arm BP (≈160/100 mm Hg) → true systemic hypertension in the proximal aorta.<br /></br>• Markedly lower left-arm BP (≈105/65 mm Hg) → flow‐limiting lesion somewhere between the origins of the right brachiocephalic trunk and the left subclavian artery.<br /></br>• No mention of arm claudication, neurologic “steal” symptoms, bruits, or diminished/unequal pulses that often accompany large-vessel atherosclerotic disease.<br /></br>• Long-standing, otherwise asymptomatic patient → suggests a congenital lesion that has been present for decades rather than an acute or subacute atherosclerotic obstruction.  </p></br><p>Pattern recognition<br /></br>Arm-to-arm systolic difference &gt;15–20 mm Hg in an otherwise healthy individual, with the higher pressure on the right, classically points to a coarctation located between the right brachiocephalic trunk and the left subclavian artery (so-called “proximal” or pre-subclavian coarctation).  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)  </p></br><ol></br><li>Localize the gradient: BP comparison localizes the obstruction to somewhere after the right common carotid/right subclavian branches but before the left subclavian branch.  </li></br><li>Consider etiologies at that location:<br /></br>   • Congenital narrowing (coarctation).<br /></br>   • Acquired focal obstruction (large‐artery atherosclerosis, arteritis, dissection).  </li></br><li>Compare epidemiology &amp; chronicity:<br /></br>   • A 67-y smoker certainly can have subclavian atherosclerosis, but that almost always produces bruits and arm claudication and is far more common distal (within) the subclavian artery itself, not across the transverse aortic arch.<br /></br>   • Coarctation is congenital, frequently missed in childhood if proximal, and presents later as HTN in the right arm with lower pressure beyond the narrowing.  </li></br><li>Choose the lesion that best explains a decades‐long, silent gradient without other vascular symptoms → coarctation.  </li></br></ol></br><p>Core takeaway:<br /></br>Adult (“late-presenting”) coarctation can lie proximal to the left subclavian, producing higher right-arm pressure and lower left-arm pressure. Always think of coarctation when you see an unexplained, fixed &gt;20 mm Hg systolic difference between the arms.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>• Coarctation = congenital narrowing of the aorta, most commonly just distal to the ligamentum arteriosum (“juxtaductal”), but variants can occur anywhere along the arch.<br /></br>• Proximal (pre-subclavian) variant:<br /></br>  – Blood destined for right arm leaves before the narrowing → normal/high BP.<br /></br>  – Blood for left arm must cross the narrowed segment → significant pressure drop.<br /></br>• Long-standing proximal coarctation leads to: systemic HTN (proximal), collateral development (internal mammary, intercostals), and relative hypotension/pulse delay distal to the lesion.<br /></br>• Associated conditions: bicuspid aortic valve, cerebral berry aneurysms, Turner syndrome.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Diagnostics &amp; Management  </p></br><p>Diagnostics (confirmatory):<br /></br>1. Four-extremity BP + pulse exam (delayed left radial &amp; femoral pulses).<br /></br>2. Echocardiography or CT/MR angiography of the aortic arch (gold-standard delineation).<br /></br>3. CXR may show “figure-3” sign (indentation + post-stenotic dilation) and rib notching (collaterals), but these are less sensitive for proximal lesions.  </p></br><p>Management:<br /></br>• Definitive: Surgical resection with end-to-end anastomosis or aortic stenting.<br /></br>• Medical (pre- or post-repair): Beta-blockers or ACE inhibitors to control proximal HTN.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“Right-wins, Left-loses → suspect Pre-Subclavian Coarctation.”<br /></br>Mnemonic: “CoARCtation: Arm Right Compresses (higher)”.<br /></br>Visualization: Picture the aortic arch as a highway—right exit open, left exit behind a traffic cone (coarctation) causing a backup (high pressure) before the cone and slow flow after it.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>b. Error during auscultation of BP<br /></br>• Would not reproducibly create a &gt;50 mm Hg gradient in two separate visits, and errors rarely affect systolic AND diastolic readings so consistently in only one arm.  </p></br><p>c. Pheochromocytoma<br /></br>• Causes episodic or sustained systemic HTN but affects BOTH arms equally; no fixed inter-arm gradient.  </p></br><p>d. Subclavian artery atherosclerosis<br /></br>• Very common in heavy smokers; usually located in the proximal subclavian itself, creating a gradient BETWEEN the subclavian &amp; the distal arm (often with arm claudication, bruits, neurologic “steal” during exertion).<br /></br>• Would not elevate the right-arm pressure; it simply lowers the left-arm pressure. You’d expect near-normal systemic BP with unilateral reduction, not truly hypertensive readings on the right.  </p></br><p>e. White-coat hypertension<br /></br>• Explains transient global elevation, not a large, fixed arm-to-arm difference.  </p></br><p>Key discriminator: Only coarctation explains both (1) persistently high right-arm pressure and (2) chronically lower left-arm pressure without other arm ischemic symptoms.</p>
59. An 18-month-old boy from Afghanistan, who immigrated to the USA with his family 2 days ago, is brought to the emergency department by his mother because of a 6-hour history of fever and difficulty breathing. The mother says he previously was doing well. Medical history is unremarkable, and he has not had contact with anyone known to be ill. He receives no medications, and immunization status is unknown. He is sitting on his mother’s lap, leaning forward, and drooling. He appears to be in significant respiratory distress. Vital signs are temperature 39.0 °C (102.2 °F), pulse 120/min, respirations 40/min, and blood pressure is 80/50 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 95%. Physical examination discloses stridor heard without auscultation. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Bronchoscopy</br>b. CT scan of the neck</br>c. Indirect laryngoscopy</br>d. Lateral x-ray of the neck</br>e. Transfer of patient to the operating room</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Transfer of patient to the operating room (for controlled airway stabilization and possible endotracheal intubation)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age (18 mo) + acute onset (6 h) of fever, inspiratory stridor, drooling, sitting forward (tripod) → classic “thumb-print” clinical picture of acute epiglottitis.<br /></br>• Recent immigration, unknown vaccinations → likely unimmunized against Haemophilus influenzae type b (Hib), the historical #1 cause.<br /></br>• Stridor audible without a stethoscope + toxic appearance = impending upper-airway obstruction.<br /></br>• O2 sat still 95 % → obstruction can worsen suddenly; do NOT delay airway control.<br /></br>Pertinent negative: no barky cough (croup) and no wheezing (lower airway).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Recognize likely epiglottitis.<br /></br>Step 2: In epiglottitis the greatest danger is sudden, complete airway obstruction precipitated by agitation or by blind instrumentation.<br /></br>Step 3: Therefore, move the child to a setting where a controlled, team-based airway intervention (ideally inhalational induction + intubation by anesthesiologist/ENT) can be performed immediately—i.e., the operating room.</p></br><p>Take-home: Suspected epiglottitis = “Do nothing that upsets the child; take them directly to OR for airway.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Etiology: Hib (↓ incidence in vaccinated populations), S. pyogenes, S. aureus, nontypeable H. influenzae.<br /></br>• Pathogenesis: Bacterial invasion → cellulitis of supraglottic structures → rapidly progressive edema → dynamic airway obstruction.<br /></br>• Clinical triad: High fever, “3 D’s” (Dysphagia, Drooling, Distress) + stridor, tripod posture.<br /></br>• Can progress to complete obstruction → sudden death.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>1. Airway first: Controlled endotracheal intubation in OR; if unable → surgical airway (tracheostomy).<br /></br>2. Once airway secured: Empiric IV antibiotics (e.g., ceftriaxone + vancomycin).<br /></br>3. Adjuncts: IV dexamethasone to reduce edema.<br /></br>Diagnostics (thumb-print sign on lateral neck radiograph, laryngoscopy) are performed ONLY after airway is safe.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Epi-GLOTT-it-is = ‘GLOTTa keep the AIRway’:<br /></br>G—Get to the OR<br /></br>L—Leave the kid calm (no poking)<br /></br>O—Observe for sudden obstruction<br /></br>T—Thumb-print sign (seen AFTER airway)<br /></br>T—Treat with Third-gen ceph + steroids”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Bronchoscopy<br /></br>• Requires instrumentation of the airway outside a controlled setting; can precipitate complete obstruction. Appropriate only after airway secured, usually in OR with ENT—NOT first step in ED.</p></br><p>b. CT scan of the neck<br /></br>• Supine imaging can worsen obstruction; delays airway control; radiation unnecessary for diagnosis.</p></br><p>c. Indirect laryngoscopy<br /></br>• Mirrors/fiberoptic exam in an awake, distressed toddler can provoke laryngospasm &amp; arrest. Done after intubation or under anesthesia.</p></br><p>d. Lateral x-ray of the neck<br /></br>• Classic “thumb-print” sign, but obtaining film forces child to lie supine &amp; cry → dangerous. Diagnostic studies are secondary to securing airway.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Key Message<br /></br>In suspected epiglottitis, never delay; secure the airway in a controlled environment (OR) before any diagnostic procedure.</p>
60. A 57-year-old man with pancreatic cancer is admitted to the hospital because of a 5-day history of moderate epigastric pain that has not responded to oral hydromorphone therapy. On admission, intravenous hydromorphone is begun. The nurse calls to report that 3 hours ago, she mistakenly administered 5 mg of hydromorphone instead of the prescribed 0.5 mg. The patient is sleeping comfortably and is easily aroused. His respirations are 12/min, and blood pressure is 120/78 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 98%. Examination shows no other abnormalities. In addition to reporting the incident to the patient safety committee, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Administer naloxone and transfer the patient to the intensive care unit</br>b. Apply a fentanyl patch</br>c. Begin oxygen therapy and transfer the patient to a telemetry unit</br>d. Discontinue hydromorphone therapy for 24 hours</br>e. No additional management is indicated at this time</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. No additional management is indicated at this time  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “5-day history of pain … on oral hydromorphone → switched to IV” tells us the patient is opioid-tolerant, not opioid-naïve.<br /></br>• Medication error: 5 mg IV instead of 0.5 mg IV (10-fold dose).<br /></br>• Current clinical status 3 h after error:<br /></br>  – Easily arousable (not obtunded/comatose)<br /></br>  – Respiratory rate 12/min (lower end of normal, but ≥12)<br /></br>  – O2 saturation 98 % on room air (no hypoxemia)<br /></br>  – Normal blood pressure, no other abnormalities  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives: awake on stimulation, adequate ventilation/oxygenation.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no hypoventilation (RR &lt; 8–10), no hypoxemia, no pinpoint pupils causing LOC, no hypotension. These facts rule out clinically significant opioid toxicity that mandates reversal.  </p></br><p>Pattern to recognize: “Opioid overdose” decisions hinge on clinical effect (especially ventilation), not on ingested dose alone.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Ask: Is there life-threatening opioid toxicity? Key markers = RR &lt; 8–10, apnea, hypoxemia, unarousable sedation, or hemodynamic instability.<br /></br>2. If YES → give naloxone, secure airway, possible ICU.<br /></br>3. If NO → simply observe; adding naloxone may precipitate severe pain, withdrawal, ↑ sympathetic drive.  </p></br><p>In this vignette, the patient is stable → observation is safest.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Treat the patient, not the number (dose). Naloxone is reserved for clinically significant respiratory or CNS depression, not for every documented overdose.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Opioids (e.g., hydromorphone) are μ-receptor agonists. They:<br /></br>• Decrease medullary respiratory center sensitivity to CO₂ → ↓ respiratory rate &amp; tidal volume.<br /></br>• Cause CNS depression, miosis, ↓ sympathetic tone.<br /></br>Tolerance develops to analgesia and some side-effects but less so to constipation &amp; miosis; tolerance to respiratory depression develops but is incomplete. Hence opioid-tolerant patients can handle higher doses without apnea.  </p></br><p>Hydromorphone IV half-life ≈ 2–3 h; maximal respiratory depression occurs 5–10 min after IV push and lasts 1–3 h. The patient is already 3 h out and stable → major toxicity unlikely to develop later.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Monitoring Pearls<br /></br>• Observe on regular ward with spot pulse-oximetry.<br /></br>• Reassess vitals &amp; mental status every 1–2 h until drug effect clearly waning.<br /></br>• Naloxone: 0.04–0.4 mg IV titrated to RR ≥12; repeat PRN. Duration 30–90 min, so continuous infusion needed if long-acting opioids were used.<br /></br>• In an opioid-tolerant cancer patient, naloxone can trigger acute pain crisis &amp; withdrawal → avoid unless absolutely necessary.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“RR 8, Sat 88, Pupils 8-pinpoint” → Give NAL-8-one.<br /></br>Mnemonic: “8’s decide the fate”<br /></br>• RR &lt;8/min<br /></br>• O₂ sat &lt;88 %<br /></br>• Unresponsive (GCS ~8)<br /></br>Any one → naloxone. Otherwise, watch &amp; wait.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Administer naloxone &amp; ICU transfer<br /></br>   – Indicated only if respiratory or CNS depression. Would unnecessarily reverse analgesia, precipitate withdrawal, and require escalation of care.  </p></br><p>b. Apply a fentanyl patch<br /></br>   – Adds another potent opioid; not a remedy for an overdose.  </p></br><p>c. Begin oxygen &amp; telemetry transfer<br /></br>   – Oxygen isn’t needed with normal saturation. Telemetry gives no benefit for isolated opioid dosing error without cardiorespiratory compromise.  </p></br><p>d. Discontinue hydromorphone for 24 h<br /></br>   – Would leave a patient with advanced cancer in pain; unnecessary since current dose is wearing off, and future dosing can be corrected.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In an opioid-tolerant patient who is awake, breathing adequately, and normoxic after an inadvertent high dose of IV hydromorphone, no active reversal or escalation of care is required—continued observation is sufficient.</p>
61. A 16-month-old boy is brought to the physician by his mother for a follow-up examination. One month ago, he completed a course of antibiotic therapy for an ear infection. His mother reports he has a 2-day history of irritability, decreased appetite, and difficulty sleeping. He has been hospitalized for pneumonia twice during the past 9 months. He appears irritable. His temperature is 38.7°C (101.6°F), pulse is 96/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 92/54 mm Hg. Skin examination shows no abnormalities. Examination of the right ear shows an erythematous, bulging tympanic membrane. Laboratory studies show a leukocyte count of 16,400/mm³ (74% segmented neutrophils, 17% lymphocytes, and 9% monocytes). Additional serum studies are most likely to show which of the following?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Complement C1q inhibitor deficiency</br>b. Decreased B-lymphocyte concentration</br>c. Increased IgM antibody concentration</br>d. Increased polyclonal IgE concentration</br>e. Increased T-lymphocyte concentration</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Decreased B-lymphocyte concentration  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 16-month-old MALE who was healthy until ~7–8 months of age (when trans-placental maternal IgG is gone).<br /></br>• Multiple, recurrent PYOGENIC upper- and lower-respiratory infections (otitis media, two hospital admissions for pneumonia) with otherwise normal skin exam and growth.<br /></br>• No viral, fungal, or opportunistic infections are mentioned; no chronic diarrhea, eczema, mucocutaneous candidiasis, or angioedema.<br /></br>• Neutrophilic leukocytosis (typical response to extracellular pyogenic bacteria).  </p></br><p>Pattern: “Boy + onset after 6 mo + recurrent sinopulmonary bacterial infections + normal T-cell function” → think X-linked (Bruton) agammaglobulinemia, a pure humoral (B-cell) defect.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Maternal IgG protects infants until ≈6 months → first infections start afterward.<br /></br>2. Pyogenic organisms (Strep pneumo, H. influenzae, etc.) are normally cleared by antibody-mediated opsonization; absent B cells → recurrent otitis, pneumonia.<br /></br>3. T-cell immunity is intact → no opportunistic/fungal/viral infections.<br /></br>4. Serum test that confirms XLA: profoundly ↓ or absent CD19⁺/CD20⁺ B-cell count and pan-hypogammaglobulinemia (IgG, IgA, IgM all low).  </p></br><p>Therefore, a decreased B-lymphocyte (CD19⁺) concentration is the most likely additional finding.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Recurrent encapsulated-bacterial infections beginning after maternal antibody wanes = humoral deficiency; in a boy, X-linked agammaglobulinemia → absent B cells.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Gene defect: Bruton tyrosine kinase (BTK) on X-chromosome → failure of pre-B cells to mature.<br /></br>• Result:<br /></br>  – Near-absent peripheral B cells and germinal centers<br /></br>  – Very low levels of all immunoglobulin isotypes<br /></br>• Clinical: Recurrent sinopulmonary infections, Giardia lamblia diarrhea, no palatine tonsils or lymph nodes on exam (lymphoid tissue hypoplasia).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Diagnosis:<br /></br>• Flow cytometry: CD19/CD20 B cells &lt;1% of lymphocytes<br /></br>• Quantitative immunoglobulins: IgG, IgA, IgM all ↓  </p></br><p>Management:<br /></br>• Lifelong IVIG (intravenous immunoglobulin) every 3–4 weeks<br /></br>• Prompt antibiotics for infections<br /></br>• Avoid live vaccines  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Boys with Bruton's lack B cells.”<br /></br>• B-cell problems begin after “Bottled” maternal IgG is gone (≈6 months).<br /></br>Visual: Picture an empty “B-cell-tank” in a 6-month-old boy’s body armor labeled “BTK.”  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Complement C1q inhibitor deficiency<br /></br>• Hereditary angioedema: episodic, non-infectious swelling of face, airway, GI tract; NORMAL infection risk. Not recurrent otitis/pneumonia.  </p></br><p>c. Increased IgM antibody concentration (Hyper-IgM syndrome)<br /></br>• Class-switch defect (CD40L, AID). Presents EARLIER (first few months) with severe opportunistic (Pneumocystis, Cryptosporidium) plus pyogenic infections, neutropenia, oral ulcers. IgM ↑ but IgG/IgA ↓. Opportunistic infections absent here.  </p></br><p>d. Increased polyclonal IgE concentration (Hyper-IgE / Job)<br /></br>• Triad: eczema, “cold” staphylococcal abscesses, coarse facies, retained primary teeth, bone fractures. None of these findings present.  </p></br><p>e. Increased T-lymphocyte concentration<br /></br>• Many T-cell disorders (e.g., DiGeorge) actually have ↓ T cells; having ↑ T cells is nonspecific and would not explain recurrent pyogenic bacterial infections without opportunists.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Key Point: Male infant with recurrent otitis/pneumonia after 6 months → check B-cell count; ↓ B cells confirms X-linked agammaglobulinemia.</p>
62. A 27-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 3-day history of fever and bloody diarrhea; episodes of diarrhea are preceded by abdominal pain. He has had no weight loss or rash. He takes no medications. His temperature is 37.8°C (100°F), pulse is 88/min, and blood pressure is 124/72 mm Hg. Abdominal examination shows diffuse tenderness with no rebound or guarding; bowel sounds are increased. Rectal examination shows no abnormalities; there is no blood or stool in the rectal vault. Which of the following is the most likely causal organism?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Adenovirus</br>b. Campylobacter jejuni</br>c. Giardia lamblia</br>d. Norwalk virus</br>e. Toxigenic Escherichia coli</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Campylobacter jejuni  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Bloody diarrhea + fever = inflammatory/invasive organism.<br /></br>• “Abdominal pain that precedes diarrhea” is classic for Campylobacter (can mimic appendicitis).<br /></br>• No chronic symptoms (weight loss, rash) → argues against inflammatory bowel disease.<br /></br>• Physical exam: diffuse tenderness but no peritoneal signs → consistent with acute infectious colitis.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Differentiate watery vs inflammatory diarrhea.<br /></br>‐ Watery, afebrile → viruses, ETEC, Giardia.<br /></br>‐ Bloody ± fever → Campylobacter, Shigella, Salmonella, EHEC.<br /></br>Step 2: Which of those choices are invasive? Only Campylobacter (and possibly EHEC, but “toxigenic E coli” in exam lingo = ETEC, watery).<br /></br>Step 3: Classic clue of pain before diarrhea seals the diagnosis.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Campylobacter jejuni is the most common bacterial cause of bloody diarrhea in the USA and often presents with fever and crampy abdominal pain that initially mimics surgical abdomen.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Gram-negative, curved, oxidase-positive rod; grows at 42 °C (“camp fire”).<br /></br>• Reservoirs: under-cooked poultry, unpasteurized milk, contaminated water, infected pets.<br /></br>• Invades intestinal mucosa → neutrophil-rich inflammation → blood &amp; pus in stool.<br /></br>• Post-infectious complications: Guillain-Barré syndrome, reactive arthritis, erythema nodosum.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>• Diagnosis: stool culture or PCR.<br /></br>• Usually self-limited (3–7 days); rehydrate.<br /></br>• Give azithromycin (or fluoroquinolone) for severe disease, high-risk patients, or to shorten illness.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Bloody Campfire”: Bloody diarrhea + fever from a curved rod that likes the heat of a camp-fire (42 °C) = Campylobacter.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Adenovirus – Viral gastroenteritis in young children; watery diarrhea, vomiting, little/no blood or fever.<br /></br>c. Giardia lamblia – Foul-smelling, greasy (steatorrhea) stools after camping or daycare; typically afebrile, not bloody.<br /></br>d. Norwalk (Norovirus) – Explosive, watery diarrhea and vomiting; common on cruise ships; no blood, minimal fever.<br /></br>e. Toxigenic Escherichia coli – NBME uses “toxigenic” to describe ETEC (heat-labile/heat-stable toxins) → traveler’s watery diarrhea, afebrile. Hemorrhagic E coli (EHEC O157:H7) would give bloody diarrhea but is usually specified as “enterohemorrhagic” and often lacks fever.  </p></br><p>Key Difference: Only Campylobacter among the listed choices causes an acute, febrile, inflammatory (bloody) diarrhea.</p>
63. A 15-year-old boy is brought to the emergency department after football practice because of a 45-minute history of bright red blood in his urine. He has no history of serious illness and receives no medications. His pulse is 115/min, and blood pressure is 119/73 mm Hg; other vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows no ecchymoses, suprapubic tenderness, or abdominal distention; there is moderate tenderness to palpation of the left flank. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Measurement of prothrombin time and partial thromboplastin time</br>b. Measurement of serum C3 and C4 concentrations</br>c. Streptozyme test</br>d. Urine culture</br>e. Abdominal CT scan</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Abdominal CT scan (with IV contrast)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “45-minute history of bright-red blood in his urine” = acute gross hematuria.<br /></br>• “After football practice … moderate tenderness to palpation of the left flank” = recent blunt flank trauma (helmet, fall).<br /></br>• “Pulse 115/min, BP 119/73” = mildly tachycardic but normotensive → hemodynamically stable.<br /></br>Key pattern: Stable patient + gross hematuria + costovertebral/flank tenderness immediately after contact sport → think renal parenchymal or collecting-system injury.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Determine if this is traumatic or medical hematuria.<br /></br> • Timing (immediately after practice) and flank tenderness strongly favor trauma rather than glomerular disease, infection, or coagulopathy.  </p></br><p>Step 2: Decide on imaging modality based on hemodynamics.<br /></br> • Hemodynamically stable with suspected renal trauma → abdominal CT with IV contrast is the gold-standard test; it grades injury, guides operative vs non-operative management.<br /></br> • If unstable, one would proceed directly to the OR with a one-shot IV pyelogram or FAST scan.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In stable trauma patients with gross hematuria, contrast CT scan is the essential first diagnostic study.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Blunt force to the flank can cause renal contusion, laceration, collecting-system disruption, or vascular pedicle injury. Hematuria results from bleeding into the collecting system. Severity correlates poorly with the amount of hematuria; therefore imaging—not hematuria volume—determines management.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Management &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• CT with IV contrast: assesses renal parenchyma, collecting system, vascular injury, and peri-renal hematoma.<br /></br>• Grading (AAST I–V) then dictates observation vs angioembolization vs surgery.<br /></br>• Labs: baseline hemoglobin, type &amp; screen, but they do not replace imaging.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Stable + Blood in urine after a Blow = CT to Know.”<br /></br>Remember:<br /></br>Stable trauma → CT.<br /></br>Unstable trauma → OR/IVP.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. PT/INR &amp; PTT – Coagulopathy causes painless, often microscopic hematuria without localized flank tenderness shortly after contact activity. No history of bleeding tendency or meds (e.g., anticoagulants).  </p></br><p>b. Serum C3/C4 – Low complement suggests immune complex GN (e.g., post-streptococcal, lupus). These present with smoky/tea-colored urine, edema, hypertension, not abrupt red urine post-trauma.  </p></br><p>c. Streptozyme test – Screens for recent group A strep infection (ASO, anti-DNase B) in post-streptococcal GN. Same reasoning as (b).  </p></br><p>d. Urine culture – Investigates cystitis/pyelonephritis, which give dysuria, frequency, fever—not isolated post-impact hematuria with flank tenderness.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Key Takeaway<br /></br>In any hemodynamically stable patient with suspected renal trauma and gross hematuria, contrast-enhanced abdominal CT is the immediate diagnostic test of choice.</p>
64. A 67-year-old man with squamous cell carcinoma of the lung comes to the office for follow-up examination 10 days after receiving his first dose of chemotherapy with carboplatin and etoposide followed by radiation therapy. He had nausea and vomiting for 2 to 3 days following the treatment. He now feels weak but well overall. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows no new abnormalities. Results of laboratory studies are shown:</br>                         Before Treatment    After Treatment</br>Hematocrit (%)                   39                 32</br>Leukocyte count (/mm³)         4,900                300</br>Segmented neutrophils (%)        50                 20</br>Eosinophils (%)                   2                  0</br>Lymphocytes (%)                  45                 75</br>Monocytes (%)                     3                  5</br>Platelet count (/mm³)         178,000             34,000</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management of this patient’s pancytopenia?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Measurement of serum vitamin B₁₂ (cobalamin) and folate concentrations</br>b. Administration of ceftazidime</br>c. Administration of granulocyte colony–stimulating factor</br>d. Transfusion of leukocytes</br>e. Transfusion of packed red blood cells</br>f. Transfusion of platelets</br>g. Bone marrow biopsy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>Administration of granulocyte colony-stimulating factor (filgrastim, pegfilgrastim)  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Timing: 10 days after carboplatin/etoposide → classic nadir of chemotherapy-induced myelosuppression (7-14 d).<br /></br>• Counts:<br /></br>  – ANC = total WBC × % neutrophils = 300 × 0.20 ≈ 60 cells/μL (severe neutropenia &lt; 500).<br /></br>  – Platelets 34 000/μL (thrombocytopenia) and Hct 32 % (mild anemia).<br /></br>• Symptoms: No fever, bleeding, or cardiorespiratory compromise → he is clinically stable.<br /></br>Key takeaway: isolated cytopenias shortly after cytotoxic chemotherapy with otherwise normal exam strongly point to marrow suppression, not a new intrinsic marrow disease or nutritional deficiency.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1: Identify the problem → chemotherapy-induced pancytopenia with life-threatening ANC.<br /></br>Step 2: Prioritize which cytopenia is most urgent → infection risk from neutropenia is the immediate threat (mortality &gt; bleeding or anemia when ANC &lt; 100).<br /></br>Step 3: Choose the intervention that reverses neutropenia and prevents infection while he is still afebrile → G-CSF.  </p></br><p>Core concept: Filgrastim/pegfilgrastim accelerates neutrophil recovery, shortens duration of severe neutropenia, and lowers risk of febrile neutropenia in high-risk patients (age &gt; 65, platinum-based regimens, ANC expected &lt; 100).  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Alkylators (carboplatin) and topoisomerase II inhibitors (etoposide) damage rapidly dividing marrow precursors → dose-limiting myelosuppression.<br /></br>• The nadir for neutrophils occurs first (7-14 d) because they have the shortest half-life; platelets and RBCs fall later/less dramatically.<br /></br>• Exogenous G-CSF binds myeloid progenitors, hastens maturation and egress of neutrophils from marrow.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests</br>• Chemotherapy-induced neutropenia, afebrile, ANC &lt; 100 → give G-CSF now.<br /></br>• If he develops fever (≥ 38.0 °C) → add broad-spectrum anti-pseudomonal IV antibiotics (e.g., cefepime, ceftazidime, piperacillin-tazobactam) within 60 min.<br /></br>• Platelet transfusion threshold: &lt; 10 000 (or &lt; 50 000 with active bleeding/procedure).<br /></br>• Packed RBC transfusion threshold: Hb ≤ 7 g/dL (≈ Hct &lt; 21 %) or symptomatic anemia.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“7–14 rule”: 7–14 d after chemo the Neutrophil nadir Hits (N → NH). Give G-CSF if ANC &lt; 500 or expected &lt; 100.<br /></br>“Bugs before Bleeds”: In post-chemo cytopenia, treat neutropenia first (infection kills faster than bleeding).  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Measure B12 &amp; folate – Megaloblastic anemia causes isolated macrocytic RBC changes over weeks–months, NOT abrupt pancytopenia 10 d post-chemo.<br /></br>b. Ceftazidime – Appropriate only if patient is febrile (febrile neutropenia). He is afebrile. Prophylactic IV antibiotics are not standard once neutropenia is already present.<br /></br>d. Leukocyte transfusion – Rarely used; short-lived, high allo-sensitization risk; reserved for refractory life-threatening infections.<br /></br>e. Packed RBCs – Hct 32 % (Hb ≈ 10.5 g/dL). Transfusion threshold not reached and patient asymptomatic.<br /></br>f. Platelets – Transfuse if &lt; 10 000 or active bleeding. He is 34 000 and bleeding-free.<br /></br>g. Bone marrow biopsy – Etiology is evident (recent chemotherapy); biopsy adds no management-altering information.  </p></br><p>Bottom Line</br>Severe, asymptomatic neutropenia 7–14 days after cytotoxic chemotherapy should be treated with G-CSF to hasten marrow recovery and prevent life-threatening infection.</p>
65. A 47-year-old man comes to the office because of a 6-month history of aching, burning pain in both legs. Initially, he had pain only after he walked one-half mile, but now the pain occurs after he walks one-half block and resolves only after he rests for at least 1 minute. He says his symptoms interfere with his job as a construction worker on a large work site. He is able to ride an exercise bicycle without pain. He has hypertension, type 2 diabetes mellitus, and hypercholesterolemia. His medications are lisinopril, metformin, and atorvastatin. Two years ago, he underwent orchiectomy and retroperitoneal radiation therapy for testicular cancer. His blood pressure is 130/70 mm Hg; other vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows absence of the left testis. Ankle brachial indices are 0.9 in both feet. Deep tendon reflexes are moderately decreased at the ankles. Sensation to pinprick is decreased over the feet. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. An MRI of the lumbosacral spine shows narrowing of the spinal canal secondary to congenital stenosis and superimposed degenerative changes. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Epidural administration of dexamethasone</br>b. Ibuprofen therapy and bed rest</br>c. Lumbar laminectomy</br>d. Somatosensory evoked potentials</br>e. Venous Doppler ultrasonography of the lower extremities</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>a. Epidural administration of dexamethasone</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• “Aching, burning pain in both legs” that<br /></br>  – is precipitated by walking a short distance (now ½ block)<br /></br>  – is relieved only after ≥1 min of rest<br /></br>  – is absent while riding an exercise bicycle (a flexed-hip/forward-lean position)  </p></br><p>• Normal/near-normal ankle-brachial index (0.9) → argues against hemodynamically significant peripheral arterial disease (PAD).</p></br><p>• Decreased ankle reflexes &amp; distal pin-prick sensation → suggest concomitant diabetic peripheral neuropathy but do not alone explain exertional leg pain pattern.</p></br><p>• MRI: congenital spinal canal stenosis + degenerative changes = anatomic substrate for neurogenic claudication (lumbar spinal stenosis).</p></br><p>• Key discriminator: pain improved by spinal flexion (bicycle test) and worsened by upright/extended lumbar posture (walking). Classic for neurogenic claudication, not vascular claudication.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1: Identify claudication type.<br /></br>  • Vascular → low ABI, pain reproducible after a fixed distance, persists when standing.<br /></br>  • Neurogenic → normal ABI, variable distance, relieved by flexion, bicycle OK.</p></br><p>Step 2: Confirm an anatomic cause. MRI already demonstrates spinal stenosis.</p></br><p>Step 3: Choose first-line therapy.<br /></br>  • Conservative measures first: NSAIDs, physical therapy, epidural glucocorticoid injections.<br /></br>  • If ≥3–6 mo of conservative therapy fails or progressive neuro deficits → surgical decompression (laminectomy).</p></br><p>Therefore, epidural steroid (dexamethasone) is the next appropriate step.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>Lumbar spinal stenosis = narrowing of the central canal/foramina (degenerative facet arthropathy, ligamentum flavum hypertrophy, congenital short pedicles). Extension further narrows the canal → cauda equina ischemia/compression → neurogenic claudication. Flexion increases canal diameter → symptom relief.</p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review</br>1.
