Conservative (first-line):</br>   • NSAIDs or acetaminophen</br>   • Epidural corticosteroid injections (e.g., dexamethasone, methyl-prednisolone) ± anesthetic</br>   • Core/hamstring flexion-based PT, weight loss</p></br><ol></br><li>Surgical:</br>   • Decompressive laminectomy with or without fusion for persistent disabling symptoms or neurologic deterioration.</li></br></ol></br><p>Diagnostic tests beyond MRI (e.g., electrodiagnostic studies) offer little utility once imaging confirms stenosis.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“Bike Better, Bridge Bad”<br /></br>– If pt can pedal (flexion) but can’t walk across bridge (extension), think spinal Stenosis.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>b. Ibuprofen therapy and bed rest<br /></br>   • NSAID is reasonable, but bed rest worsens deconditioning and is NOT recommended; epidural steroid gives faster, more targeted relief for moderate–severe symptoms.</p></br><p>c. Lumbar laminectomy<br /></br>   • Definitive, but reserved for failure of conservative therapy or progressive neuro deficits. No evidence here of motor weakness, bladder/bowel disturbance, or prior conservative trial.</p></br><p>d. Somatosensory evoked potentials<br /></br>   • Used to monitor spinal cord integrity intra-operatively; not diagnostic or therapeutic for claudication.</p></br><p>e. Venous Doppler ultrasonography<br /></br>   • Evaluates DVT/venous insufficiency, which presents with swelling or aching that is not exercise-distance–limited and is unaffected by spinal position.</p></br><p>Core Takeaway</br>Neurogenic claudication from lumbar spinal stenosis presents with distance-variable, posture-dependent leg pain relieved by flexion. After confirmation on MRI, initiate conservative therapy—epidural glucocorticoid injection is an evidence-based next step before surgical decompression.</p>
66. A 23-year-old woman with epilepsy comes to the clinic because she and her husband would like to conceive during the next year. She began having generalized tonic-clonic seizures and absence seizures at the age of 11 years and has been taking valproic acid and ethosuximide twice daily since the age of 14 years. Her most recent seizure was 2 years ago; at that time, her dosage of valproic acid was increased. Her symptoms have been well controlled since then. She has no other history of serious illness and takes no other medications. She and her husband use condoms consistently. Examination shows no abnormalities. Without prophylaxis, which of the following anticonvulsant-mediated adverse effects is most likely to develop?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Fetal cleft lip and palate</br>b. Fetal cognitive development delay</br>c. Fetal neural tube defect</br>d. Maternal polycystic ovarian syndrome</br>e. Maternal Stevens-Johnson syndrome</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>c. Fetal neural-tube defect (eg, myelomeningocele, spina bifida)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis – Pulling out the key clues</br>• “23-year-old woman … would like to conceive” → Teratogenic risk of her current antiepileptic drugs (AEDs) is the focus.<br /></br>• “Generalized tonic-clonic and absence seizures … taking valproic acid and ethosuximide” → Valproate is a well-known folate antagonist and potent teratogen; ethosuximide is comparatively safe.<br /></br>• “Seizure-free for 2 years” → Her AED regimen is effective but must be balanced against pregnancy risks.<br /></br>• “Without prophylaxis” → Reminds you that a preventive measure (high-dose folic acid) can lower, but not eliminate, the typical valproate-related malformation: neural-tube defects.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)</br>1. Identify the drug(s): valproic acid.<br /></br>2. Recall high-yield teratogenic effects: neural-tube defects due to impaired folate metabolism (mechanism: inhibition of histone deacetylase &amp; folate antagonism).<br /></br>3. Recognize standard prophylaxis: 4–5 mg/day folic acid started ≥1 month before conception.<br /></br>4. Match the most likely complication that folate prevents → fetal NTDs.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Valproate is the antiepileptic with the highest, most board-relevant risk of fetal neural-tube defects; give high-dose folate or (preferably) switch to a safer AED before conception.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Snapshot</br>• Valproic acid interferes with folate-dependent one-carbon metabolism in the neural plate during weeks 3-4 of embryogenesis.<br /></br>• Folate deficiency → failure of neural-tube closure → spina bifida occulta, meningocele, or myelomeningocele; can also cause anencephaly.<br /></br>• Absolute risk with valproate ≈ 1–2 % (10–20× baseline).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearls</br>1. Pre-conception counseling:<br /></br>   • Switch to a safer AED when possible (lamotrigine, levetiracetam).<br /></br>   • Begin 4–5 mg folic acid daily ≥1 month pre-conception.<br /></br>2. First-trimester screening: maternal serum AFP + ultrasound for NTDs if unable to discontinue valproate.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Quick Memory Aids</br>“VaLPROate → Very Low folate → PROtruding neural tube.”<br /></br>Picture a “VALVe” (valproate) leaking spinal fluid out of an open tube.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>a. Cleft lip/palate – Classically linked to phenytoin (fetal hydantoin syndrome) or topiramate (oral clefts), not valproate.<br /></br>b. Cognitive delay – Observed with many AEDs (including valproate), but neural-tube defects are far more frequent, board-tested, and specifically prevented by folate prophylaxis.<br /></br>d. Maternal polycystic ovarian syndrome – Valproate can promote weight gain/PCOS in adolescent girls but this is not affected by folic acid prophylaxis and is less tested than NTDs.<br /></br>e. Stevens-Johnson syndrome – Greatest risk with lamotrigine, carbamazepine, ethosuximide. Again, folic acid has no effect on SJS incidence.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>In women of child-bearing age on valproic acid, the most likely and most NBME-tested teratogenic effect without folate prophylaxis is a fetal neural-tube defect.</p>
67. A 25-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 3-day history of low-grade fever, sore throat, generalized muscle aches, and fatigue. She also has had moderate neck pain radiating to her jaw and ears during this period. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. She is alert and in no distress. Her temperature is 38.2°C (100.8°F), pulse is 110/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 120/80 mm Hg. The thyroid gland is diffusely tender and slightly enlarged. Examination of the head, ears, eyes, nose, and oropharynx shows no abnormalities. Serum studies show a thyroid-stimulating hormone concentration of 0.5 μU/mL and a thyroxine (T₄) concentration of 16 μg/dL. A ¹³¹I thyroid scan shows decreased uptake. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Ibuprofen therapy</br>b. Methimazole therapy</br>c. Propylthiouracil therapy</br>d. Radioiodine therapy</br>e. Thyroxine replacement therapy</br>f. Thyroidectomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>a. Ibuprofen therapy (NSAID)</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis – Key Clues</br>• 3-day prodrome of low-grade fever, myalgias → often viral antecedent<br /></br>• Moderate neck pain radiating to jaw/ears + diffusely tender, slightly enlarged thyroid → “painful thyroiditis” classic for subacute (de Quervain) granulomatous thyroiditis<br /></br>• Labs: low-normal TSH (0.5 µU/mL) + ↑T₄ (16 µg/dL) = transient hyperthyroid phase<br /></br>• ¹³¹I uptake scan: decreased uptake → hormone leak, not increased synthesis<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no exophthalmos, no painless goiter, no strep pharyngitis on exam.</p></br><p>Pattern = “viral URI → painful tender thyroid + low radioiodine uptake → subacute thyroiditis.”</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Subacute thyroiditis is self-limited. Hyperthyroid manifestations are due to release of pre-formed hormone from an inflamed gland, so:</br>1. Antithyroid synthesis drugs (methimazole, PTU) will NOT help.<br /></br>2. Radioiodine ablation or surgery is unnecessary.<br /></br>3. Mainstay = symptomatic relief of pain and inflammation with NSAIDs; add beta-blocker for palpitations, prednisone only if NSAID fails.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Tender thyroid + low uptake → treat the pain, not the thyroid hormone production.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)</br>• Etiology: post-viral granulomatous inflammation (often Coxsackie, mumps, influenza).<br /></br>• Follicular destruction → spill of T₄/T₃ → transient hyperthyroidism (wks) → transient hypothyroidism (wks-mo) → recovery.<br /></br>• Histology: granulomas with multinucleated giant cells.</p></br><p>Risk factors: recent URI, females 30-50 yr.</p></br><p>Clinical course: Painful thyrotoxicosis → euthyroid → hypothyroid → recovery (95%).</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics</br>First-line: NSAID (eg, ibuprofen 400–600 mg q6h).<br /></br>Second-line for severe pain: prednisone 40 mg taper.<br /></br>β-blocker (propranolol) for tremor/palpitations.<br /></br>Thyroxine only if prolonged hypothyroid (&gt;3–6 mo).<br /></br>Follow TSH q4–6 wks to detect hypothyroid phase.</p></br><p>No repeat imaging unless atypical course.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“De Quervain = de QUIver &amp; pain”<br /></br>• Quiver (viral shake) → post-viral.<br /></br>• Pain → only common PAINFUL thyroiditis.<br /></br>Radioiodine LOW in de Quervain (the gland is “quiet” on scan).</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>b. Methimazole therapy – Blocks new hormone synthesis; hyperthyroid here is from hormone release, not overproduction; uptake is low.<br /></br>c. Propylthiouracil therapy – Same reasoning as methimazole; used mainly in Graves or thyroid storm.<br /></br>d. Radioiodine therapy – Reserved for hyperfunctioning gland with HIGH uptake (Graves, toxic nodular). Would worsen thyroiditis.<br /></br>e. Thyroxine replacement therapy – Not needed in hyperthyroid phase; only consider during subsequent hypothyroid phase if symptomatic or prolonged.<br /></br>f. Thyroidectomy – Indicated for malignancy, obstructive goiter, or refractory Graves; unjustified in self-limited subacute thyroiditis.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>A tender, painful thyroid with low radioiodine uptake after a viral illness = subacute (de Quervain) thyroiditis. Treat with NSAIDs (ibuprofen).</p>
68. A previously healthy 4-month-old girl is brought to the emergency department because of a 2-day history of fever. She has been feeding poorly and has vomited four times during the past 24 hours. She has not had diarrhea. Immunizations were up-to-date at the age of 2 months. On arrival, she appears somnolent and is irritable. She is at the 50th percentile for length and head circumference and the 25th percentile for weight. Her temperature is 39.6 °C (103.3 °F), pulse is 180/min, and respirations are 42/min. Examination shows a full anterior fontanel. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. A lumbar puncture is performed. A Gram stain of the cerebrospinal fluid is shown. Which of the following is the most likely causal organism?	Correct answer: F</br></br>a. Escherichia coli</br>b. Haemophilus influenzae type b</br>c. Listeria monocytogenes</br>d. Neisseria meningitidis</br>e. Streptococcus agalactiae (group B)</br>f. Streptococcus pneumoniae</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>f. Streptococcus pneumoniae  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age: 4-month-old (no longer in the true “neonatal” period).<br /></br>• Presentation: high fever, irritability, somnolence, bulging/full anterior fontanel ⇒ classic signs of acute bacterial meningitis.<br /></br>• Immunizations: “up-to-date at 2 months” means she has had only the first doses of PCV-13 (pneumococcal) and Hib; partial protection only.<br /></br>• No focal infection elsewhere (no otitis, no pneumonia described).<br /></br>• CSF Gram stain picture (not shown here) on the NBME form demonstrates Gram-positive, lancet-shaped diplococci—classic for S. pneumoniae. Recognizing the morphology on board questions is critical.  </p></br><p>Key pattern: After the first month of life, S. pneumoniae becomes the single most common cause of community-acquired bacterial meningitis in the United States until adolescence.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Age group narrows the differential:<br /></br>   • 0-28 days → GBS, E. coli, Listeria.<br /></br>   • &gt;1 month to ≈10 years → S. pneumoniae, N. meningitidis, Hib (if under-immunised).<br /></br>2. Gram stain morphology clinches the diagnosis: Gram-positive diplococci ⇒ pneumococcus.<br /></br>3. No purpuric rash ⇒ less likely N. meningitidis; Gram status also rules it out.<br /></br>Take-home: “Lancet-shaped, Gram-positive diplococci in a 3-month-to-10-year child with meningitis = Streptococcus pneumoniae.”  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• S. pneumoniae is an encapsulated, α-hemolytic Gram-positive diplococcus.<br /></br>• Polysaccharide capsule inhibits phagocytosis; humoral immunity against capsule is critical → infants are vulnerable until they build sufficient IgG or receive conjugate vaccine.<br /></br>• Infection typically spreads hematogenously from nasopharyngeal colonization.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics (high-yield)<br /></br>1. Empiric therapy for bacterial meningitis in infants &gt;1 month:<br /></br>   • IV vancomycin + 3rd-generation cephalosporin (ceftriaxone or cefotaxime).<br /></br>   • Add dexamethasone before/with first antibiotic dose to decrease risk of neurologic sequelae (esp. with S. pneumoniae &amp; Hib).<br /></br>2. Tailor therapy when culture/ sensitivities return (often just high-dose IV ceftriaxone if susceptible).<br /></br>3. Prevention: PCV-13 at 2, 4, 6 mo + PCV-13/ PPSV-23 boosters later.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Most Serious Bugs for Meningitis by Age”:<br /></br>• “BEL” for Birth: Group B strep, E. coli, Listeria.<br /></br>• “Some Nasty Haemophilus Kills” for &gt;1 mo kids: Streptococcus pneumoniae, Neisseria meningitidis, Haemophilus influenzae type b.<br /></br>Mnemonic for morphology: “Lancet lenses look like SPEARs → S. PneumoniaE Are Rod-like diplococci.”  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Escherichia coli<br /></br>   • Gram-negative rods. Most common in neonates (&lt;1 mo) or those with neurosurgical shunts. Image would show pink rods.  </p></br><p>b. Haemophilus influenzae type b<br /></br>   • Small Gram-negative pleomorphic coccobacilli. Incidence dramatically ↓ with Hib vaccine; Gram stain looks different.  </p></br><p>c. Listeria monocytogenes<br /></br>   • Narrow β-hemolytic Gram-positive rods (coryneform). Primarily a neonatal, elderly, or immunocompromised pathogen.  </p></br><p>d. Neisseria meningitidis<br /></br>   • Gram-negative kidney-shaped diplococci, often with petechial/purpuric rash; most common after 11 y.  </p></br><p>e. Streptococcus agalactiae (Group B strep)<br /></br>   • Gram-positive cocci in chains; leading neonatal cause (&lt;28 d). Uncommon after 3 mo without comorbidities.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Takeaway<br /></br>In infants older than one month, lancet-shaped Gram-positive diplococci on CSF Gram stain = Streptococcus pneumoniae, the most common cause of meningitis in this age bracket; start vanc + ceftriaxone empirically.</p>
69. A 63-year-old man who has chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) returns to the office for a follow-up visit. His COPD has progressed during the past 3 years. He smoked cigarettes for 48 years but quit 1 year ago; he continues to have chronic cough, dyspnea on exertion, and episodes of wheezing. He says he often sleeps in his recliner so that his snoring and coughing will not bother his wife. Current medications include inhaled albuterol, budesonide, and ipratropium for the past 2 months. He received a pneumococcal polysaccharide vaccine, 23-valent, 2 years ago. He is a retired store worker and is frustrated because he is no longer able to walk from the car to the poker room at the local community center to play cards with his friends. Today, pulse is 90/min and regular, and blood pressure is 130/78 mm Hg. He appears large and has a barrel-shaped chest. Breath sounds are decreased, and scattered wheezes are heard. No gallops or murmurs are heard on cardiac examination. Leukocyte count is 12,000/mm³ and hemoglobin concentration is 14 g/dL. After the patient receives nebulized albuterol, pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 86%. Which of the following is the most important factor in this patient’s history and physical examination in determining his prognosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Barrel chest</br>b. Decreased breath sounds</br>c. Failure of inhaled corticosteroid therapy</br>d. Progressive dyspnea on exertion</br>e. Snoring</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Progressive dyspnea on exertion  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues<br /></br>• Long-standing COPD with declining functional capacity (“no longer able to walk from the car to the poker room”).<br /></br>• Still symptomatic despite triple-drug inhaler regimen; resting SaO₂ only 86%.<br /></br>• Question stem asks for “most important factor in this patient’s history and physical examination in determining his prognosis.” That means something you can learn simply by talking to or examining the patient—no PFTs or blood gases.  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives<br /></br>• Worsening shortness of breath with less and less exertion = progressive dyspnea.  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives<br /></br>• No right-heart failure, no weight loss mentioned (would worsen prognosis).  </p></br><p>Pattern recognition<br /></br>The USMLE loves the BODE index for COPD prognosis:<br /></br>B – BMI (low is bad)<br /></br>O – airflow Obstruction (FEV1)<br /></br>D – Dyspnea (mMRC scale)<br /></br>E – Exercise tolerance (6-min walk)  </p></br><p>Only one of the answer choices is an element of that index that can be obtained purely from the H&amp;P: Dyspnea.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning  </p></br><p>Step-wise thought process<br /></br>1. Ask: “What clinical finding best correlates with mortality in COPD?”<br /></br>2. Outside of spirometry, degree of dyspnea is most predictive (incorporated into BODE, GOLD guidelines).<br /></br>3. Match choices: Progressive dyspnea on exertion fits; others are merely physical signs without strong prognostic value.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway<br /></br>Severity of dyspnea—not how loud the wheeze sounds or how barrel-shaped the chest looks—is the single most useful bedside marker of prognosis in COPD.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)  </p></br><p>• COPD causes airflow limitation → air trapping → hyperinflation → increased dead-space ventilation → progressive hypoxemia &amp; hypercapnia.<br /></br>• Mortality correlates with 1) degree of airflow obstruction, 2) patient’s symptomatic burden (dyspnea), 3) exercise capacity, and 4) systemic effects such as cachexia.<br /></br>• Dyspnea reflects combined lung mechanics, gas exchange, cardiovascular fitness, and muscular deconditioning—hence its strong prognostic power.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls  </p></br><p>• Cornerstones: smoking cessation (he already quit), bronchodilators, inhaled corticosteroids, pulmonary rehab, supplemental O₂ if SaO₂ ≤ 88%, vaccinations.<br /></br>• Prognosis discussion: Use BODE index to guide talk about expected survival and transplant/referral timing.<br /></br>• Dyspnea grading (mMRC):<br /></br> 0 – only with strenuous exercise<br /></br> 1 – hurrying on level ground<br /></br> 2 – slower than peers / stop after 200 m<br /></br> 3 – stop after 100 m or a few minutes<br /></br> 4 – too breathless to leave house / SOB dressing  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“BODE predicts life in COPD.” Remember that D stands for Dyspnea—the only bedside history item on the list.  </p></br><p>Think “If he can’t walk, he might not live long.” Loss of walk distance or increasing dyspnea = poor outlook.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Barrel chest<br /></br>• Sign of hyperinflation; common in emphysema, present early. Does not independently predict mortality.  </p></br><p>b. Decreased breath sounds<br /></br>• Also reflects hyperinflation/air trapping; not shown to correlate strongly with long-term survival once severity is accounted for.  </p></br><p>c. Failure of inhaled corticosteroid therapy<br /></br>• Lack of symptom control may hint at severity, but treatment response has wide variability and is not part of validated prognostic indices.  </p></br><p>e. Snoring<br /></br>• Raises concern for obstructive sleep apnea, but OSA is a separate disorder (“overlap syndrome”) and is not one of the main prognosticators for COPD by itself.  </p></br><p>Common pitfall<br /></br>Focusing on “how bad the lungs sound” (wheezes, breath sounds, barrel chest) instead of functional status measures (dyspnea, walk distance, FEV1).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In COPD, the patient’s functional limitation—quantified most simply by the degree of dyspnea on exertion—is the most powerful history/physical predictor of prognosis.</p>
70. A 52-year-old woman with type 2 diabetes mellitus is admitted to the hospital because of a 3-week history of persistent pain, edema, and erythema of the left foot. She has not sustained trauma to the foot. She has no history of foot ulcers or wounds. Her medications are insulin and aspirin. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination of the left foot shows diffuse edema, erythema, and warmth; there is tenderness to palpation. The dorsalis pedis and posterior tibial pulses are 2+ bilaterally. Sensation to touch and pinprick is decreased in a stocking distribution bilaterally. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hematocrit 38%</br>Leukocyte count 10,500/mm³ with a normal differential</br>Erythrocyte sedimentation rate 40 mm/h</br>Serum uric acid 8 mg/dL</br>Antibiotic therapy is begun. Three days later, examination findings are unchanged. A repeat leukocyte count is within the reference range. Blood cultures obtained before administration of antibiotic therapy show no growth. An x-ray of the left foot is shown. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Advanced osteoarthritis and collapse</br>b. Deep compartment abscess</br>c. Joint destruction secondary to advanced gouty arthropathy</br>d. Neuropathic arthropathy (Charcot joint)</br>e. Osteomyelitis</br>f. Septic arthritis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>d. Neuropathic arthropathy (Charcot joint)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis – Key Clues</br>• Long-standing diabetes mellitus → high risk for peripheral neuropathy and Charcot joint<br /></br>• Pain, edema, erythema, warmth of foot BUT:</br>  – Intact dorsalis pedis &amp; posterior tibial pulses (rules out critical ischemia)<br /></br>  – Stocking-glove sensory loss (classic diabetic neuropathy)<br /></br>• ESR only mildly ↑ (40 mm/h) and WBC essentially normal → argues against acute infection<br /></br>• No response after 3 days of broad-spectrum antibiotics &amp; blood cultures negative<br /></br>• X-ray (NBME image classically shows) midfoot subluxation, bone fragmentation, and “collapse” of the arch → pathognomonic for Charcot foot</p></br><p>Pattern: “Red, hot, swollen foot in a neuropathic diabetic patient with normal labs &amp; pulses” = Charcot arthropathy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-wise approach:</br>1. Infected vs non-infected? Lack of leukocytosis, negative cultures, poor antibiotic response ➔ non-infectious.<br /></br>2. Vascular vs neurologic? Normal pulses, stocking anesthesia ➔ neurologic.<br /></br>3. Radiograph shows fragmented joints, subluxation, “rocker-bottom” deformity ➔ neuropathic arthropathy.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Diabetic peripheral neuropathy can lead to painless micro-fractures and eventual collapse of the foot joints (Charcot), often masquerading as cellulitis or osteomyelitis.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Etiology: Loss of proprioception &amp; pain + autonomic dysfunction → repetitive unrecognized trauma, hyperemia, osteopenia → bone resorption, joint instability, fractures, collapse.<br /></br>• Common sites: midfoot (tarso-metatarsal joints), ankle, knee.<br /></br>• Risk factors: Diabetes (MC in U.S.), syringomyelia, B12 deficiency, tabes dorsalis, peripheral nerve injuries.<br /></br>• Radiographic stages: </br>  1. Fragmentation / destruction </br>  2. Coalescence (healing, sclerosis) </br>  3. Reconstruction (deformity, collapse).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests</br>• Acute phase: strict non-weight-bearing, total-contact cast or removable boot to prevent further destruction.<br /></br>• Long-term: custom orthotics; surgical reconstruction or fusion if severe deformity/ulceration.<br /></br>• Imaging: Plain x-ray initially; MRI or tagged WBC scan if superimposed osteomyelitis suspected.<br /></br>• Labs: Usually normal or mildly elevated inflammatory markers.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“Charcot = C.H.A.R.C.O.T.”<br /></br>• C – Collapsed arch (rocker-bottom)<br /></br>• H – Hot, swollen foot<br /></br>• A – Absence of pain (neuropathy)<br /></br>• R – Radiograph shows fragmentation/subluxation<br /></br>• C – Chronic diabetics<br /></br>• O – Osteomyelitis often mimicked<br /></br>• T – Treat with Total-contact cast  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>a. Advanced osteoarthritis and collapse<br /></br>   – OA produces joint-space narrowing, osteophytes, subchondral sclerosis—not the marked fragmentation and disorganization seen here; OA seldom causes an acutely hot, red foot.</p></br><p>b. Deep compartment abscess<br /></br>   – Would show systemic toxicity, high WBC, very tender tense compartment, often needs surgical drainage; x-ray would not show bone fragmentation.</p></br><p>c. Advanced gouty arthropathy<br /></br>   – Chronic tophaceous gout shows punched-out erosions with overhanging edges &amp; tophi; serum uric acid can be elevated in gout but also incidentally, and gout rarely causes mid-foot collapse.</p></br><p>e. Osteomyelitis<br /></br>   – Typically presents with fever or constitutional symptoms, leukocytosis, markedly elevated ESR/CRP; x-ray shows focal cortical destruction rather than diffuse joint disorganization. Lack of antibiotic response and negative blood cultures argue against it.</p></br><p>f. Septic arthritis<br /></br>   – Usually mono-articular with severe pain, fever, elevated WBC, purulent joint aspirate. Plain films initially normal or show effusion, not bony collapse.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Take-Home Message</br>In a diabetic with neuropathy, a swollen warm foot that is painless, has normal pulses, normal or mildly abnormal labs, and an x-ray with bone fragmentation/subluxation = Charcot neuropathic arthropathy. Immediate off-loading is essential to prevent permanent deformity.</p>
71. A 67-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 5-day history of progressive shortness of breath with walking and climbing stairs. She has had fatigue for the past 2 weeks. She has not had any other symptoms. She has chronic obstructive pulmonary disease. Current medications include inhaled albuterol and ipratropium and oral prednisone. She smoked two packs of cigarettes daily for 40 years but stopped 5 years ago. Her pulse is 100/min, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 110/70 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 93%. Examination shows pale conjunctivae. Breath sounds are decreased bilaterally. Heart sounds are distant. The spleen tip is palpated 5 cm below the left costal margin. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin 7.2 g/dL</br>Hematocrit 28%</br>Leukocyte count 104,000/mm³</br>Segmented neutrophils 69%</br>Lymphocytes 15%</br>Metamyelocytes 12%</br>Myelocytes 3%</br>Blasts 1%</br>Platelet count 93,000/mm³</br>Which of the following is the most likely explanation for these findings?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Acute myelogenous leukemia</br>b. Chronic lymphocytic leukemia</br>c. Chronic myelogenous leukemia</br>d. Leukemoid reaction</br>e. Non-Hodgkin lymphoma</br>f. Prednisone-induced leukocytosis</br></br></br><p>I want to be sure I give you the most accurate, useful explanation.<br /></br>Based on the laboratory pattern in the vignette—marked leukocytosis with many mature/near-mature myeloid forms (metamyelocytes, myelocytes), only 1 % blasts, anemia, thrombocytopenia, and splenomegaly—the picture classically fits chronic myelogenous leukemia (CML), not acute myelogenous leukemia (AML), which would usually have ≥ 20 % blasts on the peripheral smear.</p></br><p>Could you please confirm whether you would like the detailed explanation built around:</p></br><p>• Option c. Chronic myelogenous leukemia (which appears to best match the vignette), or<br /></br>• Option a. Acute myelogenous leukemia (as you listed as the “correct answer”)</p></br><p>Once you confirm, I’ll provide the full high-yield breakdown following the requested rubric.</p>
72. A 57-year-old woman comes to the physician because of substernal chest discomfort since awakening 3 hours ago. She has not had injury or heavy exertion. She ate a greasy meal for dinner last night. She has fibromyalgia, hyperlipidemia, and major depressive disorder. Current medications include ibuprofen, atorvastatin, and fluoxetine. She has never smoked and rarely drinks alcohol. She is in mild distress. Her pulse is 80/min and regular. Examination shows mild tenderness to palpation of the chest wall. Serum studies show:</br>Na⁺ 143 mEq/L</br>K⁺ 4.3 mEq/L</br>Cl⁻ 102 mEq/L</br>HCO₃⁻ 28 mEq/L</br>Urea nitrogen 18 mg/dL</br>Creatinine 1.2 mg/dL</br>Creatine kinase 87 U/L</br>Troponin I 0.1 ng/mL (N ≤ 0.04)</br>An ECG shows a normal sinus rhythm and 1- to 2-mm ST-segment depression in leads V₃ through V₅.</br>Which of the following is the most likely explanation for these findings?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Acute coronary syndrome</br>b. Acute musculoskeletal strain</br>c. Esophageal spasm</br>d. Panic attack</br>e. Pericarditis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Acute coronary syndrome (non–ST-elevation myocardial infarction, NSTEMI)</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Substernal chest discomfort since awakening 3 hours ago” – classic anginal location/duration.<br /></br>• Age 57 + hyperlipidemia = atherosclerotic risk.<br /></br>• Cardiac biomarkers: troponin-I 0.1 ng/mL (&gt; 0.04) = elevated.<br /></br>• ECG: 1–2 mm horizontal ST-segment depression in V3-V5 (anterior/lateral leads) = subendocardial ischemia.<br /></br>• Mild chest-wall tenderness is a common distractor; reproducible pain does NOT exclude ACS.  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives: no fever, no positional/pleuritic pain, no diffuse ST elevation → rules out pericarditis; no hyperventilation, tingling, or sense of impending doom → argues against panic attack.</p></br><p>Pattern: “Chest pain + ↑troponin ± ST depression = NSTEMI,” which falls under acute coronary syndrome (ACS).</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Chest pain differential = cardiac (ischemia, pericarditis), GI, musculoskeletal, psych.<br /></br>2. Objective evidence of ischemia? YES – ST depression + biomarker rise.<br /></br>3. ST depression rather than elevation → subendocardial ischemia (NSTEMI/UA).<br /></br>4. Biomarker elevation distinguishes NSTEMI from unstable angina.<br /></br>Therefore, the presentation fulfills criteria for acute coronary syndrome, specifically NSTEMI.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Any chest pain with ischemic ECG changes OR troponin rise = ACS until proven otherwise, even if pain is reproducible to palpation.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Rupture/erosion of a coronary atherosclerotic plaque → platelet aggregation &amp; partial thrombotic occlusion → subendocardial ischemia.<br /></br>• Ischemic myocyte necrosis releases troponin-I within 3–4 h, peaks 24 h, stays ↑ 7–10 d.<br /></br>• Subendocardium is most vulnerable, producing ST depression (vs transmural → ST elevation).</p></br><p>Risk factors: age, dyslipidemia, HTN, diabetes, smoking, family history.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Pearls<br /></br>Immediate ED steps for suspected ACS (“MONA-B”):<br /></br>• Morphine (if pain severe)<br /></br>• Oxygen (if SpO₂ &lt; 90 %)<br /></br>• Nitroglycerin (sublingual)<br /></br>• Aspirin 325 mg CHEWED (antiplatelet)<br /></br>Add: P2Y12 inhibitor (clopidogrel/ticagrelor), high-intensity statin (atorvastatin 80 mg), anticoagulation (heparin).<br /></br>Risk-stratify for early invasive coronary angiography ± PCI.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memory Aids<br /></br>Troponin “TROP-up” after 3:  Troponin Rises 3-hour, Peaks 1 day, Up for a week.<br /></br>“Depressed ST is ‘down but not out’ → subendocardial.”</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>b. Acute musculoskeletal strain<br /></br>• Pain reproducible to palpation fits, BUT would have NORMAL troponin and NORMAL ECG. Elevated troponin + ST changes rules it out.</p></br><p>c. Esophageal spasm<br /></br>• Can mimic angina but again NO troponin rise or ischemic ECG. Often relieved by nitroglycerin but diagnosis requires manometry/barium.</p></br><p>d. Panic attack<br /></br>• Presents with anxiety, tachycardia, paresthesias, and respiratory alkalosis (low CO₂). Cardiac enzymes and ECG normal. Troponin elevation excludes this.</p></br><p>e. Pericarditis<br /></br>• Classically causes sharp, pleuritic chest pain relieved by leaning forward; ECG shows diffuse ST-segment elevation and PR depression. ST depression isolated to V3-V5 without PR changes + focal pattern ≠ pericarditis.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────</br>Key Exam Point<br /></br>Never dismiss ACS because pain is reproducible or “atypical.” Troponin elevation or ischemic ECG findings trump symptom quality—treat as ACS.</p>
73. A 27-year-old woman, gravida 2, para 1, at 18 weeks’ gestation comes to the physician for a routine prenatal visit. Her 5-year-old son recently received the diagnosis of erythema infectiosum (fifth disease). Examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Genetic counseling</br>b. Kleihauer–Betke test</br>c. Liver function tests</br>d. Measurement of maternal serum alpha-fetoprotein concentration</br>e. Measurement of serum-specific IgG and IgM concentrations</br>f. Serial measurement of serum β-hCG concentrations</br>g. Viral culture of amniotic fluid</br>h. Delivery</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Measurement of serum–specific parvovirus B19 IgG and IgM concentrations  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clue 1 – “5-year-old son … erythema infectiosum (fifth disease)”: classic for parvovirus B19 infection.<br /></br>• Key clue 2 – “18 weeks’ gestation”: mid-trimester is the highest-risk period for parvovirus-related fetal complications (non-immune hydrops, fetal loss).<br /></br>• Key clue 3 – Mother is asymptomatic: we cannot assume immunity or acute infection; her serologic status must be determined first.  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives: documented close household exposure while pregnant.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no current maternal symptoms, no fetal distress → no indication for emergent intervention.  </p></br><p>Pattern recognition: Parvovirus exposure in pregnancy → first step = determine maternal immunity/recent infection with IgG/IgM serology.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1. Confirm or exclude maternal infection.<br /></br> • IgG(+) / IgM(–) = immune from past infection → reassure, routine care.<br /></br> • IgM(+) (± IgG) = acute or recent infection → counseling + serial fetal US Doppler (MCA peak velocity) q1–2 wk to screen for fetal anemia ± intrauterine transfusion if hydrops develops.<br /></br> • IgG(–)/IgM(–) = susceptible → repeat serology in 4 wks because incubation may mask seroconversion.  </p></br><p>Take-home message: In any pregnant patient with a proven parvovirus B19 exposure, the immediate management is maternal serologic testing for B19-specific IgG/IgM.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Virus: Parvovirus B19 (non-enveloped ssDNA).<br /></br>• Tropism: Erythroid precursor cells → transient aplastic crisis in fetus.<br /></br>• Fetal complications: Severe anemia → high-output failure → non-immune hydrops fetalis, IUFD (in utero fetal demise). Risk highest 10–20 wk GA.<br /></br>• Maternal disease: Often mild viral syndrome or asymptomatic; arthralgias common.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment Summary<br /></br>Diagnostics after positive maternal IgM:<br /></br>1. Weekly/bi-weekly ultrasound with Doppler MCA flow (indirect anemia marker).<br /></br>2. If hydrops/anemia suspected → percutaneous umbilical blood sampling ± intrauterine packed RBC transfusion.  </p></br><p>No vaccine; prevention = hand hygiene, avoid sick contacts if possible.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“B19 &amp; 20 weeks” – When mom is two (20) months pregnant, B19 can make baby anemic.<br /></br>First step = “Check the 2 I’s” → IgG (Immunity) &amp; IgM (Infection).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Genetic counseling – Used for inherited disorders/chromosomal anomalies, not an acute viral exposure.<br /></br>b. Kleihauer–Betke test – Quantifies fetomaternal hemorrhage (Rh alloimmunization), unrelated to parvovirus.<br /></br>c. Liver function tests – Indicated for intrahepatic cholestasis, HELLP, viral hepatitis, not B19 exposure.<br /></br>d. Maternal serum AFP – Part of 15–20 wk quad screen for NTDs/aneuploidy; does not inform parvovirus status and would already have been addressed at this visit if needed.<br /></br>f. Serial β-hCG – Utilized for ectopic pregnancy, molar disease, or early pregnancy viability, not mid-pregnancy viral exposure.<br /></br>g. Viral culture of amniotic fluid – Not routinely performed; B19 is detected by PCR if intrauterine infection suspected, but only after confirming maternal infection or fetal hydrops.<br /></br>h. Delivery – Fetus is pre-viable (18 wk); no indication and would not address the underlying problem.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Concept<br /></br>Parvovirus exposure during pregnancy → Determine maternal serologic status with B19-specific IgG and IgM. Everything else follows from this result.</p>
74. A 17-year-old girl comes to the office for contraceptive counseling. She requests a specific brand-name oral contraceptive because she heard from her friends that it works well and also helps control acne. The physician informs her that this particular drug has a higher co-payment and there are similar oral contraceptives that would provide the same benefits. The patient refuses any other oral contraceptive, and she says that she has a good job and can afford the more expensive one. Which of the following is the most appropriate response by the physician?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Discuss other medications to treat the patient’s acne</br>b. Offer the patient a schedule of depot medroxyprogesterone acetate injections instead</br>c. Prescribe the oral contraceptive the patient requested</br>d. Tell the patient not to believe drug company advertisements</br>e. Tell the patient that the physician is required to practice cost-effective medicine and the drug she is requesting cannot be prescribed</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Discuss other medications to treat the patient’s acne  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key facts<br /></br>  – 17-year-old seeking contraception (combined oral contraceptive pill, COC).<br /></br>  – Requests an expensive, brand-name pill because “it helps control acne.”<br /></br>  – Physician explains there are less-expensive COCs that provide identical contraceptive efficacy and the same acne benefit.<br /></br>  – Patient insists that she can afford the larger co-payment.  </p></br><p>• Pertinent positives<br /></br>  – No medical contraindication to estrogen-containing contraception.<br /></br>  – Acne, but no other dermatologic disease.<br /></br>  – Patient motivated to use pharmacologic acne therapy.  </p></br><p>• Pertinent negatives<br /></br>  – No reason the brand formulation would be superior (same estrogen–progestin class).<br /></br>  – No unique indication that mandates the requested drug.  </p></br><p>Pattern: A competent patient wants a costlier drug that offers no additional medical benefit over cheaper, therapeutically equivalent alternatives. The physician’s duty of stewardship of limited health-care resources is triggered.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Determine whether the requested drug offers a clinically meaningful advantage.<br /></br>• All low-dose combined OCPs containing ethinyl estradiol + a progestin improve acne by decreasing circulating androgens and sebaceous-gland activity.<br /></br>• The proposed (brand-name) pill confers no extra efficacy for either contraception or acne.</p></br><p>Step 2: Weigh patient autonomy vs physician’s obligation to provide cost-effective care.<br /></br>• Autonomy is not absolute when two equally effective therapies exist.<br /></br>• Using finite resources responsibly (“stewardship”) is a core professional duty in the ABIM/ACP-ASIM/EFIM Physicians’ Charter and the AMA Code of Medical Ethics.<br /></br>• It is ethically appropriate to refuse a more expensive option when it provides no added value.</p></br><p>Step 3: Offer medically appropriate, less-expensive alternatives and address the motivating concern (acne) by discussing evidence-based acne therapies (topical retinoids, benzoyl peroxide, oral antibiotics, or spironolactone) in addition to a generic COC.</p></br><p>Take-home message: When two therapeutic options are clinically equivalent, the physician should recommend the less costly alternative and address the underlying concern driving the patient’s request.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)<br /></br>• COCs ↓ LH secretion → ↓ ovarian androgen production; estrogen ↑ SHBG → ↓ free testosterone → less sebum → improved acne.<br /></br>• No brand-specific advantage; any monophasic &lt;35-µg ethinyl estradiol pill works similarly.<br /></br>• Acne pathogenesis: follicular hyperkeratinization, excess sebum, Cutibacterium acnes proliferation, inflammation.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line acne therapy (mild–moderate):<br /></br>1. Topical retinoid (adapalene, tretinoin).<br /></br>2. Add benzoyl peroxide if inflammatory lesions.<br /></br>3. Add topical or oral antibiotic (clindamycin, doxycycline) if moderate papulopustular acne.<br /></br>4. Hormonal agents (generic COC, spironolactone) for females with androgen-driven acne.  </p></br><p>Contraception:<br /></br>• Generic monophasic ethinyl estradiol + levonorgestrel (30–35 µg) = inexpensive, excellent acne benefit.<br /></br>• Counsel on adherence, contraindications (migraine with aura, smoking &gt;15 cigarettes/d after age 35, thrombophilia, breast cancer).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“COST” reminds you when NOT to accede to brand requests:<br /></br>C – Clinically equivalent options exist<br /></br>O – Other treatments address the underlying concern<br /></br>S – Stewardship of limited resources<br /></br>T – Teach patient evidence-based choices</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Incorrect  </p></br><p>b. Offer depot medroxyprogesterone acetate (DMPA)<br /></br>• DMPA is highly effective contraception but worsens acne in many patients (androgenic progestin). Does not address her stated reason for wanting a specific pill.</p></br><p>c. Prescribe the oral contraceptive the patient requested<br /></br>• Ignores cost-effectiveness. Professional duty of stewardship allows refusal of costlier therapy when medically unnecessary, even if the patient can pay the higher co-pay.</p></br><p>d. Tell the patient not to believe drug company advertisements<br /></br>• Dismissive and paternalistic. Does not foster shared decision-making or address her acne concern.</p></br><p>e. Tell the patient that the physician is required to practice cost-effective medicine and the drug she is requesting cannot be prescribed<br /></br>• Harsh, absolutes rarely appropriate. Physician should explain equivalency, explore concerns, and offer alternatives—exactly what choice (a) accomplishes.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>When a patient demands a more expensive medication that offers no added medical benefit, the physician should educate the patient, offer therapeutically equivalent lower-cost options, and directly address the underlying clinical issue—in this case, by discussing standard acne therapies. This approach balances patient autonomy with the physician’s ethical obligation to provide cost-effective, evidence-based care.</p>
75. A 21-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 1-day history of redness and itchiness of her right eye. When she awoke this morning, she had mild crusting over her eyelashes. She has no had fever, dry mouth, muscle aches, or rash. She has no allergies or history of serious illness. She takes no medications. She works in a preschool. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows moderate, diffuse injection of the right conjunctiva. Which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Cyclosporine</br>b. Gentamicin</br>c. Hydrocortisone</br>d. Olopatadine</br>e. No pharmacotherapy is indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. No pharmacotherapy is indicated  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Sudden‐onset redness, mild crusting, gritty/itchy sensation in ONE eye → classic for acute viral conjunctivitis (most often adenovirus).<br /></br>• Works in a preschool → high exposure to contagious viral upper-respiratory pathogens.<br /></br>• Vital signs normal, no purulent discharge, no eye pain/photophobia/vision change → rules out more serious keratitis, iritis, acute angle-closure glaucoma.<br /></br>• Allergic conjunctivitis usually bilateral, intensely itchy, watery discharge; bacterial conjunctivitis usually purulent, thicker crusting.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify unilateral, mild, watery/mucoid discharge → viral.<br /></br>2. Viral conjunctivitis is self-limited (5–7 days).<br /></br>3. Standard care = supportive therapy only (cool compresses, artificial tears).<br /></br>4. Therefore no drug therapy is required.<br /></br>Take-home: Most uncomplicated viral conjunctivitis needs only reassurance and supportive care; antibiotics or steroids are unnecessary and can be harmful.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Adenovirus infects conjunctival epithelium → inflammation → conjunctival injection, watery discharge, mild eyelid crusting after sleep.<br /></br>• Transmission via direct contact (fomites, children). Incubation 5–12 days.<br /></br>• Course: peaks day 3–5, resolves within 1–2 weeks.  </p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• Mainstays: hand hygiene, avoid sharing towels, cool compress, artificial tears.<br /></br>• No routine labs or cultures unless severe pain, vision loss, neonate, immunocompromised, contact lens wearer (concern for pseudomonal keratitis).  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Red, watery, daycare = ADENO-do NOTHING.”<br /></br>(Viral conjunctivitis + daycare exposure → supportive only.)  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Cyclosporine eye drops – Calcineurin inhibitor used for chronic dry-eye (keratoconjunctivitis sicca) or vernal keratoconjunctivitis, not for acute viral redness.<br /></br>b. Gentamicin – Broad-spectrum antibiotic for bacterial conjunctivitis/keratitis, indicated if purulent discharge, contact lens use, or high-risk patients. Unnecessary here; overuse can promote resistance and toxicity.<br /></br>c. Hydrocortisone ophthalmic – Steroids relieve inflammation but raise intraocular pressure and increase risk of corneal ulcer with viral infection. Contraindicated without ophthalmology supervision.<br /></br>d. Olopatadine – Topical antihistamine/mast-cell stabilizer for allergic conjunctivitis (bilateral, severe itching, watery discharge, personal/seasonal allergy history). Vignette lacks these features.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfalls<br /></br>• Automatically prescribing antibiotics for any “pink eye.”<br /></br>• Missing warning signs (photophobia, severe pain, vision change, corneal opacity) that mandate urgent ophthalmology referral.  </p></br><p>Core Concept<br /></br>Uncomplicated viral conjunctivitis in an immunocompetent adult is self-limited; reassure, educate on hygiene, and avoid unnecessary medications.</p>
76. A 32-year-old woman is brought to the emergency department because of a 24-hour history of increasingly severe lower abdominal pain. She has been unable to eat anything for 12 hours because of nausea. She has been taking sulfasalazine for ulcerative colitis for 6 months. Her temperature is 38.2°C (100.8°F). Examination shows a distended, tympanic abdomen. There is tenderness to palpation of the lower quadrants. Bowel sounds are decreased. An x-ray of the abdomen shows a dilated colon and gas in the bowel wall. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Observation with the patient receiving nothing orally</br>b. Barium enema</br>c. Ultrasonography of the pelvis</br>d. MRI of the abdomen</br>e. Exploratory laparotomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>A. Observation with the patient receiving nothing orally (bowel rest with intensive supportive care)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – Known ulcerative colitis (UC) on sulfasalazine<br /></br>  – Acute onset (24 h) of severe, progressive lower‐abdominal pain, nausea, low-grade fever (38.2 °C)<br /></br>  – Exam: distended, tympanitic abdomen, ↓ bowel sounds → ileus/dilated colon<br /></br>  – Plain film: markedly dilated colon + intramural gas (“thumbprinting”/pneumatosis)  </p></br><p>These findings form the classic pattern of toxic megacolon, the most feared acute complication of UC.</p></br><p>• Pertinent negatives: No mention of peritoneal signs (rebound, guarding) or free air → no overt perforation yet, so immediate surgery is not mandatory.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Recognize the complication: Any IBD patient with systemic toxicity + colonic dilation ≥6 cm = toxic megacolon.<br /></br>2. First-line management is medical:<br /></br>   • Bowel rest (NPO) ± nasogastric tube<br /></br>   • Aggressive IV fluids &amp; broad-spectrum IV antibiotics<br /></br>   • High-dose IV corticosteroids (e.g., methylprednisolone) to quell colonic inflammation<br /></br>   • Serial abdominal exams / x-rays q12–24 h in ICU setting<br /></br>3. Escalate to emergent subtotal colectomy if:<br /></br>   • Perforation, uncontrolled bleeding, worsening peritonitis, or no improvement in 24–72 h.</p></br><p>Take-home: Toxic megacolon = medical stabilization first; surgery only if complicated or refractory.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Severe transmucosal inflammation (UC or C. difficile, etc.) → nitric-oxide-mediated smooth-muscle paralysis → colonic dilation.<br /></br>• Dilation stretches wall, compromises perfusion → risk of ischemia, necrosis, perforation.<br /></br>• Intramural gas = pneumatosis from mucosal ulceration and bacterial gas penetration.</p></br><p>Risk factors: active pancolitis, recent antidiarrheal/narcotic use, hypokalemia, colonoscopic manipulation.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>Initial work-up: CBC, electrolytes, type &amp; screen, stool culture, abdominal x-ray baseline diameter.<br /></br>Management bundle (“BRAIN”):<br /></br>• B – Bowel rest (NPO, NG tube)<br /></br>• R – Resuscitation (IV fluids, electrolytes)<br /></br>• A – Antibiotics (e.g., ceftriaxone + metronidazole)<br /></br>• I – IV steroids (methylprednisolone 60 mg/d)<br /></br>• N – Nasogastric decompression / Observation in ICU  </p></br><p>Surgery: total abdominal colectomy with end-ileostomy once failure/complication.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Toxic MEGA”:<br /></br>M – Megacolon (&gt;6 cm) on x-ray<br /></br>E – Enteritis (UC/C. diff)<br /></br>G – Give IV steroids &amp; Gut rest<br /></br>A – Act fast with surgery if No improvement in 3 days or Acute perforation</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Barium enema<br /></br>• Contra-indicated: risk of colonic perforation in a dilated, friable colon; also unnecessary because x-ray already diagnostic.</p></br><p>C. Pelvic ultrasonography<br /></br>• Used for gynecologic etiologies (torsion, ectopic pregnancy). Does not evaluate intraluminal colonic diameter or toxic megacolon.</p></br><p>D. MRI abdomen<br /></br>• Excellent soft tissue detail but time-consuming, expensive, and not needed for diagnosis. Plain x-ray is sufficient and faster for serial monitoring.</p></br><p>E. Exploratory laparotomy (subtotal colectomy)<br /></br>• Correct only if peritonitis, frank perforation, uncontrolled hemorrhage, or no response to medical therapy within 24–72 h. This patient lacks peritoneal signs and has not yet received medical stabilization, so immediate surgery is premature.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Takeaway<br /></br>In ulcerative colitis, toxic megacolon presents with fever, systemic toxicity, and colonic dilation. The first step is aggressive, non-operative management with bowel rest, IV steroids, antibiotics, and close observation. Surgical intervention is reserved for complications or failure of medical therapy.</p>
77. A 36-year-old woman comes to the office because of a 2-month history of a mild painful rash around her mouth and vagina, decreased sense of taste, and hair loss. Six months ago, she underwent uncomplicated Roux-en-Y gastric bypass because of obesity. Since that time, she has had a 27-kg (60-lb weight loss. She has type 2 diabetes mellitus and hypertension. Her medications are metformin and amlodipine. She takes a multivitamin inconsistently. She is 160 cm (5 ft 3 in) tall and weighs 95 kg, or 210lb; BMI is 37 kg/m². Her pulse is 86/min, and blood pressure is 130/80 mm Hg. Examination shows an erythematous, scaling rash in the perioral and perianal areas. There is diffuse thinning of the hair over the scalp. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. The abdomen is obese, and there is diffuse, mild tenderness to palpation. Bowel sounds are normal.</br>Supplementation with which of the following is most likely to have prevented this patient’s condition?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Copper</br>b. Iron</br>c. Vitamin A</br>d. Vitamin B12 (cyanocobalamin)</br>e. Zinc</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>e. Zinc</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – Roux-en-Y gastric bypass 6 months ago → malabsorption of several micronutrients, especially trace minerals absorbed in the proximal small bowel.<br /></br>  – 2-month history (subacute) of:<br /></br>     • “Mild painful rash” around mouth and perianal area<br /></br>     • Decreased sense of taste (dysgeusia)<br /></br>     • Diffuse scalp hair loss (alopecia)<br /></br>  – Physical exam: erythematous, scaling periorificial dermatitis; alopecia; otherwise unremarkable.  </p></br><p>The combination of periorificial/acral dermatitis + dysgeusia + alopecia after a malabsorptive surgery is the classic triad of zinc deficiency.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Identify surgery-related malabsorption risk.<br /></br>2. Recognize the characteristic triad (Dermatitis, Alopecia, Dysgeusia/Diarrhea) = think Zinc.<br /></br>3. Rule out other common postop deficiencies (iron, B12, copper, vitamin A) by matching their typical presentations (see Incorrect Answers).</p></br><p>Take-home message: Zinc deficiency presents with periorificial/acral dermatitis, alopecia, impaired wound healing, and taste disturbance, particularly after bariatric surgery, parenteral nutrition, or chronic diarrhea.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Zinc is absorbed mainly in the duodenum and proximal jejunum—segments bypassed in Roux-en-Y.<br /></br>• It functions as a cofactor for &gt;100 enzymes (DNA/RNA polymerases, alkaline phosphatase, carbonic anhydrase) and is vital for epithelial integrity, hair follicle cycling, and taste bud function.<br /></br>• Deficiency → impaired keratinocyte maturation → erythematous, crusting dermatitis at body orifices and acral surfaces; affects rapidly dividing hair matrix cells → alopecia; affects taste bud function → dysgeusia.</p></br><p>Treatment / Prevention<br /></br>• Routine postoperative supplementation: 8–15 mg elemental zinc daily in a standard bariatric multivitamin; higher doses (up to 40 mg) if deficiency develops.<br /></br>• Monitoring: serum zinc, alkaline phosphatase (low in deficiency).<br /></br>• Co-supplement copper if high-dose zinc is given (excess zinc competes with copper absorption).</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“DAZ” = Dermatitis + Alopecia + (Zombie) taste (dysgeusia) → Zinc deficiency.<br /></br>Or: “ZIP Code: Peri-Orifices” → Zinc = Periorificial rash, Impaired taste, Patchy hair loss.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Copper<br /></br>   – Deficiency causes brittle/kinky hair, skin hypopigmentation, neurologic deficits (ataxia, peripheral neuropathy), sideroblastic or microcytic anemia, neutropenia. No periorificial rash or dysgeusia.<br /></br>b. Iron<br /></br>   – Presents with fatigue, pallor, pica, koilonychia; rash and taste loss are uncommon.<br /></br>c. Vitamin A<br /></br>   – Causes night blindness, xerosis, bitot spots on conjunctiva, follicular hyperkeratosis. Rash is usually generalized dry skin, not a painful periorificial dermatitis; alopecia less prominent.<br /></br>d. Vitamin B12 (cyanocobalamin)<br /></br>   – Would manifest months–years postop with megaloblastic anemia, glossitis, posterior column neuropathy. Does not cause the described dermatitis or alopecia.</p></br><p>Bottom Line<br /></br>After gastric bypass, the triad of periorificial dermatitis, alopecia, and dysgeusia points to zinc deficiency—prevented by routine zinc supplementation.</p>
78. A 32-year-old man comes to the office because of episodes of the sensation of a racing heart, sweating, difficulty breathing and swallowing, and the fear that he is dying. His symptoms began when he started a new job 4 weeks ago. He has had four episodes during the past 2 weeks. He says these episodes are similar to episodes he had 10 years ago. He has a history of alcohol use disorder but has been abstinent for 2 years. He has no other history of serious illness and takes no medications. His father and paternal grandfather had alcohol use disorder. The patient is calm and cooperative. Pulse is 74/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 116/72 mm Hg. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. On mental status examination, he is engaging and says he has not felt depressed. His affect is reactive, and his thought process is goal directed. Serum studies show a thyroid-stimulating hormone concentration of 3 μU/mL and thyroxine (T₄) concentration of 5 μg/dL. Which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Bupropion</br>b. Buspirone</br>c. Propylthiouracil</br>d. Sertraline</br>e. Triazolam</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Sertraline (selective serotonin re-uptake inhibitor, SSRI)</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Classic “panic-attack” descriptors: sudden episodes of 1) palpitations (“racing heart”), 2) diaphoresis, 3) dyspnea (“difficulty breathing”), 4) choking sensation (“difficulty swallowing”), 5) intense fear of dying.<br /></br>• Recurrent events (≥2 unexpected attacks in &lt;1 mo) plus onset of anticipatory anxiety (he volunteered the episodes to his doctor, a clue that he’s concerned) satisfy criteria for Panic Disorder once ≥1 month has elapsed. At 4 weeks he is essentially there for test purposes.<br /></br>• No medical mimic: vitals normal, TSH normal, T₄ low (hypothyroid → depression/fatigue, not adrenergic surges). Alcohol withdrawal would present with tremor, ↑BP, autonomic instability—none present.<br /></br>• Past identical episodes 10 years ago → chronic, relapsing course typical of panic disorder.</p></br><p>Key pattern = recurrent unexpected panic attacks in an otherwise healthy adult.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify panic symptoms.<br /></br>Step 2: Exclude organic causes (thyroid, substance, cardiopulmonary).<br /></br>Step 3: Choose first-line chronic therapy: SSRIs (sertraline, fluoxetine, paroxetine) or SNRIs; plus CBT.<br /></br>Take-home: SSRIs are first-line long-term pharmacologic treatment for panic disorder.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Etiology: Dysregulation of locus coeruleus (noradrenergic) and limbic serotonin pathways → episodic sympathetic surges.<br /></br>• Genetics: Strong familial aggregation (up to 40% heritability).<br /></br>• Course: Onset 20-40 yrs, episodic spikes with anticipatory anxiety between attacks.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>1. Pharmacologic<br /></br>   • First-line long-term: SSRIs (sertraline, fluoxetine, paroxetine) or SNRI (venlafaxine).<br /></br>   • Acute abortive or bridge: short-acting benzodiazepine (alprazolam, lorazepam) in limited supply.<br /></br>2. Psychotherapy: Cognitive-behavioral therapy (exposure + cognitive restructuring) is as effective as medication and ideally combined.<br /></br>3. Labs/Imaging: Rule out hyperthyroidism, pheochromocytoma, arrhythmia; already done here.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“PANIC”:<br /></br>P – Palpitations/Parasthesias<br /></br>A – Abdominal distress<br /></br>N – Nausea/Numbness<br /></br>I – Intense fear of dying/losing control<br /></br>C – Chest pain/Chills/Choking<br /></br>First-line med: “S-SSRI for Sudden Surges.”</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. Bupropion<br /></br>• Norepinephrine/dopamine re-uptake inhibitor. Activating; can precipitate anxiety or panic, especially early. Contra-indicated in panic disorder.</p></br><p>b. Buspirone<br /></br>• 5-HT₁A partial agonist used for Generalized Anxiety Disorder (excessive worry ≥6 mo). Ineffective for panic attacks; takes weeks to work and does not treat acute episodes.</p></br><p>c. Propylthiouracil<br /></br>• Thionamide for hyperthyroidism. Patient has low T₄, not high; symptoms are not thyrotoxic.</p></br><p>e. Triazolam<br /></br>• Ultra-short-acting benzodiazepine used for insomnia. Could abort an individual attack but inappropriate as primary, long-term therapy; high abuse potential, tolerance, rebound.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Core Takeaway<br /></br>Recurrent, unexpected panic attacks → Panic Disorder → first-line long-term treatment is an SSRI such as sertraline, often combined with CBT.</p>
79. A 42-year-old woman is brought to the emergency department 30 minutes after the sudden onset of left-sided weakness and a moderate headache while exercising at the gym. She reports that the weakness occurred almost immediately after the sudden onset of pain in her right jaw that radiated to the right temple. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. She is awake and alert. Her temperature is 37 °C (98.6 °F), pulse is 90/min, and blood pressure is 150/90 mm Hg. Examination shows left homonymous hemianopia. The right pupil is 2 mm, and the left is 4 mm. Both pupils are reactive to light. There is partial ptosis of the right eye and drooping of the left lower aspect of the face. Muscle strength is 5/5 in the right extremities and 4/5 in the left extremities. Deep tendon reflexes are 2+ in the right extremities and 3+ in the left extremities. Babinski sign is absent on the right and present on the left. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Cardioembolic stroke</br>b. Carotid artery dissection</br>c. Hypertensive hemorrhage</br>d. Subarachnoid hemorrhage</br>e. Vertebrobasilar infarct</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Carotid artery dissection  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>1. Age/setting: 42-year-old, previously healthy → stroke in a young patient; think “unusual” causes.<br /></br>2. Preceding symptom: “Sudden pain in her right jaw radiating to the right temple” → classic unilateral, fronto-temporal/mandibular pain of carotid dissection.<br /></br>3. Ocular findings: Right partial ptosis + right miosis (anisocoria with smaller right pupil) → ipsilateral Horner syndrome (ptosis, miosis, ± anhidrosis) that localizes to the sympathetic fibers running with the internal carotid artery.<br /></br>4. Focal neurologic deficit: Left hemiparesis, left hyperreflexia, left Babinski, and left homonymous hemianopia → acute ischemic stroke in the right cerebral hemisphere (MCA/PCA territory).<br /></br>5. Timing: Pain first, then stroke within minutes to hours—typical sequence for an arterial dissection.  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives<br /></br>• No neck stiffness, photophobia, or “worst headache” → argues against subarachnoid hemorrhage.<br /></br>• No long-standing hypertension, no deep brain signs (basal ganglia bleed) → less likely hypertensive hemorrhage.<br /></br>• No atrial fibrillation, valvular disease, or known cardiac thrombus → weak support for cardio-embolic source.<br /></br>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Young patient + focal stroke → consider dissection, PFO paradoxical embolus, hypercoagulability, vasculitis.<br /></br>2. Unilateral head/jaw pain → narrows to carotid pathology.<br /></br>3. Ipsilateral Horner syndrome → sympathetic fiber injury = carotid dissection until proven otherwise.<br /></br>4. Contralateral cortical stroke signs → dissection has produced thrombo-embolic occlusion of distal carotid/MCA.  </p></br><p>Takeaway: “Pain + (partial) Horner + young stroke = carotid artery dissection.”  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Intimal tear of the internal carotid → intramural hematoma → luminal narrowing + thrombus formation → distal embolization or hemodynamic hypoperfusion.<br /></br>• Predisposing factors: Minor neck trauma (sports, chiropractic), connective-tissue disorders (Ehlers-Danlos, Marfan), migraines, hypertension.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment<br /></br>1. Imaging:<br /></br>   • First-line: CT angiography (head/neck) or MR angiography showing tapered (“string sign”) or flame-shaped occlusion, double lumen, or pseudoaneurysm.<br /></br>2. Acute therapy (≤4.5 h): IV alteplase if no contraindications—carotid dissection is still an ischemic stroke.<br /></br>3. Secondary prevention: Antithrombotic therapy for 3–6 months<br /></br>   • Either antiplatelet (e.g., aspirin 81–325 mg) or anticoagulation (e.g., heparin bridge → warfarin) are acceptable; trials show similar outcomes.<br /></br>4. Endovascular stenting reserved for persistent ischemia or expanding pseudoaneurysm.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Triad 3 P’s” for Carotid Dissection<br /></br>• Pain (unilateral head/face/neck)<br /></br>• Partial Horner (ptosis + miosis)<br /></br>• Paresis (contralateral stroke)  </p></br><p>Think: “CARrotid = CAR crash in the neck → pain first, stroke later.”  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. Cardioembolic stroke<br /></br>   • Typically older patients with atrial fibrillation, recent MI, prosthetic valves.<br /></br>   • No preceding unilateral head/neck pain or Horner syndrome.  </p></br><p>c. Hypertensive hemorrhage<br /></br>   • Presents with sudden focal deficit + severe headache/vomiting in longstanding hypertensives; CT shows deep intraparenchymal bleed (putamen, thalamus, pons, cerebellum).<br /></br>   • Would not give Horner syndrome or jaw pain; BP only mildly elevated here.  </p></br><p>d. Subarachnoid hemorrhage<br /></br>   • “Thunderclap” worst headache of life, photophobia, nuchal rigidity; focal deficits are uncommon initially.<br /></br>   • CT often shows blood in basal cisterns; vignette lacks meningeal signs and has clear focal cortical findings.  </p></br><p>e. Vertebrobasilar infarct<br /></br>   • Produces brain-stem signs: CN palsies, ataxia, dysphagia, crossed motor/sensory deficits, possible “locked-in” syndrome.<br /></br>   • Homonymous hemianopia with cortical (not brain-stem) signs and ipsilateral Horner is more typical of carotid pathology.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Core Message<br /></br>In a young patient with unilateral head/neck pain, ipsilateral partial Horner syndrome, and acute contralateral cortical stroke, internal carotid artery dissection is the classic—and most testable—diagnosis.</p>
80. A previously healthy 18-year-old primigravid woman at 22 weeks’ gestation comes to the physician because of a genital lesion for 2 days. She is sexually active with multiple partners, but has no history of sexually transmitted diseases. Examination shows a 1.5-cm, painless, superficial ulcer on the left labium majus. There is nontender, firm inguinal lymphadenopathy. Serologic testing 8 weeks ago was negative for syphilis, Neisseria gonorrhoeae, and Chlamydia trachomatis. Which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Acyclovir</br>b. Azithromycin</br>c. Benzathine penicillin G</br>d. Ceftriaxone</br>e. Tetracycline</br></br></br><p>I want to flag a potential problem before giving a full explanation:<br /></br>The clinical picture in this vignette (painless solitary ulcer with a clean base + firm, nontender inguinal nodes) is classic for a primary syphilitic chancre, and first-line therapy in a pregnant woman is a single IM dose of benzathine penicillin G. Acyclovir is the drug of choice for genital herpes, but primary herpes lesions are almost always painful and usually multiple, and the lymphadenopathy is typically tender.</p></br><p>Because accuracy is critical for NBME/Step 2 preparation, I believe the keyed answer should be “benzathine penicillin G,” not acyclovir.<br /></br>Please let me know if you would like the full high-yield explanation based on benzathine penicillin G (the evidence-based answer) or if you need an explanation built around acyclovir despite the clinical mismatch.</p>
81. A 9-month-old boy is brought to the office by his parents for a well-child examination in late autumn. He was born at 34 weeks’ gestation via spontaneous vaginal delivery to a 24-year-old woman, gravida 2, para 2, following an uncomplicated pregnancy. Medical history is noncontributory, and he receives no medications. Growth and development have been appropriate for age, and required vaccinations are up to date as of his last well-child visit. The patient is 72 cm (28.5 in; 50th percentile) long and weighs 10 kg, or 22 lb; 75th percentile). Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination discloses no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate condition to vaccinate against at this time?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Hepatitis A</br>b. Influenza</br>c. Meningococcus</br>d. Respiratory syncytial virus</br>e. Rotavirus</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Influenza  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age: 9 months (≥ 6 months).<br /></br>• Season: “late autumn” → the beginning of influenza season (Oct–Mar in the U.S.).<br /></br>• Vaccine status: “up-to-date as of his last visit,” meaning he has received everything scheduled through 6 months of age.<br /></br>• Prematurity: born at 34 weeks—important because RSV prophylaxis is sometimes considered, but guidelines restrict palivizumab to &lt; 29 weeks GA or other major risk factors (he has none).<br /></br>Key takeaway: At 9 months the only universally recommended immunization that comes due (outside of catch-up doses) is the annual inactivated influenza vaccine.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Check routine schedule at 9 months: no new primary series vaccines are scheduled except annual flu.<br /></br>2. Ask, “Is there any special-risk vaccine/prophylaxis needed?”<br /></br>   • Hep A begins at 12 months.<br /></br>   • Meningococcal (MenACWY) starts at 11 years.<br /></br>   • Rotavirus series must be completed by 8 months.<br /></br>   • Palivizumab (RSV) is not a routine vaccine and this child does not meet criteria (&lt; 29 wk GA, BPD, congenital heart disease).<br /></br>3. Conclusion → give inactivated influenza vaccine now.  </p></br><p>Core Concept: All children ≥ 6 months old should receive the inactivated influenza vaccine every fall/winter, regardless of prematurity, unless a true contraindication exists.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology &amp; Epidemiology (High-Yield)<br /></br>Influenza<br /></br>• RNA orthomyxovirus, transmitted via respiratory droplets.<br /></br>• Highest morbidity in children &lt; 5 yrs, adults ≥ 65 yrs, pregnant women, and those with chronic diseases.<br /></br>• Vaccination reduces hospitalization, otitis media, and secondary bacterial pneumonia.<br /></br>• Two pediatric formulations:<br /></br>  – Inactivated intramuscular (IIV) → ≥ 6 months (standard).<br /></br>  – Live-attenuated intranasal (LAIV) → ≥ 2 years and healthy; not for asthmatics or immunocompromised.<br /></br>• Age 6 months–8 years receiving flu vaccine for the first time need 2 doses ≥ 4 weeks apart; thereafter annual single dose.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Prevention Summary<br /></br>1. Administer 0.25 mL (6–35 mo) or 0.5 mL (≥ 36 mo) IIV today.<br /></br>2. Schedule second dose 4 weeks later because this is his first flu season.<br /></br>3. Standard anticipatory guidance: local soreness, low-grade fever.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Fall for Flu at Six Months”<br /></br>• As soon as a baby “falls” past 6 months of age and it’s fall (autumn), think influenza shot.  </p></br><p>Age Anchors<br /></br>• 2–6 mo: primary series (DTaP, Hib, IPV, PCV, rotavirus).<br /></br>• 6 mo: first influenza.<br /></br>• 9 mo: catch-up &amp; influenza only.<br /></br>• 12 mo: MMR, Varicella, Hep A.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Hepatitis A<br /></br>• Routine two-dose series starts at 12 months; too early for this patient.  </p></br><p>c. Meningococcus (MenACWY)<br /></br>• First routine dose at 11 years (with potential early dosing only for specific high-risk groups such as asplenia, complement deficiency, or travel to endemic areas).  </p></br><p>d. Respiratory Syncytial Virus (RSV)<br /></br>• No vaccine exists. Palivizumab monoclonal antibody is monthly prophylaxis for high-risk infants: GA &lt; 29 wk, hemodynamically significant heart disease, chronic lung disease, or severe immunodeficiency. This child was 34 wk GA with no comorbidities—does not qualify.  </p></br><p>e. Rotavirus<br /></br>• Oral vaccine series must be STARTED by 15 weeks and COMPLETED by 8 months. At 9 months it is contraindicated to initiate or continue the series due to risk of intussusception.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Confusing RSV prophylaxis with routine vaccination—remember palivizumab is not a standard “vaccine” and is reserved for a narrow group of infants.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>High-Yield Take-Home Message<br /></br>For any child ≥ 6 months seen in the fall/winter, always ask, “Have they had this season’s flu shot?” If not, the inactivated influenza vaccine is due regardless of gestational age or previous immunization history.</p>
82. A 17-year-old girl comes to the office because of a 2-month history of increasingly severe pain below her left knee at rest and with exertion. She now has moderate difficulty walking because of the pain. She has no history of trauma or serious illness. She takes no medications.
